What Is Kalam?
intermediate
5 min
The Reality of Nazar and Its Two Movements
Author defines nazar and distinguishes two movements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of nazar as the combination of two distinct mental movements: one to gather premises and another to arrange them into a conclusion.

Core Idea
Nazar is a two-step process: collect matter, then compose form.

This lesson unpacks the foundational definition of nazar (speculative reasoning) in Islamic kalam. The author explains that seeking a conception or assent requires the soul to move from the desired object to stored forms, eventually arriving at principles and then arranging them. This process is broken into two essential movements that together constitute the reality of nazar.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of nazar
Start by reading the first part of the passage (from 'مطلوب تصوري أو تصديقي' to 'مادة الثانية'). Focus on the description of how the soul moves to obtain premises.
The passage is dense; read slowly and note the key terms.
2
Identify the two movements
Look for where the author explicitly states 'فههنا حركتان'. The first movement obtains the matter (premises), the second obtains the form and principles through arrangement.
The first movement ends when principles are reached; the second begins from those principles.
3
Understand the reality of nazar
The author concludes that the reality (حقيقة) of nazar is the sum of both movements. No single movement alone constitutes nazar.
This definition distinguishes nazar from mere thinking or random mental activity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلوب تصوري أو تصديقي ولا محالة يكون مشعوراً به من وجه تحرکت النفس منه في الصور المخزونة عندها، منتقلاً من صورة إلى صورة إلى أن يظفر بمبادئه من الذاتيات والعرضيات والحدود الوسطى، فيستحضرها متعينة متميزة، ثم يتحرك فيها لترتيبها ترتيباً خاصاً يؤدي إلى تصور المطلوب بحقيقته، أو بوجه يمتاز به عما عداه، أو إلى التصديق به يقيناً أو غير يقين، فههنا حركتان يحصل بأولاهما المادة، وبالثانية: الصورة والمبادىء من حيث الوصول إليها منتهى الحركة الأولى، ومن حيث الرجوع عنها مبدأ الحركة الثانية، ومن حيث التصرف فيها لترتب الترتيب المخصوص مادة الثانية، وحقيقة النظر مجموع الحركتين وهما من جنس الحركة في الكيف بتوارد الصور والكيفيات على النفس، ولا محالة يكون هناك توجه نحو المطلوب، وإزالة لما والصور المضادة والمنافية وملاحظة للمعقولات ليؤخذ البعض ويحذف البعض، وترتيب للمأخوذ وغاية يقصد حصولها، وكثيراً ما يقتصر في تفسير النظر على بعض أجزائه، أو لوازمه اكتفاء بما يفيد امتيازه أو اصطلاحاً على ذلك، فيقال: هو حركة الذهن إلى مبادىء المطلوب، أو حركته عن المباديء إلى المطالب، أو ترتيب المعلومات للتأدي إلى المجهول، ويراد بالعلم الحضور عند العقل ليعم الظن والجهل المركب أيضاً، ويدخل التعريف بالفصل وحده، أو بالخاصة وحدها، بناء على أنه يكون المشتق كالناطق، والضاحك، وفيه شائبة التركيب والترتيب بين الموصوف والصفة، أو يخص التفسير بالنظر المشتمل على التأليف والترتيب لندرة التعريف بالمفرد فلا يضر خروجه وهذا ما قال ابن سينا: إن التعريف بالمفرد نزر خداع والإمام ذكر مكان المعلومات التصديقات، بناء على ما ذهب إليه من امتناع اكتساب التصورات وكثيراً ما يجعله عبارة عن نفس المعلوم المرتبة ومن قال ترتيب أمور معلومة أو مظنونة للتأدي إلى مجهول، أراد بالعلم التصور والتصديق الجازم المطابق الثابت على ما هو معنى اليقين، وبالظن ما يقابل اليقين فيتناول الظن الصرف، والجهل المركب والاعتقاد على ما صرح به في شرح الإشارات، وحينئذ لا يرد ما ذكر في المواقف من أن هذا ليس تفسيراً للنظر الصحيح، والإلزام أن يقيد الظن بالمطابقة ليخرج الفاسد من جهة المادة المظنونة الكاذبة، وأن يقال بدل للتأدي بحيث يؤدي ليخرج الفاسد من جهة الصورة بل لمطلق النظر، ومقدماته قد لا تكون معلومة ولا مظنونة بل مجهولة
A sought [object] is either a conception or an assent, and inevitably it is perceived in some respect, [as] the soul moves from it in the forms stored with it, passing from one form to another until it hits upon its principles from the essential [accidents], accidental [accidents], and middle terms. Then it brings them to mind as determinate and distinct, then it moves in them to arrange them in a specific arrangement that leads to a conception of the sought in its reality, or in a respect by which it is distinguished from what is other than it, or to an assent to it with certainty or without certainty. So here [there are] two movements: by the first is obtained the matter, and by the second: the form and the principles – in respect of reaching them being the end of the first movement, and in respect of turning back from them being the starting point of the second movement, and in respect of handling them to arrange the specific arrangement being the matter of the second. And the reality of nazar is the sum of the two movements, and they are of the genus of movement in quality by the succession of forms and qualities upon the soul. And inevitably there is an orientation toward the sought, and an elimination of what and the opposing and contrary forms, and an observation of the intelligibles so that some are taken and some omitted, and an arrangement of what is taken, and a goal intended to be obtained. And often in the interpretation of nazar one confines [oneself] to some of its parts or its concomitants, relying on what suffices to indicate its distinction or as a convention for that, so it is said: it is the movement of the mind to the principles of the sought, or its movement from the principles to the sought [objects], or the arrangement of information to lead to the unknown. And by 'ilm [knowledge] is meant [mental] presence with the intellect, so as to include opinion and compound ignorance as well. And the definition by differentia alone, or by property alone, enters [into this], based on the fact that the derivative [like 'rational' and 'laughing'] is [composite] and has a tinge of composition and arrangement between the described and the attribute. Or the interpretation is restricted to nazar that includes composition and arrangement, because definition by a single term is rare, so its exclusion does no harm. And this is what Ibn Sina said: 'Definition by a single term is rare and defective.' And the Imam [al-Razi] put 'assents' in place of 'information', based on his view that the acquisition of conceptions is impossible, and he often makes it an expression of the very arranged information. And whoever said 'arrangement of known or conjectured matters to lead to an unknown', he meant by 'knowledge' conception and the decisive, conformable, fixed assent – which is the meaning of certainty – and by 'conjecture' what is opposite to certainty, so it includes pure conjecture, compound ignorance, and belief, as stated in Sharh al-Isharat. And then the objection mentioned in al-Mawaqif – that this is not an interpretation of correct nazar – does not apply. And [the objection] that [one] must restrict conjecture to conformity to exclude the corrupt from the aspect of matter – the false conjectured – and that one should say 'so that it leads' instead of 'to lead' to exclude the corrupt from the aspect of form – rather [this definition is] for absolute nazar, and its premises may be not known nor conjectured but unknown.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of nazar (speculative reasoning). The author begins by describing the process of seeking either a conception or an assent. The mind moves from the sought object to stored images, then to essential and accidental attributes and middle terms, to obtain premises. Two movements are identified: the first obtains the matter (the premises), the second composes them into a specific arrangement leading to the desired knowledge. The true nature of nazar is the combination of both movements, which are a type of qualitative change as forms and qualities succeed each other in the soul. Additionally, there is orientation toward the goal, elimination of contrary forms, selection of relevant intelligibles, and ordering. Definitions of nazar often focus on parts: movement from the mind to premises, or from premises to conclusions, or arrangement of information. The author notes that 'ilm here is broad, including knowledge, opinion, and compound ignorance. Definitions by differentia or property alone are included because they contain composition. Ibn Sina is cited that definition by a single term is rare and flawed. Imam al-Razi replaced 'information' with 'assents' due to his denial of acquired conceptions. Finally, a definition using 'known or conjectured matters' is defended: 'knowledge' means certainty, 'conjecture' includes pure conjecture, compound ignorance, and belief. This avoids objections from al-Mawaqif about requiring correctness of matter or form, because the definition covers absolute nazar, whose premises may be unknown.

Key Terms
النظر
الحركة
المادة
الصورة
المبادىء
التصور
التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think nazar is just any type of thinking or mental activity without a structured process.
Correction: Nazar is specifically a two-step movement: first collecting premises (matter) and then arranging them (form). It is a disciplined reasoning process, not random thought.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what constitutes the reality (حقيقة) of nazar?
A) Only the first movement that obtains the matter.
B) Only the second movement that arranges the form.
C) The sum of the two movements.
D) The arrangement of information.

Key Takeaway
Nazar is the combined process of gathering and arranging premises to reach a sought conception or assent; each movement alone is insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to understand a matter of your faith, do you rush to a conclusion without first gathering the essential premises? How does the two-movement model of nazar expose your impatience or reliance on shortcuts?
?
Think of a belief you hold with certainty. Can you trace the two movements of nazar in how you arrived at it? If not, what does that say about the strength of your conviction?
?
The author says nazar is the combination of both movements. Which movement do you most often neglect in your own reasoning—gathering evidence or properly arranging it? What will you do this week to practice both?

Extensions and Refinements of Nazar
Author discusses partial definitions and refines the scope of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars defined nazar in various partial ways and how they refined the definition to include unknown premises and different types of knowledge.

Core Idea
The definition of nazar can focus on part of the process, but must be broad enough to cover all reasoning.

After establishing the core two-movement definition, the author discusses numerous alternative definitions that focus on single aspects, such as movement to principles or arrangement of information. He also clarifies that terms like 'ilm (knowledge) and zann (conjecture) are used broadly to include compound ignorance. Finally, he addresses objections from critics like al-Razi and explains how the definition can stand despite requiring correct premises.

Guided Path
1
Examine the partial definitions
Read the section from 'وكثيراً ما يقتصر' to 'نزر خداع'. Note the three common definitions: movement to principles, movement from principles, and arrangement of information.
Each definition captures only part of the full process.
2
Understand the broad use of 'ilm and zann
The author clarifies that here 'ilm means 'mental presence' to include opinion and compound ignorance, and zann covers opposite of certainty, including belief.
This prevents the definition from being too narrow.
3
See the refinements by Ibn Sina and al-Razi
Ibn Sina criticizes definition by a single term. Al-Razi substitutes 'assents' for 'information' due to his denial of acquired conceptions. The passage defends the broad definition against objections from al-Mawaqif.
These refinements show the living tradition of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلوب تصوري أو تصديقي ولا محالة يكون مشعوراً به من وجه تحرکت النفس منه في الصور المخزونة عندها، منتقلاً من صورة إلى صورة إلى أن يظفر بمبادئه من الذاتيات والعرضيات والحدود الوسطى، فيستحضرها متعينة متميزة، ثم يتحرك فيها لترتيبها ترتيباً خاصاً يؤدي إلى تصور المطلوب بحقيقته، أو بوجه يمتاز به عما عداه، أو إلى التصديق به يقيناً أو غير يقين، فههنا حركتان يحصل بأولاهما المادة، وبالثانية: الصورة والمبادىء من حيث الوصول إليها منتهى الحركة الأولى، ومن حيث الرجوع عنها مبدأ الحركة الثانية، ومن حيث التصرف فيها لترتب الترتيب المخصوص مادة الثانية، وحقيقة النظر مجموع الحركتين وهما من جنس الحركة في الكيف بتوارد الصور والكيفيات على النفس، ولا محالة يكون هناك توجه نحو المطلوب، وإزالة لما والصور المضادة والمنافية وملاحظة للمعقولات ليؤخذ البعض ويحذف البعض، وترتيب للمأخوذ وغاية يقصد حصولها، وكثيراً ما يقتصر في تفسير النظر على بعض أجزائه، أو لوازمه اكتفاء بما يفيد امتيازه أو اصطلاحاً على ذلك، فيقال: هو حركة الذهن إلى مبادىء المطلوب، أو حركته عن المباديء إلى المطالب، أو ترتيب المعلومات للتأدي إلى المجهول، ويراد بالعلم الحضور عند العقل ليعم الظن والجهل المركب أيضاً، ويدخل التعريف بالفصل وحده، أو بالخاصة وحدها، بناء على أنه يكون المشتق كالناطق، والضاحك، وفيه شائبة التركيب والترتيب بين الموصوف والصفة، أو يخص التفسير بالنظر المشتمل على التأليف والترتيب لندرة التعريف بالمفرد فلا يضر خروجه وهذا ما قال ابن سينا: إن التعريف بالمفرد نزر خداع والإمام ذكر مكان المعلومات التصديقات، بناء على ما ذهب إليه من امتناع اكتساب التصورات وكثيراً ما يجعله عبارة عن نفس المعلوم المرتبة ومن قال ترتيب أمور معلومة أو مظنونة للتأدي إلى مجهول، أراد بالعلم التصور والتصديق الجازم المطابق الثابت على ما هو معنى اليقين، وبالظن ما يقابل اليقين فيتناول الظن الصرف، والجهل المركب والاعتقاد على ما صرح به في شرح الإشارات، وحينئذ لا يرد ما ذكر في المواقف من أن هذا ليس تفسيراً للنظر الصحيح، والإلزام أن يقيد الظن بالمطابقة ليخرج الفاسد من جهة المادة المظنونة الكاذبة، وأن يقال بدل للتأدي بحيث يؤدي ليخرج الفاسد من جهة الصورة بل لمطلق النظر، ومقدماته قد لا تكون معلومة ولا مظنونة بل مجهولة
A sought [object] is either a conception or an assent, and inevitably it is perceived in some respect, [as] the soul moves from it in the forms stored with it, passing from one form to another until it hits upon its principles from the essential [accidents], accidental [accidents], and middle terms. Then it brings them to mind as determinate and distinct, then it moves in them to arrange them in a specific arrangement that leads to a conception of the sought in its reality, or in a respect by which it is distinguished from what is other than it, or to an assent to it with certainty or without certainty. So here [there are] two movements: by the first is obtained the matter, and by the second: the form and the principles – in respect of reaching them being the end of the first movement, and in respect of turning back from them being the starting point of the second movement, and in respect of handling them to arrange the specific arrangement being the matter of the second. And the reality of nazar is the sum of the two movements, and they are of the genus of movement in quality by the succession of forms and qualities upon the soul. And inevitably there is an orientation toward the sought, and an elimination of what and the opposing and contrary forms, and an observation of the intelligibles so that some are taken and some omitted, and an arrangement of what is taken, and a goal intended to be obtained. And often in the interpretation of nazar one confines [oneself] to some of its parts or its concomitants, relying on what suffices to indicate its distinction or as a convention for that, so it is said: it is the movement of the mind to the principles of the sought, or its movement from the principles to the sought [objects], or the arrangement of information to lead to the unknown. And by 'ilm [knowledge] is meant [mental] presence with the intellect, so as to include opinion and compound ignorance as well. And the definition by differentia alone, or by property alone, enters [into this], based on the fact that the derivative [like 'rational' and 'laughing'] is [composite] and has a tinge of composition and arrangement between the described and the attribute. Or the interpretation is restricted to nazar that includes composition and arrangement, because definition by a single term is rare, so its exclusion does no harm. And this is what Ibn Sina said: 'Definition by a single term is rare and defective.' And the Imam [al-Razi] put 'assents' in place of 'information', based on his view that the acquisition of conceptions is impossible, and he often makes it an expression of the very arranged information. And whoever said 'arrangement of known or conjectured matters to lead to an unknown', he meant by 'knowledge' conception and the decisive, conformable, fixed assent – which is the meaning of certainty – and by 'conjecture' what is opposite to certainty, so it includes pure conjecture, compound ignorance, and belief, as stated in Sharh al-Isharat. And then the objection mentioned in al-Mawaqif – that this is not an interpretation of correct nazar – does not apply. And [the objection] that [one] must restrict conjecture to conformity to exclude the corrupt from the aspect of matter – the false conjectured – and that one should say 'so that it leads' instead of 'to lead' to exclude the corrupt from the aspect of form – rather [this definition is] for absolute nazar, and its premises may be not known nor conjectured but unknown.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of nazar (speculative reasoning). The author begins by describing the process of seeking either a conception or an assent. The mind moves from the sought object to stored images, then to essential and accidental attributes and middle terms, to obtain premises. Two movements are identified: the first obtains the matter (the premises), the second composes them into a specific arrangement leading to the desired knowledge. The true nature of nazar is the combination of both movements, which are a type of qualitative change as forms and qualities succeed each other in the soul. Additionally, there is orientation toward the goal, elimination of contrary forms, selection of relevant intelligibles, and ordering. Definitions of nazar often focus on parts: movement from the mind to premises, or from premises to conclusions, or arrangement of information. The author notes that 'ilm here is broad, including knowledge, opinion, and compound ignorance. Definitions by differentia or property alone are included because they contain composition. Ibn Sina is cited that definition by a single term is rare and flawed. Imam al-Razi replaced 'information' with 'assents' due to his denial of acquired conceptions. Finally, a definition using 'known or conjectured matters' is defended: 'knowledge' means certainty, 'conjecture' includes pure conjecture, compound ignorance, and belief. This avoids objections from al-Mawaqif about requiring correctness of matter or form, because the definition covers absolute nazar, whose premises may be unknown.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that nazar must always involve certain knowledge and correct premises.
Correction: Nazar can also proceed from conjectured or even unknown premises; it is the process of arrangement, not the quality of premises.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did Imam al-Razi replace 'information' with 'assents' in the definition of nazar?
A) Because he thought assents are more important than information.
B) Because he believed that conceptions cannot be acquired through reasoning.
C) Because he wanted to emphasize certainty.
D) Because he followed Ibn Sina's view.

Key Takeaway
The comprehensive definition of nazar must be broad enough to include both certain and uncertain premises, and it encompasses various partial definitions that each highlight one aspect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own search for truth, do you sometimes dismiss a line of reasoning because its premises are not certain? How does this lesson's recognition that nazar can operate on unknown premises challenge your habit? Will you now give more room to uncertain reasoning in your reflections?
?
The lesson shows that even definitions of a just process — nazar — can be varied and incomplete. How comfortable are you when your understanding of truth is not captured by a single neat formula? When have you insisted on a narrow definition of what constitutes valid 'proof' in your own life, and what did you miss?
?
You rely on 'nazar' every day without labeling it. When was the last time you honestly examined the premises behind a deeply held belief? Could some of your 'certainties' actually fall under the category of 'conjecture' or 'compound ignorance'? What would it cost you to admit that?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
6 min
The Three Definitions of Nazar and the Scope of Knowledge
Al-Taftazani lists three definitions of nazar and expands the meaning of 'ilm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three formulations of nazar as movement of the mind and the broad meaning of 'ilm that includes opinion and compound ignorance.

Core Idea
Nazar is mental movement toward knowledge; 'ilm here means any mental presence, even false belief.

Al-Taftazani begins by defining nazar (speculative reasoning) in three interrelated ways, all describing the mind's movement toward or arrangement of information. He then clarifies that the term 'ilm (knowledge) in these definitions is used broadly to include opinion (zann) and compound ignorance (jahl murakkab). This sets the stage for discussing what counts as proper reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the three definitions of nazar
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where al-Taftazani presents three formulations of nazar: movement to principles, movement from principles, and arranging information.
Notice that these definitions are variations on a theme.
2
Understand the broad meaning of 'ilm
The author states that 'ilm here means presence with the intellect, so it includes opinion and compound ignorance. Why do you think he broadens the term?
This is to make the definition cover all types of reasoning.
3
Consider definition by a single term
Read the discussion about defining nazar by a single term (differentia or property) and Ibn Sina's objection that such definitions are 'a meager deception.'
Ibn Sina's quote highlights the rarity and inadequacy of simple definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حركة الذهن إلى مبادىء المطلوب، أو حركته عن المباديء إلى المطالب، أو ترتيب المعلومات للتأدي إلى المجهول، ويراد بالعلم الحضور عند العقل ليعم الظن والجهل المركب أيضاً، ويدخل التعريف بالفصل(۱) وحده، أو بالخاصة وحدها، بناء على أنه يكون المشتق كالناطق والضاحك، وفيه شائبة التركيب والترتيب بين الموصوف والصفة، أو يخص التفسير بالنظر المشتمل على التأليف والترتيب لندرة التعريف بالمفرد فلا يضر خروجه وهذا ما قال ابن سينا : إن التعريف بالمفرد، نزر خداع(۲) والإمام ذكر مكان المعلومات التصديقات، بناء على ما ذهب إليه من امتناع اكتساب التصورات وكثيراً ما يجعله عبارة عن نفس المعلوم المرتبة ومن قال ترتيب أمور معلومة أو مظنونة للتأدي إلى مجهول، أراد بالعلم التصور والتصديق الجازم المطابق الثابت على ما هو معنى اليقين، وبالظن ما يقابل اليقين فيتناول الظن الصرف، والجهل المركب والاعتقاد على ما صرح به في شرح الإشارات، وحينئذ لا يرد ما ذكر في المواقف من أن هذا ليس تفسيراً للنظر(۳) الصحيح، والإلزام أن يقيد الظن بالمطابقة ليخرج الفاسد من جهة المادة المظنونة الكاذبة، وأن يقال بدل للتأدي بحيث يؤدي ليخرج الفاسد من جهة الصورة بل لمطلق النظر، ومقدماته قد لا تكون معلومة ولا مظنونة بل مجهولة جهلاً مركباً، ولا يتناوله التفسير فلا يكون جامعاً
Movement of the mind to the principles of the desired [conclusion], or its movement from the principles to the desired [conclusions], or arranging known things to lead to the unknown. By 'knowledge' is meant presence with the intellect so that it includes opinion and compound ignorance as well. The definition enters via the differentia alone, or via the property alone, based on the fact that a derivative term like 'rational' and 'laughing' [may be used], and in it there is a tinge of composition and arrangement between the described and the attribute. Or the interpretation is restricted to consideration that includes composition and arrangement, because definition by a simple term is rare, so its exclusion does no harm. And this is what Ibn Sina said: 'Definition by a simple term is a meager deception.' The Imam mentioned instead of 'informations' the 'assents', based on his view of the impossibility of acquiring concepts, and often he makes it an expression of the arranged known [things] itself. And if someone said: 'Arranging known or opined things to lead to an unknown,' he meant by 'knowledge' concept and definitive, correspondence-based, stable assent according to the meaning of certainty, and by 'opinion' what is opposite of certainty, so it includes pure opinion, compound ignorance, and belief, as he explained in Sharh al-Isharat. Then the objection mentioned in al-Mawaqif that this is not an interpretation of valid consideration does not apply. The necessity is that one should restrict opinion to correspondence in order to exclude the corrupt from the material aspect of false opined [propositions], and one should say 'such that it leads' instead of 'to lead' in order to exclude the corrupt from the formal aspect. Rather [the definition applies] to absolute consideration, and its premises may not be known nor opined but unknown with compound ignorance, and the interpretation does not include it, so it is not comprehensive.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents several definitions of 'nazar' (speculative reasoning) from different scholars and critiques their comprehensiveness. Al-Taftazani begins by listing three formulations: movement of the mind from to the principles of the desired conclusion, movement from the principles to the conclusions, and arranging known information to yield the unknown. He then explains that the term 'ilm (knowledge) in these definitions is intended broadly as presence in the mind, so as to include opinion (zann) and compound ignorance (jahl murakkab). He discusses the possibility of defining nazar by a single term (fasl or khassa) but notes that such definitions imply composition and are rare. Ibn Sina is quoted as saying that definition by a simple term is 'a meager deception'. The Imam (al-Razi) replaced 'informations' with 'assents' based on his theory that concepts cannot be acquired. Then al-Taftazani considers a definition that includes both known and opined premises. The defender of this definition intends by 'ilm' only certainty (yaqin) and by 'zann' everything else including compound ignorance, thereby avoiding the objection from al-Mawaqif. However, al-Taftazani points out that even this definition fails to cover cases where the premises are not known or opined but are actually compoundly unknown, thus making the definition non-comprehensive. The central issue is that a proper definition of nazar must cover all cases of reasoning, including defective ones, which these definitions do not.

Key Terms
نظر
علم
ظن
جهل مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'ilm and zann are mutually exclusive in these definitions.
Correction: Al-Taftazani explicitly broadens 'ilm to include both, so that the definition covers all reasoning.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Taftazani broaden the meaning of 'ilm in the definitions of nazar?
A) To include only certain knowledge
B) To include opinion and compound ignorance as well
C) To exclude philosophical reasoning
D) To make the definition match Aristotle's logic

Key Takeaway
Nazar is essentially mental arrangement; definitions vary but must be interpreted with a wide sense of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
In your pursuit of understanding Islam, do you settle for simple definitions and quick answers, or do you engage in the deeper arrangement of evidence? Where might a 'meager deception' be hiding in your current understanding?
?
When you encounter a belief that feels certain but is actually based on weak reasoning, are you willing to uproot it? What will you do today to test the foundations of your most cherished convictions?

Al-Razi's Modification and the Problem of Comprehensiveness
Al-Taftazani critiques the comprehensiveness of a proposed definition of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani argues that the definition 'arranging known or opined things to lead to an unknown' fails to cover all cases of reasoning.

Core Idea
A definition of nazar must include invalid reasoning, not just valid.

After presenting the basic definitions, al-Taftazani considers a refined definition that includes known and opined premises. He examines al-Razi's replacement of 'informations' with 'assents' and then critiques the definition for failing to cover cases where premises are themselves compoundly unknown. This reveals the challenge of crafting a comprehensive definition of speculative reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recognize al-Razi's modification
Read where al-Taftazani discusses that al-Razi replaced 'informations' with 'assents' in the definition, based on his theory that concepts cannot be acquired.
Al-Razi's change reflects his epistemological views.
2
Understand the defense of the broader definition
The defender of the definition 'arranging known or opined things' interprets 'ilm as certainty and 'zann' as encompassing everything else, including compound ignorance.
This defense attempts to avoid earlier objections.
3
See al-Taftazani's critique
Al-Taftazani points out that premises might be neither known nor opined but compoundly unknown, so the definition does not cover such cases, making it non-comprehensive.
This critique shows the difficulty of defining nazar universally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حركة الذهن إلى مبادىء المطلوب، أو حركته عن المباديء إلى المطالب، أو ترتيب المعلومات للتأدي إلى المجهول، ويراد بالعلم الحضور عند العقل ليعم الظن والجهل المركب أيضاً، ويدخل التعريف بالفصل(۱) وحده، أو بالخاصة وحدها، بناء على أنه يكون المشتق كالناطق والضاحك، وفيه شائبة التركيب والترتيب بين الموصوف والصفة، أو يخص التفسير بالنظر المشتمل على التأليف والترتيب لندرة التعريف بالمفرد فلا يضر خروجه وهذا ما قال ابن سينا : إن التعريف بالمفرد، نزر خداع(۲) والإمام ذكر مكان المعلومات التصديقات، بناء على ما ذهب إليه من امتناع اكتساب التصورات وكثيراً ما يجعله عبارة عن نفس المعلوم المرتبة ومن قال ترتيب أمور معلومة أو مظنونة للتأدي إلى مجهول، أراد بالعلم التصور والتصديق الجازم المطابق الثابت على ما هو معنى اليقين، وبالظن ما يقابل اليقين فيتناول الظن الصرف، والجهل المركب والاعتقاد على ما صرح به في شرح الإشارات، وحينئذ لا يرد ما ذكر في المواقف من أن هذا ليس تفسيراً للنظر(۳) الصحيح، والإلزام أن يقيد الظن بالمطابقة ليخرج الفاسد من جهة المادة المظنونة الكاذبة، وأن يقال بدل للتأدي بحيث يؤدي ليخرج الفاسد من جهة الصورة بل لمطلق النظر، ومقدماته قد لا تكون معلومة ولا مظنونة بل مجهولة جهلاً مركباً، ولا يتناوله التفسير فلا يكون جامعاً
Movement of the mind to the principles of the desired [conclusion], or its movement from the principles to the desired [conclusions], or arranging known things to lead to the unknown. By 'knowledge' is meant presence with the intellect so that it includes opinion and compound ignorance as well. The definition enters via the differentia alone, or via the property alone, based on the fact that a derivative term like 'rational' and 'laughing' [may be used], and in it there is a tinge of composition and arrangement between the described and the attribute. Or the interpretation is restricted to consideration that includes composition and arrangement, because definition by a simple term is rare, so its exclusion does no harm. And this is what Ibn Sina said: 'Definition by a simple term is a meager deception.' The Imam mentioned instead of 'informations' the 'assents', based on his view of the impossibility of acquiring concepts, and often he makes it an expression of the arranged known [things] itself. The necessity is that one should restrict opinion to correspondence in order to exclude the corrupt from the material aspect of false opined [propositions], and one should say 'such that it leads' instead of 'to lead' in order to exclude the corrupt from the formal aspect. Alternative Definition of Nazar and the Analogy of Sight
Al-Taftazani gives an alternative definition of nazar via ملاحظة المعقول and illustrates with an analogy of sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an alternative definition of nazar as 'observing the intelligible' and how it is analogous to physical sight.

Core Idea
Nazar is like focusing the mind's eye to see the truth.

Al-Taftazani offers a simpler definition of nazar that avoids earlier complications. He defines it as 'observing the intelligible' and then deepens the understanding through an analogy with physical sight. This passage reveals the intellectual movement involved in nazar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Taftazani uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the alternative definition
Find the phrase that defines nazar as 'observing the intelligible' and note how 'intelligible' is broadly defined to include even compound ignorance.
This definition avoids the need for prior specification of premises.
3
Follow the analogy with sight
Al-Taftazani compares intellectual sight to physical sight. Notice the steps: averting gaze, moving the pupil, until the object is perceived. Apply this to the mind's operation.
The analogy shows nazar as an active, directed process.
4
Examine the further definition
From the analogy comes a further definition: nazar as stripping the mind of heedlessness or directing the intellect toward intelligibles. Understand what this adds to the earlier definition.
This highlights the need to remove distractions for divine illumination.
5
Reflect on the unity of the definitions
Consider how these definitions together depict nazar as a purposeful intellectual movement toward unknown knowledge, not just random thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يفسر بملاحظة المعقول لتحصيل المجهول، ويراد بالمعقول الحاصل عند العقل، واحداً كان أو أكثر تصوراً كان أو تصديقاً، علماً كان(۱) أو ظناً أو جهلاً مركباً، فلا يفتقر إلى شيء من التكلفات السابقة. وفي كلام الإمام أن نظر البصيرة أشبه بنظر البصر، فكما أن من يريد إدراك شيء ببصره يقطع نظره عن سائر الأشياء ويحرك حدقته من جانب إلى جانب إلى أن يقع في مقابله ذلك الشيء فيبصره . كذلك من يريد إدراك شيء ببصيرته يقطع نظره عن سائر الأشياء ويحرك حدقة عقله من شيء إلى شيء إلى أن يحصل له العلوم المرتبة المؤدية إلى ذلك المطلوب . فمن ههنا يقال : النظر تجريد الذهن عن الغفلات، بمعنى إخلائه عن الصوارف والشواغل العائقة عن إشراق النظر الإلهي ، الموجب لفيضان المطلوب، أو تحديق العقل نحو المعقولات طلباً(٢) لما بعده لفيضان المطلوب عليه
And it may be interpreted as observing the intelligible in order to attain the unknown, and by 'intelligible' is meant what is present in the intellect, whether one or many, concept or assent, knowledge or opinion or compound ignorance. So it does not need any of the previous contrivances. And in the words of the Imam, 'The sight of insight is similar to the sight of the eye; just as one who wants to perceive something with his eyes averts his gaze from other things and moves his pupil from side to side until that thing confronts him and he sees it, likewise one who wants to perceive something with his insight averts his gaze from other things and moves the pupil of his intellect from thing to thing until he obtains the ordered knowledges leading to that desired [object].' Hence it is said: 'Nazar is stripping the mind of heedlessness, meaning emptying it of distractions and preoccupations that hinder the illumination of divine nazar, which necessitates the flow of the desired, or directing the intellect toward the intelligibles, seeking what is after it for the flow of the desired upon it.'
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative definition of nazar: 'observing the intelligible in order to attain the unknown.' Al-Taftazani explains that 'intelligible' (ma'qul) here means whatever is present in the intellect, whether one or many, concept or assent, knowledge, opinion, or compound ignorance. This definition avoids the earlier complications of having to specify known or opined premises. Al-Taftazani then quotes Imam al-Razi's analogy comparing intellectual sight (nazar al-basira) to physical sight (nazar al-basr). Just as one seeking to see an object averts his gaze from other things and moves his eyes until the object is in front of him, the seeker of intellectual understanding averts his attention from other things and moves the 'pupil of his intellect' from one idea to another until he obtains the ordered knowledges leading to what he seeks. From this analogy comes a further definition: nazar is stripping the mind of heedlessness (ghaflat), meaning removing distractions that block divine illumination, or directing the intellect toward intelligibles in pursuit of what follows, so that the desired knowledge flows upon it.

Correction: The passage defines 'intelligible' broadly to include opinion and even compound ignorance, so nazar can begin with imperfect knowledge. It is an active process of directing the intellect, not just deduction.

Definition of Nazar and Its Distinction from Imagination
Al-Taftazani defines nazar as goal-oriented thought and distinguishes it from imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of nazar (rational reflection) as used by the mutakallimun, how it is distinguished from imagination (takhayyul), and the importance of its goal-oriented nature.

Core Idea
Nazar is thought aimed at knowledge or opinion, not aimless imagination.

In this passage, al-Taftazani clarifies a foundational term in Islamic theology: nazar. The mutakallimun defined it precisely to separate it from other mental activities. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the epistemology behind kalam arguments. Let's look at the definition and why imagination is excluded.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to the key terms: fikr, takhayyul, ma'ani, and ghaya.
2
Identify the core definition and the role of ghaya
Notice that the mutakallimun defined nazar as 'the thought by which knowledge or opinion is sought'. The word 'ghaya' (goal) is central—it distinguishes nazar from other movements of the soul.
3
Understand the distinction between fikr and takhayyul
Al-Taftazani explains that fikr is movement in meanings (intelligibles), while takhayyul is movement in sensed things. Think about why this distinction matters for theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان امتياز النظر عن سائر حركات النفس بالغاية في غاية الظهور، حتى إن شيئاً من تفاسيره لا تخلو عن إشارة إليها، ذهب المتكلمون إلى أنه الفكر الذي يطلب به علم أو ظن والمراد بالفكر حركة النفس في المعاني واحترز بقيد المعاني عن التخيل على ما قال في شرح الإشارات: إن الفكر قد يطلق على حركة النفس بالقوة، التي آلتها مقدم البطن الأوسط(۳) في الدماغ، أي حركة كانت إذا كانت تلك الحركة في المعقولات، وأما إذا كانت في المحسوسات فقد تسمى تخيلاً فما وقع في المواقف أن المراد به الحركات(٤) التخييلية ليس كما ينبغي، والصواب ما ذكر في شرح الأصول: إنه انتقال
And since the distinction of nazar from other movements of the soul by its goal is extremely obvious, such that none of its interpretations are free from an indication to it, the mutakallimun held that it is the thought (fikr) by which knowledge or opinion is sought. And by 'thought' is meant the movement of the soul in meanings (ma'ani). And the restriction to 'meanings' guards against imagination (takhayyul), as stated in Sharh al-Isharat: 'Thought may be applied to the movement of the soul in potentiality, whose instrument is the anterior part of the middle ventricle of the brain, whatever movement it is, when that movement is in intelligibles. But if it is in sensed things, it may be called imagination.' So what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif that by it is meant imaginative movements is not as it should be. The correct [view] is what is mentioned in Sharh al-Usul: that it is a transition.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of nazar adopted by the mutakallimun (theologians): it is 'the thought (fikr) by which knowledge or opinion is sought.' Al-Taftazani explains that the purpose of nazar—its goal (ghaya)—distinguishes it from other mental movements. He clarifies that fikr here means the movement of the soul in meanings (ma'ani), i.e., concepts and intellectual content, not sensory images. The phrase 'in meanings' excludes imagination (takhayyul), which is movement in sensed things. Al-Taftazani cites Sharh al-Isharat: thought (fikr) is a movement of the soul in potentiality, whose physical instrument is the anterior part of the middle ventricle of the brain, when that movement is in intelligibles. If it is in sensed things, it is called imagination, not thought. He then criticizes the view in al-Mawaqif that the movements referred to are imaginative (takhayyuliya), saying this is incorrect. The correct view, according to Sharh al-Usul, is that it is a transition (intiqal). Thus, the passage establishes a terminological distinction crucial for understanding Islamic epistemology: nazar is rational thought aiming at knowledge or opinion, as opposed to free-flowing imagination connected to sensory content.

Key Terms
فكر
تخيل
معاني
محسوسات
الغاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Its reliance on sense perception
B) Its goal (ghaya) of seeking knowledge or opinion
C) Its use of imaginative movements
D) Its physical location in the brain

Key Takeaway
Nazar is rational thought directed at acquiring knowledge or probable opinion, distinct from free imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Definition of Nazar: Two Descriptions
Author gives two descriptions of nazar and presents the mutakallimun's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two technical definitions of nazar given by the author and why the mutakallimun define it as the thinking by which knowledge or opinion is sought.

Core Idea
Nazar is the mind's focused effort to attain knowledge or opinion.

The author begins by explaining the meaning of nazar, a central term in kalam. He provides two complementary descriptions that highlight its role in directing the mind toward divine illumination and rational inquiry. He then notes that the mutakallimun consistently define nazar as the thinking by which knowledge or opinion is sought, a definition that underlies all later discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the two descriptions of nazar
Read carefully the first sentence where nazar is described first as abstraction from heedlessness and then as focusing the intellect on intelligibles.
These are not competing definitions but complementary aspects.
2
Notice the mutakallimun's definition
After noting that nazar's distinction from other mental acts is obvious, the author presents the mutakallimun's definition: nazar is the fikr by which knowledge or opinion is sought.
This definition becomes standard in kalam.
3
Connect the descriptions to the definition
Reflect on how the two descriptions serve as preliminary explanations leading to the precise technical definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن ههنا يقال : النظر تجريد الذهن عن الغفلات، بمعنى إخلائه عن الصوارف والشواغل العائقة عن إشراق النظر الإلهي ، الموجب لفيضان المطلوب، أو تحديق العقل نحو المعقولات طلباً لما بعده لفيضان المطلوب عليه ولما كان امتياز النظر عن سائر حركات النفس بالغاية في غاية الظهور، حتى إن شيئاً من تفاسيره لا تخلو عن إشارة إليها، ذهب المتكلمون إلى أنه الفكر الذي يطلب به علم أو ظن والمراد بالفكر حركة النفس في المعاني واحترز بقيد المعاني عن التخيل على ما قال في شرح الإشارات: إن الفكر قد يطلق على حركة النفس بالقوة، التي آلتها مقدم البطن الأوسط في الدماغ، أي حركة كانت إذا كانت تلك الحركة في المعقولات، وأما إذا كانت في المحسوسات فقد تسمى تخيلاً فما وقع في المواقف أن المراد به الحركات التخييلية ليس كما ينبغي، والصواب ما ذكر في شرح الأصول: إنه انتقال النفس في المعاني انتقالاً بالقصد وعن سائر حركاتها، لا عن قصد وبالجملة هو بمنزلة الجنس للنظر على ما قال إمام الحرمين : أن الفكر قد يكون لطلب علم أو ظن فيسمى نظراً، وقد يكون كأكثر حديث النفس، فسقط اعتراض الآمدي بأن لفظ الفكر زائد لأن باقي الحد معن عنه ، واعتذاره بأنه لم يجعله جزءاً من الحد بل كأنه قال : النظر الفكر وهو الذي يطلب له علم أو ظن، وإن كان صحيحاً من جهة أن الفكر في الاصطلاح المشهور كالمرادف للنظر لا أعم منه، ليمتنع تفسيره بما يطلب به علم أو ظن. لكنه بعيد من جهة أن العبارة لا تدل عليه أصلا، ولم يعهد في التعريفات أن يقال الإنسان البشر الذي هو حيوان ناطق مثلا، على أن مجرد قولنا الذي يطلب به علم أو ظن لا يصلح تفسيراً للنظر والفكر إلا بتكلف، وأما اعتراضه بأن الظن قد لا يكون مطابقاً وهو جهل يمتنع أن يكون مطلوباً فمدفوع، بأن المطلوب هو الظن من حيث إنه ظن، وهو لا يستلزم طلب الأخص أعني غير المطابق
From here it is said: 'Nazar' is the abstraction of the mind from heedlessness, meaning its emptying of distractions and preoccupations that hinder the illumination of divine consideration, which necessitates the emanation of the sought thing; or it is the focusing of the intellect towards intelligibles in order to seek what is after it for the emanation of the sought thing upon it. And since the distinction of nazar from other movements of the soul is extremely evident, to the extent that none of its interpretations are free from reference to it, the theologians (mutakallimun) held that it is the thinking (fikr) by which knowledge or opinion is sought. And by 'fikr' is meant the movement of the soul among meanings, and by the qualifier 'meanings' it is distinguished from imagination (takhayyul), as was said in Sharh al-Isharat: 'Fikr may be applied to the movement of the soul by a power whose instrument is the anterior part of the middle ventricle of the brain, any movement, when that movement is among intelligibles. But if it is among perceptibles, it is called imagination (takhayyul).' So what occurred in al-Mawaqif that what is intended by it are imaginative movements is not as it should be. The correct view is what was mentioned in Sharh al-Usul: that it is the transition of the soul among meanings intentionally, and from its other movements, not intentionally. In short, it is like the genus for nazar, according to what Imam al-Haramayn said: 'Fikr may be for seeking knowledge or opinion, and it is then called nazar, and it may be like most inner speech.' Thus the objection of al-Amidi falls away, that the word 'fikr' is superfluous because the rest of the definition is sufficient for it, and his excuse that he did not make it a part of the definition but as if he said: 'Nazar is the fikr by which knowledge or opinion is sought' – even if this is correct from the standpoint that fikr in popular usage is synonymous with nazar, not more general, so that it is impossible to interpret it as that by which knowledge or opinion is sought, yet it is far-fetched because the expression does not indicate it at all, and it has not been customary in definitions to say 'insan is the human being who is a rational animal', for example, besides that our mere saying 'that by which knowledge or opinion is sought' is not suitable as an interpretation for nazar and fikr except with artificiality. As for his objection that opinion may not be in accordance [with reality] and is ignorance, and it is impossible that it be sought – it is refuted, because the sought thing is opinion insofar as it is opinion, and that does not entail seeking the specific [type] that is non-conforming.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage defines 'nazar' (consideration/reasoning) and distinguishes it from 'fikr' (thinking) and 'takhayyul' (imagination). The author begins by giving two descriptions of nazar: first, as abstraction of the mind from heedlessness to allow divine illumination, and second, as focusing the intellect on intelligibles to attain the sought knowledge. Since nazar's distinction from other mental acts is obvious, the mutakallimun define it as 'the fikr by which knowledge or opinion is sought.' The author then clarifies that 'fikr' is movement of the soul among meanings, excluding mere imagination (which is movement among perceptibles). He criticizes the view from al-Mawaqif that nazar involves imaginative movements, asserting instead that correct definition comes from Sharh al-Usul: fikr is intentional transition among meanings. He then notes that Imam al-Haramayn considered fikr as a genus for nazar, because fikr can be aimless inner speech or directed (then called nazar). This refutes al-Amidi's objection that including 'fikr' in the definition is redundant; the author explains that al-Amidi's attempted defense (that 'fikr' is merely an explanatory addition) is strained because definitions typically do not include synonyms in that manner. Finally, al-Amidi's objection that opinion cannot be a valid object of seeking because it may be false is dismissed: the object sought is opinion qua opinion, not necessarily a specifically non-conforming opinion.

Key Terms
النظر
الذهن
المعقولات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nazar is any kind of thinking, including aimless mental activity.
Correction: Nazar is specifically thinking directed at seeking knowledge or dominant opinion, as stated in the mutakallimun's definition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the mutakallimun's definition of nazar?
A) Abstraction of the mind from heedlessness
B) Focusing the intellect on intelligibles
C) The thinking by which knowledge or opinion is sought
D) Movement of the soul among meanings

Key Takeaway
Nazar is technically defined as the thinking that seeks knowledge or opinion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you deliberately cleared your mind of all distractions to seek knowledge of Allah? What prevented you, and what will you do about it today?
?
Is your daily thinking purposeful—seeking truth—or do you let your thoughts drift? What would change if you treated every moment of reflection as an act of worship?
?
If nazar is the thinking that seeks knowledge, how much of your thinking is actually directed at knowing Allah and His religion? What is one distraction you can remove this week to make your reflection more intentional?

What Is Kalam?

Distinguishing Nazar from Fikr and Takhayyul
Author defines fikr, distinguishes from takhayyul, critiques al-Mawaqif, and presents correct view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how fikr is defined, how it differs from takhayyul, and why the author criticizes the view in al-Mawaqif.

Core Idea
Fikr is intentional movement among meanings; takhayyul is among perceptibles.

Having given the basic definition of nazar, the author now clarifies the term 'fikr' used in that definition. He explains that fikr is movement of the soul among meanings, intentionally, which distinguishes it from mere imagination (takhayyul). He also critiques a rival definition from al-Mawaqif and supports the view from Sharh al-Usul, citing Imam al-Haramayn to show that fikr is a genus for nazar.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of fikr
Read how fikr is defined as movement of the soul among meanings, and note the qualifier 'meanings' to exclude imagination.
This definition is from Sharh al-Isharat.
2
Compare with takhayyul
Notice that takhayyul is movement among perceptibles, not meanings. The author rejects the view in al-Mawaqif that nazar involves imaginative movements.
3
See the correct view and the genus relation
The author endorses the definition from Sharh al-Usul: fikr is intentional transition among meanings. Then he presents Imam al-Haramayn's point that fikr when directed becomes nazar.
This shows that fikr is broader (genus) and nazar is a specific type.

A) Fikr is movement among perceptibles, takhayyul among intelligibles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How much of your daily thinking about faith is intentional pursuit of knowledge (nazar) versus idle, wandering thoughts? What would it take for you to transform more of your mental activity into purposeful reflection?
?

When you imagine worldly scenarios or dwell on material things, are you aware that you are engaging in takhayyul rather than fikr? How can you train yourself to redirect your imagination toward intellectual and spiritual meanings?
?
Imam al-Haramayn says that fikr can be like inner speech (unintentional). How much of your inner dialogue is unintentional chatter, and how much is deliberate consideration of the truth? What step will you take today to increase your deliberate, thoughtful engagement with your beliefs?

Refuting al-Amidi's Objections to the Definition
Author refutes al-Amidi's two objections to the definition of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's two objections to the definition of nazar and how the author refutes them.

Core Idea
Including 'fikr' in the definition is not redundant, and seeking opinion is valid.

The author now addresses critical objections raised by al-Amidi against the mutakallimun's definition of nazar. The first objection is that the word 'fikr' is superfluous. The second is that seeking opinion is invalid because opinion may be false. The author carefully dismantles both objections, showing that al-Amidi's own attempted defense is strained, and that opinion is sought qua opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection and the author's response
Read al-Amidi's claim that the word 'fikr' is redundant because the rest of the definition suffices. Then see how the author refutes it by noting that al-Amidi's own excuse is far-fetched and not customary in definitions.
2
Examine the second objection and refutation
Al-Amidi argues that opinion (zann) may not conform to reality and is ignorance, so it cannot be sought. The author responds that what is sought is opinion in itself, not necessarily the non-conforming kind.
3
Grasp the overall refutational structure
The author uses logical analysis and reference to definitional conventions to uphold the standard definition against al-Amidi's criticisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception: Including 'fikr' in the definition of nazar is redundant, and seeking opinion is invalid because opinion can be false.
Correction: The author shows that 'fikr' is not redundant and that seeking opinion (zann) is valid because it is sought in its general sense, not specifically false opinion.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute al-Amidi's objection that seeking opinion is invalid because opinion may be false?
A) He argues that opinion is never false.
B) He states that what is sought is opinion in general, not specifically false opinion.
C) He admits that seeking opinion is problematic but defends it by necessity.
D) He redefines opinion as certain knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Al-Amidi's objections to the definition of nazar are refuted: 'fikr' is not redundant, and seeking opinion is valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a time when you dismissed a valid approach because it seemed redundant or imperfect (like al-Amidi's objection). How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider your assumptions about the validity of seeking opinion (zann) in matters of faith? Will you now be more open to considering opinions that are not certain, as long as they are sought sincerely?
?
This lesson defends seeking opinion as a valid goal. In your own life, do you ever avoid seeking knowledge because you fear you might not reach certainty? How can you change that to embrace the process of searching, even when the outcome is only probable?
?
The author argues that 'the sought thing is opinion insofar as it is opinion.' This means opinion is pursued not for its truth value but for its nature as a considered judgment. Can you think of an area in your religious practice where you have settled for less than certainty but have not even tried to seek a reasoned opinion? Imam al-Haramayn's Definition of Nazar and the Redundancy Objection
Imam al-Haramayn defines nazar as purposeful thought and defends against al-Amidi's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Haramayn defines nazar as intentional thought and how he defends it against the charge that the term 'fikr' is redundant.

Core Idea
Nazar is not just any thought; it is thought with a purpose.

In this passage, Imam al-Haramayn clarifies that nazar is a specific kind of thought: the soul's intentional movement toward understanding. He then addresses an objection by al-Amidi that the term 'fikr' is unnecessary in the definition. This lesson unpacks the definition, the objection, and the defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the technical terms: nazar, fikr, hads, qasd.
2
Identify the definition of nazar
Find where nazar is defined as the soul's intentional transition among meanings. Note what it excludes (hads, non-intentional thought).
The definition serves as a genus for nazar.
3
Understand al-Amidi's objection
Al-Amidi claimed that 'fikr' is redundant because the rest of the definition already covers its meaning. Identify why he thought so.
He viewed fikr as synonymous with nazar, not a separate element.
4
Examine the defense
The defense argues that fikr is retained as a genus to specify the type of thought. The definition implicitly means 'fikr that seeks knowledge or conjecture'.
This is a common technique in definitions: using a broader term to set the context.
5
Reflect on the role of genus in definitions
Consider why including a genus like 'fikr' is important for clarity, even if it seems redundant to some.
It prevents confusion with other mental processes like intuition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن النفس في المعاني انتقالاً بالقصد وكأنه احترز بالقصد عن الحدس سائر حركاتها، لا عن قصد وبالجملة هو بمنزلة الجنس للنظر على ما قال إمام الحرمين : أن الفكر قد يكون لطلب علم أو ظن فيسمى نظراً، وقد يكون كأكثر حديث النفس، فسقط اعتراض الآمدي بأن لفظ الفكر زائد لأن باقي الحد معن عنه، واعتذاره بأنه لم يجعله جزءاً من الحد بل كأنه قال : النظر الفكر وهو الذي يطلب له علم أو ظن، وإن كان صحيحاً من جهة أن الفكر في الاصطلاح المشهور كالمرادف للنظر لا أعم منه، ليمتنع تفسيره بما يطلب بـه علم أو ظن. لكنه بعيد من جهة أن العبارة لا تدل عليه أصلا، ولم يعهد في التعريفات أن يقال الإنسان البشر الذي هو حيوان ناطق مثلا، على أن مجرد قولنا الذي يطلب به علم أو ظن لا يصلح تفسيراً للنظر والفكر إلا بتكلف
And concerning the soul's transition among meanings by intention—as if he avoided by 'intention' the other movements of the soul that are not intentional. In short, it is like a genus for nazar according to what Imam al-Haramayn said: that thought may be for seeking knowledge or conjecture, in which case it is called nazar, or it may be like most self-talk. Thus al-Amidi's objection that the word 'fikr' is redundant because the rest of the definition covers its meaning falls away. And his excuse that he did not make it a part of the definition but rather said: 'nazar is thought by which knowledge or conjecture is sought,' although it is correct from the aspect that 'fikr' in common usage is synonymous with 'nazar' and not more general, so it is not possible to define it as 'that by which knowledge or conjecture is sought,' but it is far-fetched because the expression does not indicate that at all, and it is not customary in definitions to say, for example, 'man is the human who is a rational animal,' and moreover, merely saying 'that by which knowledge or conjecture is sought' is not a valid interpretation of nazar and fikr except with difficulty.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage defines 'nazar' (speculative thought) and addresses an objection about redundancy. It begins by describing the definition: nazar is the soul's intentional transition among meanings, excluding non-intentional movements and 'hads' (intuition). The author notes that this definition functions as a genus for nazar. Then it presents Imam al-Haramayn's statement that thought (fikr) can be for seeking knowledge or conjecture—this is called nazar—or it can be mere self-talk. Al-Amidi objected that the word 'fikr' in the definition is redundant because the rest of the definition ('seeking knowledge or conjecture') already covers it. The response defends the inclusion by explaining that 'fikr' serves as a genus and that the definition implicitly says 'nazar is the thought that seeks knowledge or conjecture.' Although this interpretation is plausible given that 'fikr' and 'nazar' are often synonymous, the passage points out that the phrasing does not explicitly support it and that such implicit additions are uncommon in definitions. Thus, the objection is dismissed.

Correction: The passage shows that 'fikr' is kept as a genus to specify the type of thought, ensuring that nazar is understood as intentional, purposeful thought and not confused with unrelated mental processes.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Haramayn defines nazar as intentional thought (fikr) directed toward knowledge or conjecture, defending the inclusion of 'fikr' as an essential genus.

Al-Baqillani's Definition of Nazar and al-Amidi's Defense
Al-Baqillani defines nazar as seeking knowledge or dominant conjecture; al-Amidi defends it against a new objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Baqillani's definition of nazar, the objection that it does not cover seeking original conjecture, and how al-Amidi defended the definition.

Core Idea
Nazar seeks knowledge or dominant conjecture, and that includes seeking the original conjecture.

Al-Baqillani, a leading Ash'ari theologian, defined nazar (speculative reflection) as the seeking of knowledge or dominant conjecture. However, an objector argued that this formulation omits the act of seeking the very first occurrence of conjecture before it becomes dominant. In this lesson, we examine the objection and al-Amidi's nuanced response, which shows that the definition is comprehensive enough to include the search for original conjecture.

Guided Path

The objector claims al-Baqillani's definition does not cover seeking the original conjecture (aṣl al-zann). Ask yourself: why might someone think that?
3
Analyze al-Amidi's response
Al-Amidi argues that the definition covers three aims: seeking knowledge, seeking conjecture, and seeking dominance of conjecture. Notice how 'seeking conjecture' includes the original conjecture.
The word 'aw' (or) is inclusive, not restrictive.

The phrase 'or' (aw) indicates that any of these three is a specific property (khāṣṣa) of nazar. Thus, the definition is comprehensive enough to include seeking original conjecture as part of seeking conjecture in general.

Partial Properties and the Role of 'Or' in Definition
A rebuttal and counter-rebuttal on defining by partial properties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection against using partial properties in definitions, the function of 'or' for division versus ambiguity, and why al-Amidi's appeal to 'ghalabat zann' is contested.

Core Idea
In definitions, 'or' can divide, not confuse, but only if each alternative covers the whole meaning.

This passage presents a sharp exchange between the author of al-Mawaqif and al-Amidi about how to define a complex concept like 'ilm (knowledge) when its instances vary. The debate hinges on whether a definition can use partial properties without listing all of them, and whether the word 'or' can indicate legitimate division or only ambiguity. Al-Amidi's response introduces the idea that 'conjecture' (zann) is inherently preponderant, but the author finds this move unconvincing. Let's work through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text once without analyzing. Then follow the literal translation. Let the structure of objection–response–critique settle before you interpret.
The passage ends mid-sentence; focus on the complete argument up to that point.
2
Identify the core objection
The objection from al-Mawaqif says that limiting a definition to some properties is only valid if each property is comprehensive (khāṣṣa shāmila). Since none of the three properties (knowledge, conjecture, dominance) are comprehensive, one must list all of them using division.
Pay attention to the word 'aw'—the objector insists it should indicate division, not ambiguity.
3
Examine al-Amidi's response and its critique
Al-Amidi argues that 'conjecture' (zann) is expressed as 'dominance of conjecture' because preponderance (rajāḥān) is part of its real definition. The author finds this unclear: inherent preponderance does not justify substituting it with 'ghalabat zann'.
Consider the alternative interpretation: perhaps 'dominance' is added for specification (ikhtiṣāṣ), not as a genitive construction.
4
Reflect on the role of 'aw' and comprehensive property
The passage ends by suggesting that all three properties might actually be comprehensive. Think about what makes a property 'comprehensive' and why the debate matters for defining kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا خلل في الاقتصار على بعض الخواص ورده في المواقف بأن هذا إنما يكون في الخاصة الشاملة، وظاهر أن شيئاً من الثلاثة ليست كذلك، ولهذا لم يجز الاقتصار على قولنا يطلب به علم لخروج ما يطلب به ظن بل وجب في تعريف الشيء بالخواص التي لا يشمل كل منها إلا بعض أقسامه أن يذكر الجميع بطريق التقسيم تحصيلاً لخاصة شاملة لكل فرد هي كونه على أحد الأوصاف، وتقع كلمة « أو » لبيان أقسام المحدود لا للإبهام والترديد لينافي التحديد، فأجاب بأن الظن هو المعبر عنه بغلبة الظن، لأن الرجحان مأخوذ في حقيقته، إذ هي الاعتقاد الراجح وهذا عذر غير واضح لأن اعتبار رجحان الحكم في حقيقته لا يصلح مصححاً، أو باعثاً على التعبير عنه برجحان الظن، اللهم إلا أن يريد أن إضافة الغلبة إليه للاختصاص، أي الرجحان المعتبر في الظن، وليست من إضافة المصدر إلى الفاعل، بمعنى كون الظن غالباً راجحاً . وقد يقال إن كلاً من الثلاث خاصة شاملة، إذ ليس المراد طلب العلم أو
And there is no fault in limiting to some properties. It was rebutted in al-Mawaqif that this only applies to the comprehensive property, and it is apparent that none of the three is like that. Hence it is not permissible to limit to our saying 'seeking knowledge' because it excludes what seeks conjecture; rather, in defining a thing by properties each of which covers only some of its parts, one must mention all by way of division to obtain a comprehensive property for every individual, which is its being upon one of the descriptions. And the word 'or' occurs to clarify the divisions of the defined, not for ambiguity and hesitation that would contradict definition. So [al-Amidi] answered that conjecture is expressed by 'dominance of conjecture' because preponderance is included in its reality, since it is the preponderant belief. But this excuse is not clear, because considering the preponderance of the judgment in its reality is not a valid justification or motivator for expressing it by the preponderance of conjecture, unless he means that the addition of 'dominance' to it is for specification, i.e., the preponderance considered in conjecture, and it is not from the addition of the source to the agent, meaning conjecture being predominant. And it may be said that each of the three is a comprehensive property, because what is meant is not seeking knowledge or...
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on definitional properties. An argument from al-Mawāqif objectes that limiting a definition to some properties (like only 'seeking knowledge') is only valid if each property is comprehensive (khāṣṣa shāmila). Since the three properties (knowledge, conjecture, dominance) are each partial, the correct method is to list them all using division. The word 'aw' should indicate division (taqsīm) of the defined term, not ambiguity (ibhām). In response, al-Āmidī argues that 'conjecture' (zann) is properly expressed as 'dominance of conjecture' because rajāḥān (preponderance) is inherent to its definition as preponderant belief. The author notes that this response is unclear, as the inherent rajāḥān does not justify substituting it with 'ghalabat zann'. A possible interpretation is that the addition of 'ghalaba' is for specification (ikhtiṣāṣ), not for the genitive construction. The passage ends with a further point that perhaps all three are in fact comprehensive properties. The text is cut off, but the argument clearly critiques al-Āmidī's position.

Key Terms
الخاصة الشاملة (al-khāṣṣa al-shāmila)
التقسيم (al-taqsīm)
الإبهام (al-ibhām)
الترديد (al-tardīd)
الرجحان (al-rajāḥān)
الاعتقاد الراجح (al-iʿtiqād al-rājiḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'aw' always creates ambiguity, but in definitions it can indicate legitimate division of the defined concept.
Correction: In definitions, 'aw' is used to list alternatives that together cover all instances, provided each alternative is a comprehensive property. The objection in the passage insists on this usage.

Checkpoint
According to the objection from al-Mawaqif, when is it permissible to limit a definition to some properties?
A) When each property is comprehensive (khassa shāmila)
B) When the properties are numerous
C) When the definition uses 'aw'
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The debate highlights that valid definitions require each property to be comprehensive; otherwise, all partial properties must be listed via division, with 'or' serving a precise logical role.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Your heart knows the difference between certainty and a strong guess. Are you willing to honestly inventory your 'certainties' and see which are truly built on clear proof, and which are only preponderant opinion? What would you change if you did?

Scope of 'ilm and nazar in Kalam
Commentator clarifies the scope of 'ilm and nazar in kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun restrict the terms 'ilm and nazar to tasdiqat (judgments) rather than including tasawwurat (conceptions).

Core Idea
In kalam, 'ilm and nazar are about judgments, not concepts.

The commentator clarifies a crucial terminological point: when the mutakallimun speak of 'ilm (knowledge) and nazar (speculative reflection) without qualification, they mean them in the context of tasdiqat (affirmative judgments), not tasawwurat (conceptions). This lesson unpacks that distinction and its importance for the study of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Pay attention to the commentator's opening statement.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two definitions mentioned
The passage references definitions of 'ilm and nazar. Locate these and note what they would cover if taken generally.
The definitions include 'ilm as an attribute causing distinction and nazar as reflection seeking knowledge.
3
Understand the commentator's point about forced interpretation
The commentator says that interpreting these definitions to include both tasawwur and tasdiq is 'takalluf' (forced). Grasp why this is significant.
This shows the mutakallimun's technical usage is narrower.
4
Reflect on why this restriction matters
Consider how narrowing 'ilm and nazar to judgments affects the scope of kalam as a discipline.
Kalam deals with propositions, not mere concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا وظاهر كلام المتكلمين أنهم يريدون بالعلم والنظر عند الإطلاق ما يخص التصديقات، وأن ما ذكرنا في قولهم العلم صفة توجب تمييزاً لا يحتمل النقيض والنظر فكر يطلب به علم أو ظن أنه يعم التصور والتصديق تكلف منا.

Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage defines the technical terms 'ilm (knowledge) and nazar (speculative thought) as used by the mutakallimun. The commentator states that when these terms are used without qualification, they refer specifically to tasdiqat (judgments or assertions) rather than tasawwurat (concepts). He then notes that the definition of 'ilm as 'an attribute that necessitates a distinction that does not admit its opposite' and nazar as 'reflection by which knowledge or opinion is sought' would, if taken generally, include both tasawwur and tasdiq. But the commentator clarifies that such a general understanding is a forced interpretation (takalluf); the mutakallimun's actual usage restricts them to tasdiqat.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun restrict 'ilm and nazar to judgments, not concepts, in their technical usage.

The Ash'ari View on Nazar Producing Knowledge
One short descriptive phrase (max 15 words).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that reasoned reflection (nazar) produces knowledge through Allah's habitual creation, not by necessity, and the two views on the servant's power.

Core Idea
Reasoned reflection leads to knowledge by Allah's habitual creation, not by human power.

This lesson introduces the foundational Ash'ari epistemology of reasoned reflection (nazar). Before exploring arguments about God, we must understand how the Ash'aris view the process of reasoning itself. The author states that knowledge results from nazar by Allah's will, not by inherent causality, and then notes a division among Ash'aris about the servant's role.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's own formulation sink in.
Notice the technical terms: al-nazar, ijra' al-ada, kharaq al-ada, al-kasb.
2
Identify the key mechanism
The passage states that Allah creates knowledge at the completion of nazar by way of habit (ijra' al-ada). This is not a necessary causal connection; it is a repeated divine act that could be broken (kharaq al-ada).
Contrast this with views that claim nazar necessitates knowledge by itself.
3
Distinguish the two groups
Note the two groups within the Ash'ari school: one holds that knowledge is solely from eternal power (servant only presents premises), the other holds it is acquired (kasbi) and within the servant's power.
This difference concerns the role of human capacity, not the divine creation of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يشير إلى كيفية إفادة النظر للعلم فعندنا هي بخلق الله تعالى العلم عقيب تمام النظر، بطريق إجراء العادة أي تكرر ذلك دائماً من غير وجنوب، بل مع جواز أن لا يخلقه عن طريق خرق العادة وذلك لما سيجيء من استناد جميع الممكنات إلى قدرة الله تعالى واختياره ،ابتداء وأثر المختار لا يكون واجبا تم القائلون بهذا المذهب فرقتان . منهم من جعله بمحض القدرة القديمة من غير أن يتعلق به قدرة العبد وإنما قدرته على إحضار المقدمتين وملاحظة وجود النتيجة فيهما بالقوة ومنهم من جعله كسبياً مقدوراً
It indicates how reasoned reflection (al-nazar) yields knowledge. According to us (the Ash'aris), it is by Allah the Most High creating knowledge at the completion of nazar, by way of the habit of acting (ijra' al-ada), i.e., it repeats that always without necessity, but with the permissibility that He does not create it by way of breaking the habit, due to what will come of the dependence of all possibles upon Allah's power and choice, from the beginning, and the effect of the chooser is not necessary. Then the proponents of this school are two groups: some of them made it (the knowledge) solely by the eternal power without the servant's power being connected to it, but his power is only to present the two premises and to notice the existence of the conclusion in them potentially; and some of them made it acquired (kasbi) and within his power (maqdur).
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage explains how reasoned reflection (nazar) results in knowledge according to the Ash'ari school. It begins by stating that according to the Ash'aris, Allah creates knowledge immediately after the completion of nazar, not through any necessary causal connection but through the habitual practice (ijra' al-ada) of Allah, meaning that this occurs repeatedly without fail, though it is not obligatory upon Allah—it is within His power to break the habit and not create knowledge (kharaq al-ada). This is because all contingent things depend on Allah's power and choice, and the effect of a chooser is not necessitated. Then it notes that those who hold this view are divided into two groups: one holds that knowledge is created solely by eternal power without any involvement of the servant's power—the servant's power is only to bring the two premises to mind and to notice the conclusion potentially; the other group holds that the knowledge is acquired (kasbi) and within the servant's power (maqdur). This presents the foundational Ash'ari position on the epistemology of nazar.

العلم
خلق العلم
إجراء العادة
خرق العادة
القدرة القديمة
الكسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nazar necessarily produces knowledge by its own power without divine intervention.
Correction: No, the Ash'ari view is that knowledge is a direct creation of Allah after nazar by His habitual practice, not by any inherent necessity in the act of reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view described in this passage, how does nazar produce knowledge?
A) By necessary causation between nazar and knowledge.
B) By Allah creating knowledge after nazar through His habitual practice, not by necessity.
C) By the servant's power directly causing knowledge.
D) By the Mu'tazilite theory of generation (tawlid).

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view is that knowledge from nazar is a divine creation by habit, not a necessary effect, and scholars differ on whether the servant's power plays an effective role (kasbi) or not.

?
