Definition of Correct Inquiry and Its Four Components
The author defines correct nazar and enumerates its four components with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of correct inquiry (nazar sahih) and its four distinct components: dalil, madlul, dalala, and jiha al-dalala.

Core Idea
Correct inquiry means looking at the evidence from the right angle to reach the indicated truth.

In this passage, the author lays out the essential conditions for inquiry in Islamic theology. He clarifies that not any investigation counts—only one directed at genuine evidence (dalil) rather than sophistry (shubha), and from the proper aspect of indication. Through the classic example of inferring the Maker from the world, he distinguishes four distinct elements that are often conflated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic slowly, then the translation. Notice the technical terms: نظر صحيح, دليل, مدلول, دلالة, جهة الدلالة.
Do not worry about understanding everything at first.
2
Identify the two conditions
The passage states that nazar sahih is conditioned on two things: it must be inquiry into dalil (evidence) not shubha (sophistry), and it must be from the perspective of dalala (indication). Find these in the text.
These conditions distinguish theological inquiry from mere argumentation.
3
Walk through the example
The author gives the classic cosmological argument. Identify each component: what is the dalil? what is the madlul? what is the dalala? what is jiha al-dalala?
Notice that the evidence is the world itself, not the premises.
4
Understand the four components' distinctness
The passage emphasizes that these four are conceptually different. See how the definition of each gives a different kind of knowledge.
This distinction is important for avoiding confusion in later arguments.
5
Reflect on the importance for kalam
Think about how clear definitions prevent errors. If you confuse dalil with madlul, your argument collapses. This passage provides the basic conceptual toolkit.
This is foundational for all subsequent theological proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النظر الصحيح فيشترط أن يكون نظراً في الدليل دون الشبهة، وأن يكون النظر فيه من جهة دلالته وهي الأمر الذي بواسطته ينتقل الذهن من الدليل إلى المدلول. فإذا استدللنا بالعالم - على الصانع بأن نظرنا فيه وحصلنا قضيتين إحداهما أن العالم حادث والأخرى أن كل حادث له صانع ليعلم من ترتيبها أن العالم له صانع. فالعالم هو الدليل عند المتكلمين لا نفس المقدمتين المرتبتين على ما هو اصطلاح المنطق وثبوت الصانع هو المدلول، وكون العالم بحيث يفيد النظر فيه العلم بثبوت الصانع هو الدلالة، وإمكان العالم أو حدوثه الذي هو سبب الاحتياج إلى المؤثر هو جهة الدلالة وهذه الأربعة أمور متغايرة، بمعنى أن المفهوم من كل منها غير المفهوم من الآخر، فتكون العلوم المتعلقة بها متغايرة بحسب الإضافة.
As for correct inquiry (al-nazar al-sahih), it is conditioned upon being an inquiry into the evidence (dalil) rather than sophistry (shubha), and upon the inquiry being from the perspective of its indication (dalala), which is the thing by means of which the mind transfers from the evidence to the indicated (madlul). For example, when we infer by the world to the Maker: we look into it and obtain two propositions: one that the world is originated (hadith), and another that every originated thing has a maker, so that from their arrangement it is known that the world has a maker. So the world is the evidence according to the theologians (mutakallimun), not the two arranged premises as per the terminology of logic; the existence of the Maker is the indicated (madlul); the fact that the world is such that inquiry into it yields knowledge of the Maker's existence is the indication (dalala); and the possibility (imkan) or origination (huduth) of the world, which is the cause of need for an effecter (mu'aththir), is the aspect of indication (jiha al-dalala). These four things are distinct, meaning that the concept of each is different from the concept of the other, and thus the knowledge related to them differs by relation.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage defines correct nazar (inquiry) and clarifies its constitutive elements. It first states that correct nazar must be directed at evidence (dalil) rather than sophistry (shubha), and must consider the evidence from the aspect of its indication (dalala) — the means by which the mind moves from evidence to conclusion. The author illustrates with the classic example: inferring the existence of the Maker (al-Sani') from the world (al-'alam). The theologian's dalil is the world itself (not the two premises as in logic). The madlul is the existence of the Maker. The dalala is the fact that the world is such that examining it yields knowledge of the Maker. The jiha al-dalala is the aspect of the world — its possibility or origination — that necessitates the need for an effecter. These four (world as evidence, Maker as indicated, the indicative relationship, and the specific aspect) are distinct concepts, so knowledge of each is different relative to its object.

دليل (dalil)
مدلول (madlul)
دلالة (dalala)
جهة الدلالة (jiha al-dalala)
شبهة (shubha)
مقدمات (muqaddimat)
منطق (mantiq)
إمكان (imkan)
حدوث (huduth)
مؤثر (mu'aththir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the evidence (dalil) is the logical premises (muqaddimat), as in Aristotelian logic.
Correction: In kalam, the evidence is the concrete object (e.g., the world) itself, not the premises. The premises are used in reasoning but the evidential object is the focus.

Checkpoint
In the theological example given, what is the 'aspect of indication' (jiha al-dalala)?
A) The world itself
B) The existence of the Maker
C) The possibility or origination of the world
D) The arrangement of the two premises

Key Takeaway
Correct inquiry requires focusing on evidence from the aspect of its indication, and this involves four distinct concepts: the evidence itself, the indicated conclusion, the indicative relationship, and the specific aspect that makes the evidence point to the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you genuinely examine it as evidence for its Maker, or do you often get distracted by doubts (شبهات)? What specific habit can you change to ensure your inquiry is always into the dalil and not the shubha?
?
The passage shows that correct inquiry requires focusing on the 'aspect of indication'—the very property that makes the world need a Creator. Are you truly convinced that the world's existence depends on God, or have you allowed intellectual complacency to blur that urgency? What would it take for you to internalize this reasoning as a daily reality?
?
You have learned that the four components of nazar are distinct. Have you ever confused the evidence (e.g., nature) with the conclusion (God) or the method? How can you guard against such confusion in your own thinking about faith?

The First Obligation: Different Views
The author enumerates opinions on the first obligation and states the correct view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three opinions on the first obligation and the author's resolution based on the definition of obligation.

Core Idea
The first obligation depends on whether we define obligation as an end or a means.

The author presents a classic debate in kalam: what is the very first thing a person is obligated to do? Is it to know God, to reflect, or even to doubt? The answer hinges on how we define 'obligation'. Let's examine the arguments and the correct view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the three opinions listed: knowledge, reflection, and doubt.
Pay attention to the refutation of the doubt opinion.
2
Identify the three opinions
List the three opinions mentioned: 1) Knowledge of God, 2) Reflection or intention to reflect, 3) Doubt. Understand why each was proposed.
Knowledge is the foundation; reflection is needed for knowledge; doubt is a precursor to reflection.
3
Understand the author's resolution
The author says the truth depends on how we define obligation. If obligation is something intended for its own sake, knowledge is first. If it includes means, reflection is first.
Knowledge itself is not within capacity, so its obligation is restricted.
4
Consider the special case
For the imitator or one with compound ignorance, the obligation is direct reflection on evidence to lead to knowledge.
This shows the practical application for those lacking certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس في أول الواجبات قال : المبحث الخامس : اختلفوا في أول الواجبات فقيل : معرفة الله تعالى (۱) لأنها الأصل ، وقيل : النظر فيها أو القصد إليه لتوقفها عليه ، (۲) والحق أنه إن قيد الواجب بما يكون مقصوداً في نفسه فالأول. وإلا فالثاني. وأما المعرفة فليس بمقدور، إذ الوجوب مقيد به واستدامته ليست بمقدمة . وقيل : الشك (۳) لأن النظر بعده ، ورد بأنه ليس بمقدور لكونه من الكيف كالعلم ولا مقدمة لتأتي النظر عند الظن والوهم، وإن أريد به ما يتناولهما وجعل مقدوراً بمعنى إمكان تحصيله فوجوب النظر مقيد به، إذ لا نظر عند الجزم والواجب على المقلد أو الجاهل جهلاً مركباً هو النظر في وجه الدلالة، ليقوده إلى العلم .
The fifth topic: On the first of obligations. He said: The fifth topic: They differed regarding the first of obligations. It was said: Knowledge of God (1) because it is the foundation. And it was said: Reflection concerning it or the intention to it because knowledge depends on it. (2) And the truth is that if obligation is restricted to what is intended in itself, then the first. Otherwise, then the second. As for knowledge, it is not within capacity, since obligation is restricted by it and its continuation is not a premise. And it was said: Doubt (3) because reflection comes after it. And it is refuted by the fact that it is not within capacity because it is a quality like knowledge, and it is not a premise for reflection to occur upon presumption and imagination. And if by it is meant what encompasses both and it is made within capacity in the sense of possibility of attainment, then the obligation of reflection is restricted by it, since there is no reflection upon certainty. And what is obligatory upon the imitator or the one with compound ignorance is reflection on the aspect of evidence to lead him to knowledge.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of different opinions regarding the first obligation (أول الواجبات) incumbent upon a person. The author lists three opinions: (1) Knowledge of God (معرفة الله تعالى) because it is the foundation of all obligations. (2) Reflection on God (النظر) or the intention to reflect (القصد إلى النظر) because knowledge depends on reflection. (3) Doubt (الشك) because reflection comes after doubt. The author then states the correct view (الحق): if 'obligation' is defined as something intended for its own sake, then knowledge is the first obligation; otherwise, reflection is first. He refutes the opinion of doubt by arguing that doubt is not within human capacity (مقدور) because it is a quality like knowledge, and it is not a premise for reflection because reflection occurs even with presumption or imagination. If doubt is redefined as a broader concept within capacity, then the obligation of reflection would depend on it, but reflection does not occur with certainty. Finally, the author notes that for the imitator (مقلد) or one with compound ignorance, the obligation is to reflect on the evidence to attain knowledge.

الواجب
المقلد
الجهل المركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the first obligation is always knowledge of God without considering the role of reflection.
Correction: The first obligation depends on the definition of obligation: if it is intended for its own sake, knowledge is first; otherwise, reflection is first.

Checkpoint

A) When obligation is defined as what is intended in itself.
B) When obligation is defined as a means to another end.

Key Takeaway
The first obligation depends on the definition: if obligation is an end in itself, knowledge is first; if it includes means, reflection is first; doubt is not a valid candidate.

Two Views on the First Duty of the Mukallaf
Presentation of two scholarly opinions on the first duty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between two major scholarly opinions regarding the first duty of a morally responsible person in Islamic theology: knowledge of God versus reflection leading to knowledge.

Core Idea
The first duty: is it knowledge or reflection? The scholars differ.

In determining what a morally responsible person (mukallaf) must do first, scholars have offered different answers. This passage presents two key opinions: one prioritizing direct knowledge of God, the other prioritizing reflection (nazar) as the necessary premise. Understanding this difference is foundational for studying kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. It is a short but dense passage stating two opinions. Note the key terms: الشيخ, الأستاذ, معرفة الله, النظر.
The footnote identifies al-Shaykh as Abu Hashim al-Jubbai, a leading Mu'tazilite.
2
Identify each opinion and its reasoning
Al-Shaykh says the first duty is knowledge of God because obligations are built on knowledge. Al-Ustadh says it is reflection on knowledge of God because reflection is the premise that leads to knowledge. Compare the two.
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this difference matters. If the first duty is knowledge, then that knowledge must already be present. If it is reflection, then the duty is to engage in rational inquiry. This shapes the entire approach to theology.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يجب على المكلف، فقال الشيخ (٤) : هو معرفة الله تعالى لكونها مبنى الواجبات وقال الأستاذ : هو النظر في معرفة الله تعالى لما مر من كونه المقدمة .
What is obligatory upon the one charged with duties (mukallaf)? So al-Shaykh (4) said: It is knowledge of God Most High because it is the foundation of obligations. And al-Ustadh said: It is reflection on knowledge of God Most High because of what preceded that it is the premise.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents a brief comparative statement of two scholarly opinions regarding the first duty incumbent upon the morally responsible person (mukallaf). The author cites two authorities: 'al-Shaykh' (identified in a footnote as Abu Hashim al-Jubbai, a leading Mu'tazilite) and 'al-Ustadh' (likely al-Ash'ari or another figure, but not specified in the text). Al-Shaykh holds that the first duty is knowledge of God (معرفة الله تعالى) because it is the foundation upon which all other obligations are built. Al-Ustadh holds that the first duty is reflection (النظر) on knowledge of God, because reflection is the premise that leads to knowledge, as previously established. The passage does not give a final resolution but presents both views.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Al-Shaykh makes knowledge itself the duty, while al-Ustadh makes reflection the duty. They differ on which comes first: the end (knowledge) or the means (reflection).

Division of Nazar's Product into Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Al-Taftazani divides the product of nazar into tasawwur and tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the product of nazar is divided into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent), and the types of paths (definers and proofs) that lead to each.

Core Idea
The path to knowledge is either a definition for concepts or a proof for judgments.

Al-Taftazani begins the sixth inquiry by explaining that the completion of nazar results in acquiring a path that leads to a sought. This sought is either a conception (tasawwur) or an assent (tasdiq). For conceptions, the path is a definition (hadd or rasam); for assents, it is a proof (dalil) which includes syllogisms, induction, and analogy. Understanding this division is foundational for mastering Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms used.
2
Identify the two types of matlub
The passage states the matlub is either tasawwuri (conceptual) or tasdiqi (assertoric). Find these and note the different paths that lead to each.
3
Explore the types of proofs
The passage lists several types of proofs: syllogisms (exceptional, categorical), induction, and analogy. Observe the subcategories mentioned.
This prepares you for later lessons on epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس
انقسامه إلى التصور والتصديق
(قال) : المبحث السادس : كمال النظر تحصيل طريق، يوصل (۱) بالذات إلى مطلوب، إما تصوري وهو المعروف، حدا(۲) ورسماً تاماً وناقصاً، إذ لا بد من مميز ذاتي أو عرضي مع الجنس القريب أو بدونه وإما تصديقي وهـو الدليل، إما قياساً استثنائياً متصلاً أو منفصلاً ، أو اقترانياً حملياً وشرطياً وإمـا استفزازاً تاماً وناقصاً وإما تمثيلاً قطعياً أو ظنياً، إذ لا بد من اندراج المطلوب تحت الدليل أو بالعكس، أولهما تحت ثالث .
قد سبقت إشارة إلى أن الحركة الأولى : من النظر تحصيل مادة مركب يوصل إلى المطلوب والثانية صورته والمطلوب إما تصور أو تصديق فالموصل إلى التصور ويسمى المعرف إما حد أو رسم ، وكل منهما إما تام أو ناقص، لأن التمييز أمر لا بد منه في التعريف، لامتناع المعرفة بدون التمييز عند العقل، فالمميز إن كان ذاتياً للماهية يسمى المعرف حداً لأنه اللغة المنع،
في
(1) أي توصل بذاتها من غير حاجة إلى ضم مقدمة أجنبية كما في قياس المساواة، فإن تلك الحاجة تنفي كمال النظر .
(۲) ينقسم الحد إلى تام وناقص : فالتام هو ما يتركب من الجنس والفصل القريبين كتعريف الإنسان بالحيوان الناطق .
والناقص : هو ما يكون بالفصل القريب وحده أو به وبالجنس البعيد كتعريف الإنسان بالجسم الناطق. ومن شرط الحد التام أن يكون جامعاً مانعاً أي يجمع الحدود، ويمنع غيره من الدخول فيه، ومن شرطه أيضاً أن يكون مطرداً ومنعكساً. ومعنى الاطراد: أنه متى وجد الحد عدم المحدود، ولو لم يكن مطرداً لما كان مانعاً ، ولو لم يكن منعكساً لما كان جامعاً .
The sixth inquiry: Its division into tasawwur and tasdiq. He said: The sixth inquiry: The completion of nazar is the acquisition of a path that leads (1) essentially to a sought, either tasawwuri which is the known, as a complete and incomplete hadd and rasam (2), because there must be a distinctive [feature] essential or accidental with the proximate genus or without it; or tasdiqi which is the proof, either an exceptional syllogism, conjunctive or disjunctive, or a categorical syllogism, predicative or conditional, or a complete and incomplete induction, or a certain or probable analogy, because there must be subsumption of the sought under the proof or vice versa, or both under a third. A previous indication has passed that the first movement of nazar is the acquisition of the matter of a composite that leads to the sought, and the second is its form. And the sought is either tasawwur or tasdiq. So that which leads to tasawwur is called the definer, either hadd or rasam, and each is either complete or incomplete, because distinction is necessary in definition, since knowledge without distinction is impossible for the intellect. So the distinctive if it is essential to the quiddity is called hadd because in language it means prevention.
In
(1) That is, it leads essentially without need to add an extraneous premise, as in the syllogism of equality, for that need negates the completion of nazar.
(2) The hadd is divided into complete and incomplete: the complete is what is composed of the proximate genus and differentia, like defining man as rational animal. And the incomplete is what is by the proximate differentia alone or by it and the distant genus, like defining man as rational body. Among the conditions of the complete hadd is that it be comprehensive and exclusive, i.e., it gathers the limits and prevents others from entering into it, and also that it be regular (mutarid) and reflexive (mun'akis). The meaning of regular is: that whenever the hadd exists, the mahdud does not exist; if it were not regular, it would not be exclusive; and if it were not reflexive, it would not be comprehensive.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the product of nazar. The author begins by stating that the completion of nazar consists of acquiring a path that essentially leads to a sought (matlub). The sought is divided into two types: tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). For tasawwur, the path is the 'definer' (mu'arraf), which is either a hadd (definition) or a rasam (description), each of which is either complete or incomplete. The reason is that definition requires a distinctive feature (mumayyiz), which can be essential (dhati) or accidental (aradi), with or without the proximate genus. For tasdiq, the path is the 'proof' (dalil), which includes various types of syllogisms (exceptional, conjunctive, disjunctive, categorical, predicative, conditional), induction (complete and incomplete), and analogy (certain or probable). The subsumption of the sought under the proof (or vice versa) is necessary. The author then reminds that the first movement of nazar is acquiring the matter of a composite that leads to the sought, and the second is its form. The sought is either tasawwur or tasdiq. The path to tasawwur is called the definer, which is either hadd or rasam, each being complete or incomplete. Distinction is necessary because knowledge without distinction is impossible. If the distinctive is essential to the quiddity, it is called hadd (definition); if accidental, it is rasam. The footnote explains the conditions of a complete hadd: it must be comprehensive (jami') and exclusive (mani'), and also regular (mutarid) and reflexive (mun'akis). 'Regular' means that whenever the hadd exists, the mahdud (defined thing) does not exist (i.e., they are mutually exclusive in extension), and 'reflexive' ensures that the hadd covers all instances of the mahdud.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that both tasawwur and tasdiq are reached by proofs.
Correction: The passage clarifies that tasawwur is reached by a definer (hadd/rasam), not by a proof.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of path leads to a conceptual sought (tasawwuri)?
A) A proof (dalil)
B) A definer (hadd or rasam)
C) A syllogism
D) An analogy

Key Takeaway
The product of nazar is either a conception requiring a definition or an assent requiring a proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to understand a new Islamic concept, do you first clarify its definition (tasawwur) or jump to proving it (tasdiq)? What does this lesson reveal about the proper order of seeking knowledge?
?
Think of a belief you hold strongly. Is it based on a clear concept or a proof? How might confusing the two affect the strength of your belief?
?
If you have ever struggled to 'prove' something about Allah, could it be that you were actually lacking a clear concept? How can you apply the distinction between tasawwur and tasdiq to your own search for certainty?

Conditions of a Complete Hadd
Al-Taftazani states the conditions of a complete hadd: comprehensive, exclusive, regular, reflexive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions of a complete hadd: being comprehensive (jami‘), exclusive (mani‘), regular (mutarid), and reflexive (mun‘akis).

Core Idea
A complete definition must cover all instances and exclude outsiders.

After distinguishing hadd from rasam, al-Taftazani explains the conditions that make a complete hadd valid: it must be comprehensive (including all members of the defined) and exclusive (excluding others), and also regular and reflexive in logical terms. These conditions ensure precision in definition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the need for distinction
The passage begins by repeating that definition requires a distinctive feature. Review why distinction is necessary for knowledge.
This sets the stage for the conditions.
2
Learn the four conditions
Identify the four conditions: jami' (comprehensive), mani' (exclusive), mutarid (regular), mun'akis (reflexive). Note their definitions.
The order in the text: jami', mani', then mutarid and mun'akis.
3
Grasp mutarid and mun'akis
The passage explains mutarid: whenever the hadd exists, the mahdud does not exist (i.e., the hadd is not present without the mahdud? Actually, it says: when the hadd exists, the mahdud does not exist? That seems off. The footnote says: 'The meaning of regular is: that whenever the hadd exists, the mahdud does not exist.' This is counterintuitive; careful: Actually, 'mutarid' often means that the definition applies to all instances (i.e., if it is a cat, it is an animal). The footnote might be misphrased. Better to stick with the literal translation and explain. In classical logic, mutarid means that the definition is present in all instances of the defined, and mun'akis means that the defined is present in all instances of the definition. So 'mutarid' means that if something is a man (mahdud), then it is a rational animal (hadd) – the hadd is present. But the footnote says 'whenever the hadd exists, the mahdud does not exist'. This seems reversed. Possibly a typo. We'll explain the standard interpretation.
Standard interpretation: mutarid = the hadd covers all individuals of the mahdud; mun'akis = all individuals that have the hadd are individuals of the mahdud.

المبحث السادس

(قال) : المبحث السادس : كمال النظر تحصيل طريق، يوصل (۱) بالذات إلى مطلوب، إما تصوري وهو المعروف، حدا(۲) ورسماً تاماً وناقصاً، إذ لا بد من مميز ذاتي أو عرضي مع الجنس القريب أو بدونه وإما تصديقي وهـو الدليل، إما قياساً استثنائياً متصلاً أو منفصلاً ، أو اقترانياً حملياً وشرطياً وإمـا استفزازاً تاماً وناقصاً وإما تمثيلاً قطعياً أو ظنياً، إذ لا بد من اندراج المطلوب تحت الدليل أو بالعكس، أولهما تحت ثالث .

The Two Products of Nazar: Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Classification of nazar's result into tasawwur and tasdiq with methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of sought results from nazar—conceptual and assertive—and the methods used to attain each, including definitions and proofs.

Core Idea
Nazar yields either a concept or an assertion, each with its own path of proof.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of what rational consideration (nazar) seeks to attain. The author divides the product into two categories: tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assertion). Each category has specific methods—definitions for concepts and various proofs for assertions. Understanding this division is crucial for navigating the rest of the text on logic and argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is a single dense sentence enumerating categories.
2
Identify the two main categories
The passage divides the sought thing into tasawwuri (conceptual) and tasdiqi (assertive). Mark where each is introduced.
The words 'إما تصوري وإما تصديقي' are the key.
3
Examine the methods for tasawwur
Notice that tasawwur is attained through hadd (definition) and rasm (description), each complete or incomplete. Understand the need for a distinguisher (mumayyiz).
The distinguisher can be essential (dhati) or accidental (aradi).
4
Examine the methods for tasdiq
Tasdiq is attained through dalil (proof), which includes four types: qiyas istithna'i, qiyas iqtirani, istiqra, and tamthil. Each type has subtypes.
The passage specifies the conditions for each: conjunct/disjunct, categorical/conditional, complete/incomplete, certain/probable.
5
Understand the condition of proof
The passage concludes that the sought thing must fall under the proof, or the proof under it, or both under a third. This ensures logical connection.
This condition is essential for the proof to be valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس : كمال النظر تحصيل طريق، يوصل بالذات إلى مطلوب، إما تصوري وهو المعروف، حداً ورسماً تاماً وناقصاً، إذ لا بد من مميز ذاتي أو عرضي مع الجنس القريب أو بدونه وإما تصديقي وهو الدليل، إما قياساً استثنائياً متصلاً أو منفصلاً، أو اقترانياً حملياً وشرطياً وإما استقراءً تاماً وناقصاً وإما تمثيلاً قطعياً أو ظنياً، إذ لا بد من اندراج المطلوب تحت الدليل أو بالعكس، أولهما تحت ثالث.
The sixth investigation: The perfection of rational consideration (nazar) is the attainment of a path that leads by essence to a sought thing. It is either conceptual (tasawwuri), which is the known thing, by definition (hadd) and description (rasm), complete and incomplete, because there must be a distinguisher, essential or accidental, with the proximate genus or without it; or assertive (tasdiqi), which is the proof (dalil), whether it be an exceptional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i), conjunct or disjunct, or a conjunctive (qiyas iqtirani) categorical and conditional, or complete and incomplete induction (istiqra'), or certain or probable analogy (tamthil), because the sought thing must fall under the proof or vice versa, or both under a third.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth topic: the perfection of nazar results in a path leading to a sought item. It divides the sought item into two: a conception (tasawwur) and an assent (tasdiq). For conception, the path is through definition (hadd) or description (rasm), each either complete (tamm) or incomplete (naqis). The necessity of a distinguisher, either essential or accidental, is highlighted, with or without the proximate genus. For assent, the path is through proof (dalil), which includes various types: exceptional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) which can be conjunct or disjunct, conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) which can be categorical or conditional, complete or incomplete induction (istiqra'), and certain or probable analogy (tamthil). The condition is that the sought must be included under the proof, or vice versa, or both under a third. This sets the foundation for the detailed discussion.

Correction: The passage clearly states that tasawwur is attained by both hadd and rasm, each with complete and incomplete forms.

Detailed Conditions and Types of Definitions (Hadd and Rasm)
Classification and conditions of definitions (hadd/rasm, tām/nāqiṣ, musāwin).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between hadd and rasm, the concepts of complete and incomplete definitions, and the later scholars' condition of equivalence (musāwin).

Core Idea
Definitions must be precise: essential for hadd, accidental for rasm, and always equivalent.

Building on the earlier overview of nazar, this passage dives into the types of definers (muʻarrif) used for conceptions. It clarifies that a definition must provide complete distinction, and it categorizes definitions as complete or incomplete based on whether they include the full genus and differentia. The later scholars added the key requirement that the definer be exactly coextensive with the defined—neither broader nor narrower.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author’s own classification come through.
Notice how the passage moves from general categories to specific conditions.
2
Identify the two main types of definers
The passage distinguishes between hadd and rasm. Note what makes each one fit for definition: essential vs. accidental distinguisher.
The terms 'hadd' and 'rasm' are rooted in Arabic usage.
3
Understand complete vs. incomplete
For each definer, there is a complete and an incomplete version. See how completeness is determined by inclusion of the proximate genus and differentia.
The complete hadd is unique: proximate genus + proximate differentia.
4
Grasp the later condition of equivalence
The later scholars insisted that any proper definer must be musāwin (coextensive). Ask yourself why a broader definition would be problematic.
This condition ensures precision and avoids circular definitions.
5
Review the misconception about accidental differentia
The text warns against thinking that a combination of proximate differentia with a common accident could form a hadd. Confirm that only pure essentials count for hadd.
The majority restrict hadd to pure essentials.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد سبقت إشارة إلى أن الحركة الأولى : من النظر تحصيل مادة مركب يوصل إلى المطلوب والثانية صورته والمطلوب إما تصور أو تصديق فالموصل إلى التصور ويسمى المعرف إما حد أو رسم ، وكل منهما إما تام أو ناقص، لأن التمييز أمر لا بد منه في التعريف، لامتناع المعرفة بدون التمييز عند العقل، فالمميز إن كان ذاتياً للماهية يسمى المعرف حداً لأنه اللغة المنع، ولا بد في المعرف من منع خروج شيء من الأفراد، ودخول شيء فيه مما سواها، فما كان ذلك فيه باعتبار الذات والحقيقة كان أولى بهذا الاسم، وإن كان عرضياً لها المعرف رسماً لكونه بمنزلة الأثر يستدل به على الطريق، سمي . ثم إن المميز إن كان مع كمال الجزء المشترك أعني ما يقع جواب السؤال بما هو عن الماهية وعن كل ما يشاركها وهو المسمى بالجنس القريب فالمعرف تام، أما الحد فلاشتماله على جميع الذاتيات وأما الرسم فلاشتماله على كمال الذاتي المشترك، وكمال العرضي المميز، وإلا فناقص، فالحد التام واحد ليس إلا وهو الجنس القريب مع الفصل القريب ويشترط تقديم الجنس حتى لو أخر لكان الحد ناقصاً، ومبنى هذا الكلام على أنه لا اعتبار بالعرض العام لأنه لا يفيد الامتياز، ولا الاطلاع على أجزاء الماهية ولا بالخاصة مع الفصل القريب، وإلا يلزم أن يكون المركب من الفصل القريب مع العرض العام أو مع الخاصة حداً ناقصاً ، وليس كذلك في اصطلاح الجمهور، حيث خصوا اسم الحد بما يكون من محض الذاتيات وقد يصطلح على تسمية كل معرف حداً حتى اللفظي منه أعني بيان مدلول اللفظ بلفظ آخر أوضح دلالة، وكثير من المتقدمين على أن الرسم التام ما يفيد امتياز الماهية عن جميع ما عداها، والناقص ما يفيد الامتياز عن البعض فقط، إلا أنه استقر رأي المتأخرين على اشتراط كون المعرف مساوياً أي مطرداً، ومنعكساً، حتى لا يجوز التعريف بالأعم محافظة على الضبط
There has been previous indication that the first movement of rational consideration (nazar) is the attainment of the material of a composite that leads to the sought thing, and the second is its form. The sought thing is either a conception or an assent. The means to the conception, called the definer (muʻarrif), is either a definition (hadd) or a description (rasm), each of which is either complete or incomplete. Because distinction is necessary in definition, since knowledge is impossible without distinction according to the intellect. If the distinguisher is essential to the quiddity, the definer is called a definition (hadd), because it is in the language 'prevention', and the definer must prevent any individual from exiting and any extraneous thing from entering. So if that prevention is by virtue of essence and reality, it is more worthy of this name. If the distinguisher is accidental to it, the definer is called a description (rasm), because it is like a trace used to indicate the path. Then, if the distinguisher is accompanied by the completeness of the common part—namely what answers the question 'what is it?' about the quiddity and about everything that shares in it, which is called the proximate genus—then the definer is complete. As for the definition, it is complete because it includes all essentials; as for the description, it is complete because it includes the complete common essential and the complete accidental distinguisher. Otherwise, it is incomplete. The complete definition is only one, which is the proximate genus with the proximate differentia, and it is required to present the genus; if it were delayed, the definition would be incomplete. This discussion is based on the fact that the common accident is not considered because it does not provide distinction or knowledge of the parts of the quiddity, nor is the proper accident with the proximate differentia, otherwise the composite of the proximate differentia with the common accident or with the proper accident would be an incomplete definition, which is not the case according to the terminology of the majority, who restrict the name 'definition' to what is composed of pure essentials. But it is sometimes agreed to call every definer a definition, even the verbal one, i.e., explaining the meaning of a word with another more clearly indicative word. Many of the earlier scholars held that the complete description is what gives distinction of the quiddity from all others, and the incomplete gives distinction only from some. However, the later scholars settled on the condition that the definer be equivalent (musāwin), i.e., coextensive (muṭṭarid) and conversive (munʻakis), so that definition by a broader term is not allowed, in order to preserve precision.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the definitions introduced earlier. It explains that the first movement of nazar obtains the material of a composite, and the second obtains its form. The sought thing is either a conception or an assent. The means to the conception is called the definer (muʿarrif), which is either a definition (hadd) or a description (rasm), each complete or incomplete. The necessity of distinction is stressed: without it, knowledge is impossible. If the distinguisher is essential to the quiddity, the definer is called hadd; if accidental, it is called rasm. The condition of preventing inclusion of extraneous things is required. Then it discusses completeness: if the distinguisher comes with the complete common part (proximate genus), the definer is complete. For hadd, completeness means including all essentials; for rasm, it means including the complete common essential and the complete accidental distinguisher. The complete hadd is only the proximate genus with the proximate differentia, with the genus presented first. The common accident and proper accident are not considered because they do not provide distinction. The majority restrict hadd to pure essentials, but some call every definer a hadd, including verbal definition. Earlier scholars defined complete rasm as distinguishing from all others, incomplete as from some. Later scholars settled that the definer must be equivalent (musāwin), i.e., coextensive and conversive, so that broader terms are not allowed.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a combination of the proximate differentia with a common accident forms a valid definition (hadd).
Correction: The text explains that the common accident does not provide distinction or knowledge of the quiddity's parts, and according to the majority such a composite would not be a hadd at all.

Checkpoint
What condition did later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirun) require for the definer (mu'arrif)?
A) It must be composed of pure essentials
B) It must be equivalent (musāwin), i.e., coextensive and conversive
C) It must include the proximate genus
D) It must prevent any individual from exiting

Key Takeaway
A proper definer must distinguish the defined from everything else using essential attributes (hadd) or accidental ones (rasm), and must be exactly equivalent in scope.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define your identity as a Muslim, do you rely more on essential attributes (like belief and action) or accidental ones (like culture or appearance)? Is your definition complete or incomplete according to the standards of this passage? What would it take to ensure it is equivalent (musāwin) to what Allah expects?
?
The passage warns against definition by a broader term to preserve precision. In your understanding of key Islamic concepts like 'iman' or 'taqwa', have you ever accepted vague or overgeneralized meanings? What precision might you be missing?
?
Distinction is necessary for knowledge. Examine your own heart: are your distinctions between halal and haram, truth and falsehood, as sharp as a complete definition? Where have you allowed blurriness, and what step will you take today to bring them into clear focus?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate

The Definition of Dalil and Its Key Features
The author defines dalīl, distinguishes it from amāra, and explains the processes of inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of evidence (dalīl) in Islamic logic and theology, its essential features, and how it differs from mere indication (amāra).

Core Idea
Dalīl is that which, through sound reflection, yields knowledge—whether certain or conjectural.

The author begins by clarifying the core term dalīl, which is central to all theological reasoning. He defines it as that which, through sound reflection, leads to a ruling, using the example of the world pointing to the Creator. He then distinguishes it from amāra, which yields only conjecture, and explains the processes of istidlāl and ta'līl.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two definitions of dalīl
The passage presents a broad definition (that through sound reflection one arrives at a ruling) and a logical definition (arranged premises producing a conclusion). Note how the author uses the example of the world for the Maker.
The broad definition includes both certain and conjectural outcomes.
2
Understand the conditions: sound reflection and possibility
The author stresses that evidence remains evidence even if not reflected upon, and that reflection must be sound (not corrupted by error).
3
Grasp the distinction between dalīl and amāra, and the two inferential processes
Dalīl often yields certainty; amāra yields conjecture. Istidlāl goes from effect to cause; ta'līl goes from cause to effect.
This distinction is crucial in theological argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وقد يقال الدليل لما يمكن التوصل بصحيح النظر فيه إلى حكم كالعلم للصانع، وكثيراً ما يختص بالجارم وتقابله الأمارة في اصطلاح المنطق هو المقدمات المرتبة المنتجة للمطلوب. وقد يقال للأمر الذي يمكن أن يتأمل فيه وتستنبط [؟] منه المقدمات المرتبة كالعالم للصانع، فيفسر بما يمكن التوصل بصحيح النظر فيه إلى حكم قطعياً كان أو ظنياً، وذكر الإمكان لأن الدليل لا يخرج عن كونه دليلاً بعدم النظر فيه، وقيد النظر بالصحيح لأنه لا توصل بالفاسد إليه، وذلك بأن لا يكون النظر فيه من جهة دلالته، وأطلق الحكم ليتناول التفسير الأمارة، وكثيراً ما يخص الدليل بما يفيد العلم، ويسمى ما يتوصل به إلى الظن أمارة والاستدلال هو التوصل المذكور، وقد يخص بما يكون من الأثر إلى المؤثر، كالتوصل بالنظر في العالم إلى الصانع، ويسمى عكسه تعليلاً كالتوصل بالنظر في النار إلى الإشراق، أي إلى التصديق بذلك، وما يقال إن الدليل هو الذي يلزم من العلم به العلم بوجود المدلول فمعناه العلم بتحقق النسبة إيجاباً كان أو سلباً، من غير اعتبار وصف المدلولية، حتى كأنه قيل بتحقق شيء آخر وهو المدلول، وحينئذ لا يخرج مثل الاستدلال بنفي الحياة على نفي العلم، ولا يلزم اليدور بناء على تضايف الدليل والمدلول، وذلك لأن الدليل عندهم اسم لما يفيد التصديق دون التصور، والعلم قسم من التصديق يقابل الظن، وعلى هذا فمعنى العلم بالدليل إذا حملناه على مثل العالم للصانع هو العلم بما يؤخذ [؟] من النظر في وجه دلالته من المقدمات المرتبة، مع سائر الشرائط التي من جملتها التفطن لجهة الإنتاج وكيفية الاندراج، إذ لا يلزم العلم بالمدلول إلا حينئذ، ولا يقال العلم بالنتيجة لازم للعلم بالمقدمات المرتبة إلا أنه قد يفتقر إلى وسط لكونه غير بين لأنا نقول لو كان كذلك لامتنع تحقق العلم الأول بدون الثاني، كالمثلث لا يتحقق بدون تساوي زواياه القائمتين والموقوف على الوسط إنما هو العلم بذلك، والحاصل أن اللازم يمتنع انفكاكه عن الملزوم بيناً كان أو غير بين، والتفرقة إنما تظهر في العلم باللزوم وبتحقق اللازم. (قال) : والدليل إن لم يتوقف على نقل أصلاً فعقلي وإلا فنقلي، سواء توقف كل من مقدماته القريبة على النقل أو لا وقد يخص النقل الأول ويسمى الثاني مركباً، أما النقلي المحض فباطل إذ لا بد من ثبوت صدق المخير بالعقل، والمطلوب إن استوى طرفاه عند العقل
He said: And the term 'evidence' (dalīl) may be applied to that by which, through sound reflection upon it, one can arrive at a ruling, such as the world [is evidence] for the Maker. And frequently it is specified for the categorical [syllogism] and is opposed by 'indication' (amāra). In the terminology of logic, it is the arranged premises that produce the required conclusion. And it may be applied to the thing which can be reflected upon and from which the arranged premises are deduced, such as the world for the Maker. Thus it is interpreted as that by which, through sound reflection upon it, one can arrive at a ruling, whether certain or conjectural. And [the author] mentioned possibility because evidence does not cease to be evidence by not reflecting upon it. And he restricted reflection to sound because through unsound reflection one does not arrive at it. And that is by not reflecting upon it from the aspect of its indication. And he generalized the ruling to include the interpretation of 'indication'. And frequently evidence is specified as that which yields knowledge, and that by which one arrives at conjecture is called 'indication'. And 'inference' (istidlāl) is the aforementioned arriving. And it may be specified as being from the effect to the cause, like arriving through reflection on the world to the Maker, and its opposite is called 'explanatory reasoning' (ta'līl), like arriving through reflection on fire to illumination, i.e., to belief in that. And what is said that evidence is that from knowledge of which follows knowledge of the existence of the indicated object, its meaning is knowledge of the realization of the relation, whether affirmative or negative, without considering the attribute of being indicated, such that it is as if it is said [to be] the realization of something else, namely the indicated. And then the likes of inference by negating life to negate knowledge would not be excluded. And no circularity is required based on the correlation of evidence and the indicated. That is because evidence, in their terminology, is a name for that which yields assent, not conception. And knowledge is a division of assent opposite to conjecture. And on this basis, the meaning of knowledge of evidence, if we apply it to the likes of the world for the Maker, is knowledge of what is taken from reflection on the aspect of its indication of the arranged premises, along with the rest of the conditions, among which is attention to the mode of production and the manner of subsumption. For knowledge of the indicated does not follow except then. And it is not said that knowledge of the conclusion is necessary from knowledge of the arranged premises, except that it may need a middle term because it is not evident. For we say: if it were so, the first knowledge would be impossible without the second, like a triangle does not exist without its angles equaling two right angles. What depends on the middle term is only knowledge of that. And the result is that the necessary cannot be separated from the necessitating, whether evident or not evident. The distinction only appears in knowledge of the entailment and the realization of the necessary. (He said): And evidence, if it does not depend on transmission at all, is rational; otherwise it is transmitted, whether every one of its proximate premises depends on transmission or not. And the first may be specified as transmitted and the second called composite. As for pure transmitted [evidence], it is invalid, for the truthfulness of the informant must be established by reason, and the required [conclusion] if its two sides are equal in reason.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition of the term 'evidence' (dalīl) as used in logic and theology, followed by its classification into rational and transmitted. The author begins by noting that dalīl can be understood as that through which, by sound reflection, one arrives at a ruling—using the example of the world as evidence for the Creator. In logic, it specifically refers to the arranged premises that produce the conclusion. He distinguishes between dalīl and 'indication' (amāra), where amāra leads only to conjecture. The author then explains that the definition includes the word 'possibility' because evidence remains evidence even if not reflected upon. He restricts 'reflection' to 'sound' because unsound reflection does not lead to the conclusion. The ruling is generalized to include both certain and conjectural knowledge, thereby encompassing amāra. He then notes that sometimes dalīl is restricted to that which yields certain knowledge, leaving amāra for conjecture. The term 'inference' (istidlāl) is the process of arriving at the conclusion, and it may be specifically used when reasoning from effect to cause (e.g., world to Creator); the reverse is called 'explanatory reasoning' (ta'līl), e.g., from fire to heat. He then addresses a definition: 'evidence is that from knowledge of which follows knowledge of the existence of the indicated object.' He clarifies that this means knowledge of the relation (affirmative or negative) without considering the attribute of being indicated, so that it covers cases like inferring the absence of knowledge from the absence of life. He rejects circularity based on the correlation of evidence and indicated object because evidence yields assent, not mere conception. He elaborates on the conditions for knowledge of the evidence, including understanding the mode of deduction. Finally, he classifies evidence into rational ('aqlī) and transmitted (naqlī). Transmitted evidence depends on revelation or testimony. Pure transmitted evidence is invalid because the truthfulness of the transmitter must be established by reason. Thus, rational evidence underlies all valid transmission.

Key Terms
الدليل
الأمارة
الاستدلال
التعليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the logical definition of dalīl in the terminology of logic?
A) That which yields certain knowledge
B) The arranged premises that produce the required conclusion
C) That which can be reflected upon
D) The process of inference from effect to cause

Key Takeaway
Dalīl is defined broadly as that which yields knowledge through sound reflection, and logically as arranged premises producing a conclusion; its types include certain evidence (dalīl) and conjectural evidence (amāra), and inference can be from effect to cause (istidlāl) or cause to effect (ta'līl).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you actively engage in sound reflection to arrive at knowledge of the Creator, or do you passively accept what you've been told? What would change if you took this process more seriously?
?
In your daily reasoning, are you careful to distinguish between certain evidence and mere indications (amāra)? Can you recall a time when you mistook a conjecture for certainty? How did that affect your judgment?
?
The passage teaches that evidence remains evidence even when not reflected upon. Are there truths in your life that you have not yet reflected upon? What evidence for God's existence, for example, have you left unexamined, and what step can you take today to begin sound reflection?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate

Rational and Transmitted Evidence
The author classifies evidence into rational and transmitted and explains the invalidity of pure transmitted evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the division of evidence into rational and transmitted categories, and why pure transmitted evidence is invalid without rational foundation.

Core Idea
All evidence is either rational or transmitted; pure transmitted evidence is impossible because reason must validate the transmitter.

After defining evidence, the author now classifies it based on its dependence on transmission. If it does not rely on transmitted reports at all, it is rational; if it does, it is transmitted. He also notes that pure transmitted evidence—one that depends entirely on transmission—is invalid because the truthfulness of the informant must be established rationally.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification and note the two categories
The author divides evidence into rational (ʿaqlī) and transmitted (naqlī). Transmitted evidence can depend on transmission in its proximate premises or not. Note the nuance.
2
Understand the 'pure transmitted' and 'composite' subcategories
The author mentions that sometimes the first (rational) is specified as 'transmitted' and the second as 'composite'. This might be confusing; focus on the main division.
The term 'composite' refers to evidence that mixes rational and transmitted elements.
3
Grasp why pure transmitted evidence is invalid
The author states that pure transmitted evidence is invalid because one must use reason to establish the truthfulness of the transmitter. The conclusion (the required belief) would be uncertain if both sides are equal in reason.
This is a key principle: reason is foundational for all knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition of the term 'evidence' (dalīl) as used in logic and theology, followed by its classification into rational and transmitted. The author begins by noting that dalīl can be understood as that through which, by sound reflection, one arrives at a ruling—using the example of the world as evidence for the Creator. The author then explains that the definition includes the word 'possibility' because evidence remains evidence even if not reflected upon. He restricts 'reflection' to 'sound' because unsound reflection does not lead to the conclusion. The ruling is generalized to include both certain and conjectural knowledge, thereby encompassing amāra. He then notes that sometimes dalīl is restricted to that which yields certain knowledge, leaving amāra for conjecture. The term 'inference' (istidlāl) is the process of arriving at the conclusion, and it may be specifically used when reasoning from effect to cause (e.g., world to Creator); the reverse is called 'explanatory reasoning' (ta'līl), e.g., from fire to heat. Thus, rational evidence underlies all valid transmission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on many transmitted teachings for your faith. Have you ever taken the time to rationally verify the trustworthiness of those who conveyed them? If not, what does that say about the foundation of your belief?
?
The passage says that pure transmitted evidence is invalid because reason must validate the informant. Is there any belief you hold solely because 'so-and-so said it' without ever questioning it? Will you now commit to examining one such belief with your reason?
?
If you accept a hadith or a scholar's statement without rational scrutiny, are you not falling into the trap of pure transmitted evidence? How can you ensure that your faith is built on a valid mixture of reason and transmission?

Evidence and Necessary Entailment in Kalām
Defines al-dalīl and explains necessary entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-dalīl in kalām and the nature of necessary entailment between evidence and indicated, including how it avoids circularity and distinguishes between assent and conception.

Core Idea
Evidence necessarily points to its indicated, but only when properly understood.

Before we can argue about theological claims, we must understand what counts as valid evidence. In this passage, the author defines al-dalīl and explains the necessary link between evidence and what it indicates. He addresses key concerns about circularity and the role of knowledge, setting the foundation for all subsequent reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, paying attention to the definition of al-dalīl and the key terms used.
The author uses a dense style; take your time.
2
Identify the two concerns
Notice how the author addresses two potential objections: that the definition might exclude valid inferences or lead to circularity.
The terms تضايف (mutual correlation) is crucial for the circularity argument.
3
Understand the epistemological distinction
Grasp the difference between تصديق (assent) and تصور (conception), and why evidence pertains to assent.
This distinction shapes how we know the Maker.
4
Grasp the necessity despite mediation
The final part clarifies that necessary entailment holds even when a middle term is needed; only our awareness of the entailment may require mediation.
This refutes the misconception that non-self-evident conclusions break necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يقال إن الدليل هو الذي يلزم من العلم به العلم بوجود المدلول فمعناه العلم بتحقق النسبة إيجاباً كان أو سلباً، من غير اعتبار وصف المدلولية، حتى كأنه قيل بتحقق شيء آخر وهو المدلول، وحينئذ لا يخرج مثل الاستدلال بنفي الحياة على نفي العلم، ولا يلزم الدور بناء على تضايف الدليل والمدلول، وذلك لأن الدليل عندهم اسم لما يفيد التصديق دون التصور، والعلم قسم من التصديق يقابل الظن، وعلى هذا فمعنى العلم بالدليل إذا حملناه على مثل العالم للصانع هو العلم بما يؤخذ من النظر في وجه دلالته من المقدمات المرتبة، مع سائر الشرائط التي من جملتها التفطن لجهة الإنتاج وكيفية الاندراج، إذ لا يلزم العلم بالمدلول إلا حينئذ، ولا يقال العلم بالنتيجة لازم للعلم بالمقدمات المرتبة إلا أنه قد يفتقر إلى وسط لكونه غير بين لأنا نقول لو كان كذلك لامتنع تحقق العلم الأول بدون الثاني، كالمثلث لا يتحقق بدون تساوي زواياه القائمتين، والموقوف على الوسط إنما هو العلم بذلك، والحاصل أن اللازم يمتنع انفکاکه عن الملزوم بيناً كان أو غير بين، والتفرقة إنما تظهر في العلم باللزوم وبتحقق اللازم.
And what is said: 'The evidence (al-dalīl) is that from knowledge of which knowledge of the existence of the indicated (al-madlūl) necessarily follows.' So its meaning is knowledge of the realization of the relation (al-nisba), whether affirmative or negative, without considering the attribute of being indicated (al-madlūliyya), so that it is as if it were said: the realization of something else, namely the indicated. In that case, something like inferring negation of life from negation of knowledge is not excluded, and circularity (al-dawr) does not follow based on the mutual correlation (al-taḍāyuf) of the evidence and the indicated. That is because the evidence, according to them, is a name for what yields assent (al-taṣdīq) rather than conception (al-taṣawwur), and knowledge is a part of assent in contrast to opinion (al-ẓann). On this basis, the meaning of knowledge of the evidence, if we apply it to something like the knowledge of the Maker (al-Ṣāni‘), is knowledge of what is taken from reflection on the aspect of its indication from the arranged premises, along with the other conditions, among which is paying attention to the direction of production (jiha al-intāj) and the manner of subsumption (kayfiyya al-indirāj). For knowledge of the indicated does not necessarily follow except then. And it is not said that knowledge of the conclusion necessarily follows from knowledge of the arranged premises, except that it may require a middle term because it is not self-evident. For we say: if it were so, then the realization of the first knowledge without the second would be impossible, like the triangle that cannot be realized without the equality of its two base angles. What depends on the middle term is only knowledge of that. The upshot is that the necessary (al-lāzim) cannot be separated from the necessitating (al-malzūm), whether it is evident or not. The distinction only appears in knowledge of the entailment (al-luzūm) and of the realization of the necessary.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage defines what constitutes a valid evidence (dalīl) in kalām and clarifies the necessary relation between evidence and indicated (madlūl). The author explains that the common definition—'that from knowledge of which knowledge of the existence of the indicated follows'—should be understood as knowledge of the relation (whether affirmative or negative) without considering the attribute of being indicated. He then addresses two potential concerns: (1) inferring negation of life from negation of knowledge is still valid as an evidence; (2) the relationship between evidence and indicated is not circular because they are mutually correlative (mutadāyifān). The author further distinguishes evidence as pertaining to assent (taṣdīq), not conception (taṣawwur). The knowledge of the evidence, when applied to the knowledge of the Maker, involves knowledge of the arranged premises and the conditions for valid inference, including the direction of production (how the conclusion is produced) and the manner of subsumption (how premises encompass the conclusion). He argues that knowledge of the conclusion necessarily follows from knowledge of the premises only when the premises are fully understood; if the conclusion is not self-evident, a middle term may be needed, but that does not detract from the necessary entailment. The key conclusion is that the necessary (lāzim) cannot be separated from the necessitating (malzūm), whether the entailment is self-evident or not; the difference lies only in our awareness of the entailment or of the realization of the necessary.

مدلول (madlūl)
لزوم (luzūm)
لازم (lāzim)
ملزوم (malzūm)
تصديق (taṣdīq)
تصور (taṣawwur)
ظن (ẓann)
تضايف (taḍāyuf)
مقدمات مرتبة (muqaddimāt murattaba)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a conclusion requires a middle term, the entailment is not necessary.
Correction: The author clarifies that the necessary relation between evidence and indicated remains; only our knowledge of the entailment may depend on a middle term. The necessary (lāzim) is inseparable from the necessitating (malzūm).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the definition of evidence (al-dalīl) refer to when it says 'knowledge of the existence of the indicated'?
A) Knowledge of the very essence of the indicated.
B) Knowledge of the realization of the relation, whether affirmative or negative, without considering the attribute of being indicated.
C) Knowledge of the premises only.
D) Knowledge of the conclusion without any conditions.

Key Takeaway
The evidence (dalīl) is defined by a necessary entailment that is unaffected by whether the conclusion is self-evident or requires a middle term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that the existence of the world necessarily entails a Creator—this is not a mere probability but a necessary entailment. Yet you often live as if this fact is optional or distant. What specific habit of thought or action do you need to change today so that you live in constant awareness of that necessary connection?
?
The evidence for the Creator is all around you, and the author says that from true knowledge of that evidence, knowledge of the Creator necessarily follows. If you do not feel that certainty, what are you missing in your reflection? Will you commit to daily, structured contemplation (tafakkur) on a single sign of creation to let that entailment become real for you?
?
You often rely on scholars or proofs (middle terms) to strengthen your faith, but the lesson teaches that the entailment itself is direct and unbreakable, even if you do not see it. Today, sit in silence and ask yourself: Is my belief in God a mere inference from arguments, or has it become a direct, necessary certainty from my own reflection? What step will you take to move from inference to direct witnessing?

Division of Evidence: Rational, Transmitted, and Composite
Al-Taftazani discusses the division of evidence and clarifies the rational basis of transmitted evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars divide evidence into rational, transmitted, and composite, and why transmitted evidence necessarily has a rational foundation.

Core Idea
Even transmitted evidence ultimately depends on rational premises.

This passage from al-Taftazani tackles a foundational issue in Islamic epistemology: how do we classify evidence? He presents the twofold and threefold divisions of evidence and immediately addresses a common misunderstanding—that 'transmitted' evidence is purely based on authority without any rational component. By reading this, you will see how even transmitted arguments must rest on rational foundations, and you will learn the precise definitions scholars use to avoid confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the full Arabic passage and the English translation to get an overview of the argument.
Notice that the author uses a conditional structure: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the two divisions
Find where al-Taftazani mentions the twofold division (rational & transmitted) and the threefold division (adding composite). Understand the difference.
The threefold division is mentioned as a potential misunderstanding.
3
Understand the rebuttal
The author argues that the impression that 'transmitted' means no rational premises is false. Trace his reasoning: chain of reporters must end with someone known by reason, otherwise circularity or infinite regress.
This is a key epistemological point in kalam.
4
Grasp the nuance about premises
Note the distinction between proximate and remote premises. The author explains that even transmitted arguments have rational remote premises, so they are not opposed to composite arguments but rather fall under them when considering the premises themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد يقسم إلى العقلي والنقلي، وقد يقسم إليهما وإلى المركب من العقلي والنقلي، وهذا يوهم أن المراد بالنقلي ما لا يكون شيء من مقدماته عقلياً وهو باطل، إذ لو لم تنته سلسلة صدق المخبرين إلى من يعلم صدقه بالعقل لزم الدور أو التسلسل، فدفع ذلك بأن من حصره فيهما أراد بالنقلي ما يتوقف شيء من مقدماته القريبة أو البعيدة على النقل والسماع من الصادق، وبالعقلي ما لا يكون كذلك، ومن ثلث القسمة أراد بالنقلي ما يكون جميع مقدماته القريبة نقلية كقولنا : الحج واجب وكل واجب فتاركه يستحق العقاب، وبالمركب ما يكون بعض مقدماته القريبة عملياً وبعضها نقلياً كقولنا : الوضوء عمل، وكل عمل فصحته الشرعية بالنية وكقولنا : الحج واجب وكل واجب فتاركه عاص، إذ لا معنى للعصيان إلا ترك امتثال الأوامر والنواهي وإنما قيد المقدمات بالقريبة لأن النقلي أيضاً بعض مقدماته البعيدة عقلية كما مر فلا يقابل المركب بل يندرج فيه هذا إن أريد بالدليل نفس المقدمات المرتبة، وأما إذا أريد مأخذها كالعالم للصانع والكتاب والسنة والإجماع للأحكام، فلا معنی للمركب
It may be divided into rational (ʿaqlī) and transmitted (naqlī), and it may be divided into these two and into the composite (murakkab) of rational and transmitted. This gives the impression that what is meant by 'transmitted' is that none of its premises is rational—and this is false, for if the chain of veracity of the reporters does not end with one whose veracity is known by reason, circularity or infinite regress would follow. So this is rebutted by saying that the one who confined it to two [categories] meant by 'transmitted' that which depends on some of its premises, whether proximate or remote, upon transmission and hearing from the truthful one, and by 'rational' that which is not so; and the one who made a threefold division meant by 'transmitted' that whose all proximate premises are transmitted, like our saying: 'Pilgrimage is obligatory, and every obligatory act is such that its abandoner deserves punishment,' and by 'composite' that whose some proximate premises are rational and some transmitted, like our saying: 'Ablution is an act, and every act has its legal validity through intention,' and like our saying: 'Pilgrimage is obligatory, and every obligatory act is such that its abandoner is disobedient,' for the meaning of disobedience is nothing but leaving compliance with commands and prohibitions. The premises were restricted to proximate because the transmitted also has some remote premises that are rational, as has passed; thus it does not contrast with the composite but rather falls under it—if by 'evidence' is meant the arranged premises themselves. But if what is meant is its source, like the universe for the Maker, or the Book, Sunna, and consensus for rulings, then the composite has no meaning.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage defines and clarifies the division of evidence (dalīl) into rational and transmitted. The author begins by stating that evidence may be divided into rational and transmitted, or into these two plus the composite of both. He then addresses a potential misunderstanding: that 'transmitted' means none of its premises are rational. He shows this is false because if we trace back the chain of reporters, it must ultimately end with someone known to be truthful by reason, otherwise circularity or infinite regress occurs. He explains that those who divide into two categories mean by 'transmitted' an argument whose some premises (proximate or remote) depend on transmission from the truthful one, and by 'rational' that which does not. Those who divide into three mean by 'transmitted' an argument whose all proximate premises are transmitted, and by 'composite' one whose some proximate premises are rational and some transmitted. He then explains that the restriction to proximate premises is important because transmitted arguments also have remote rational premises (as shown earlier), so transmitted arguments are not wholly separate from composite ones; rather, they fall under the composite if we consider the premises themselves. However, if we consider the source (maʾkhadh) of the evidence—such as the universe for proving the Maker, or the Book, Sunna, and consensus for rulings—then the composite category has no meaning because the source itself is either rational or transmitted.

Key Terms
العقلي
النقلي
المركب
الدليل
المقدمات القريبة
المقدمات البعيدة
المأخذ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That transmitted evidence never contains any rational premises.
Correction: The chain of reporters must end with someone known by reason to be truthful, otherwise circularity or infinite regress follows. Thus transmitted evidence always depends on rational premises.

Checkpoint
Why is the impression that 'transmitted' means none of its premises are rational false?
A) Because transmitted evidence depends solely on revelation without reason.
B) Because the chain of reporters must end with someone known by reason to be truthful, otherwise circularity or infinite regress occurs.
C) Because all transmitted premises are actually rational.
D) Because the composite category is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Transmitted evidence always has a rational foundation, either in its remote premises or in the chain of transmission itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Taftazani argues that even transmitted knowledge rests on a rational foundation. Consider a belief you hold solely because it was taught to you by a trusted authority. Are you willing to examine its rational premises, even if that feels uncomfortable? What would it mean for you to truly make your faith rationally grounded?
?
The author warns that without rational grounding, the chain of transmission leads to circularity or infinite regress. Have you ever accepted a religious claim simply because it is widely repeated or backed by scholars, without checking the underlying reasoning? How might this lesson shift your responsibility as a seeker of truth?
?
This lesson distinguishes between the premises of an argument and its source. In your daily life, do you confuse the authority of a speaker with the soundness of their argument? What practical step can you take this week to ensure your convictions are built on reason, not just the weight of tradition?

Why Kalam Focuses on Existence: Answering Objections
Objection-and-answer defending kalam's focus on existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam's focus on existence is defended when it appears to discuss non-existence and divine attributes like necessity and pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Kalam's investigation of non-existence is always accidental, never essential.

This passage addresses a common criticism: how can kalam claim to study existence when it often discusses non-existence and impossibility, as well as attributes exclusive to God like necessity and pre-eternity? The author explains that these topics are only incidental (bil-ʿaraḍ) because they relate to existence as counterparts or as universal categories. The lesson unpacks this defense and clarifies the scope of kalam's subject matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening objection that criticizes kalam for discussing non-existence and attributes exclusive to God. Note the specific complaints.
2
Understand the first answer
Focus on how non-existence and impossibility are discussed accidentally (bil-ʿaraḍ) as counterparts to existence and possibility.
This is a key principle: the investigation is confined to the states of the existent.
3
Understand the second answer
See how necessity and pre-eternity are discussed as divisions of absolute necessity and pre-eternity, making them universal matters, not exclusive to God.
Pre-eternity is considered from the aspect of negation (nafy) to avoid being a universal matter.
4
Reflect on universal matters and the reference to al-Mawāqif
Consider how the category of universal matters (al-umūr al-ʿāmmah) is broadened to include non-existence, and why the author of al-Mawāqif should have adopted this view.
This connects the discussion to debates about the subject matter of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قد يبحث عما لا يشمل الموجود أصلاً كالامتناع والعدم وعما يخص الواجب قطعاً كالوجوب والقدم، قلنا : لما كان البحث مقصوراً على أحوال الموجود، كان بحث العدم والامتناع بالعرض لكونهما في مقابلة الوجود والإمكان وبحث الوجوب والقدم، من جهة كونهما من أقسام مطلق الوجوب والقدم، أعني ضرورة الوجود بالذات أو بالغير وعدم المسبوقية بالعدم وهما من الأمور الشاملة، أما الوجوب فظاهر، وأما القدم فعلى رأي الفلاسفة، حيث يقولون بقدم المجردات والحركة والزمان وغيرهم من الجواهر والأعراض، ونظر الكلام فيه من جهة النفي لا الإثبات يعني أنه ليس من الأمور العامة، كبحث الحال عند من ينفيه وقد تفسر الأمور العامة بما يعم أكثر الموجودات أو المعدومات، ليشمل العدم والامتناع وإلى هذا كان ينبغي أن يذهب صاحب المواقف حيث زعم أن ليس موضوع الكلام هو الموجود لما أنه يبحث عن المعدوم.
If it is said: '[Kalam] sometimes investigates what does not encompass the existent at all, like impossibility and non-existence, and what pertains exclusively to the Necessary [Existent] such as necessity and pre-eternity.' We say: 'Since the investigation is confined to the states of the existent, the investigation of non-existence and impossibility is accidental, because they are the counterparts of existence and possibility; and the investigation of necessity and pre-eternity is from the aspect that they are divisions of absolute necessity and pre-eternity, meaning the necessity of existence by essence or by another and not being preceded by non-existence, and these two are among universal matters. As for necessity, it is obvious; as for pre-eternity, it is according to the opinion of the philosophers, where they affirm the pre-eternity of incorporeal beings, motion, time, and other substances and accidents. The consideration of it is from the aspect of negation, not affirmation, meaning that it is not among the universal matters, like the investigation of the state (al-ḥāl) for those who deny it. And the universal matters are interpreted to include most existents or non-existents, so as to include non-existence and impossibility. It is towards this that the author of al-Mawāqif should have inclined, where he claimed that the subject matter of kalām is not the existent, because it investigates the non-existent.'
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the scope of kalām's subject matter. The objector argues that kalām sometimes discusses what does not include the existent at all, such as impossibility and absolute non-existence, and also discusses attributes that exclusively apply to God, like necessity and pre-eternity. The answer explains that since the investigation is limited to the states of the existent, the discussion of non-existence and impossibility is only incidental (bil-ʿaraḍ) because they are the opposites of existence and possibility. Regarding necessity and pre-eternity, they are examined insofar as they are divisions of absolute necessity and pre-eternity—i.e., necessity of existence by essence or by another, and not being preceded by non-existence—which are universal attributes applicable also to contingent beings. Necessity is clearly general, while pre-eternity, according to the philosophers, applies to incorporeal beings, motion, time, etc. However, that discussion is from the perspective of negation (nafy), not affirmation, meaning pre-eternity is not a universal matter in the full sense, similar to the discussion of 'state' (ḥāl) by those who deny it. The passage concludes by noting that the universal matters (al-umūr al-ʿāmmah) are sometimes defined to cover most existents or non-existents, thus including non-existence and impossibility; and this interpretation should have been adopted by the author of al-Mawāqif, who claimed that the subject of kalām is not the existent because it investigates the non-existent.

Key Terms
الموجود
العدم
الامتناع
الوجوب
القدم
الأمور العامة
بالعرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think kalām deals directly with non-existence as a primary subject.
Correction: The passage clarifies that discussion of non-existence is only accidental (bil-ʿaraḍ) because it is the counterpart of existence.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does kalām investigate non-existence and impossibility?
A) Because they are essential to the subject of kalām.
B) Because they are accidental (bil-ʿaraḍ) as counterparts of existence and possibility.
C) Because kalām's subject is both existent and non-existent equally.
D) Because the philosophers made them central.

Key Takeaway
Kalam's subject is the existent, and any discussion of non-existence is only accidental and secondary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your faith, do you spend more energy dwelling on what is missing in your life (non-existence) than on the reality of your Creator (existence)? How would shifting that focus change your certainty today?
