This passage shows that even the discussion of non-existence is only accidental—it exists only in relation to existence. What 'non-existences' (fears, lacks, doubts) are you treating as primary that should only be secondary to the existence of Allah? Will you consciously re-center your mind on the existent?
?
The author argues that investigating necessity and pre-eternity is from a universal aspect—they apply to both God and creation. Does your understanding of God's attributes (like necessity, pre-eternity) remain abstract, or do you reflect on how they manifest in your own contingency? If your knowledge does not humble you, what is your knowledge for?

The Subject of Kalam Covers Non-Existents
Discusses whether the subject of kalam includes non-existents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the subject of kalam includes non-existents from the aspect of negation, and how this relates to the definition of 'universal matters'.

Core Idea
Kalam investigates both existents and non-existents through negation.

This passage clarifies a subtle but important point: the science of kalam does not limit itself to affirming things about existents. Instead, it often approaches matters from the side of negation, which allows it to examine non-existents and impossibilities. We will see how this connects to the broader concept of 'universal matters'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key contrast between 'negation' and 'affirmation'.
The passage is short yet dense. Pause after each phrase.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for the terms 'النفي' (negation), 'الإثبات' (affirmation), and 'الأمور العامة' (universal matters). Understand their role in defining kalam's subject.
These terms are foundational for the entire discipline.
3
Understand the negation vs. affirmation distinction
The passage explains that kalam deals with things from the aspect of negation, not affirmation. This means it investigates what is not the case, which includes non-existents.
Contrast this with other sciences that affirm properties.
4
Reflect on the inclusion of non-existents
Consider why the author of al-Mawaqif should have agreed that kalam's subject includes non-existents. How does this affect the scope of kalam?
Think about examples like investigating the impossibility of multiple gods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام فيه من جهة النفي لا الإثبات يعني أنه ليس من الأمور العامة، كبحث الحال عند من ينفيه وقد تفسر الأمور العامة بما يعم أكثر الموجودات أو المعدومات، ليشمل العدم والامتناع وإلى هذا كان ينبغي أن يذهب صاحب المواقف حيث زعم أن ليس موضوع الكلام هو الموجود لما أنه يبحث عن المعدوم .
Speech about it is from the aspect of negation not affirmation, meaning that it is not among universal matters, like the investigation of the state (al-hal) according to those who negate it. And 'universal matters' have been explained as what encompasses most existents or non-existents, to include non-existence and impossibility. And to this the author of 'al-Mawaqif' should have inclined when he claimed that the subject of kalam is not the existent because it investigates the non-existent.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of kalam's subject matter. It clarifies that kalam deals with things from the perspective of negation rather than affirmation, meaning it considers what is not the case. This is in contrast to other sciences that affirm properties. The text then explains that some interpret 'universal matters' as covering both existents and non-existents, thus including non-existence and impossibility. The author points out that al-Taftazani (Sahib al-Mawaqif) should have adopted this view, since he himself argued that the subject of kalam is not limited to existence because it investigates non-existents.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think kalam only deals with existents and affirms things about them.
Correction: Kalam investigates from the aspect of negation, which includes non-existents and impossibilities.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the subject of kalam from the aspect of?
A) Affirmation only
B) Negation not affirmation
C) Universal matters only
D) Existence only

The Self-Evidence of a Proposition and Its Parts
Defends a position on self-evidence and refutes an objection about circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how self-evidence of a composite proposition relates to the self-evidence of its parts without circularity.

Core Idea
The whole's self-evidence reveals parts' self-evidence without circular reasoning.

This passage tackles a subtle point in Islamic logic: if a proposition is self-evident, does that require each of its parts to be individually known as self-evident? The author defends a nuanced view, showing that knowledge of the whole's self-evidence can ground knowledge of the parts' self-evidence without falling into circularity. He also refutes objections from al-Mawaqif about conceiving composites without detailed analysis.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
The relationship between the self-evidence of a whole proposition and its parts is at stake. The author argues that knowledge of the whole is either identical to knowledge of the parts or obtained through them.
This sets up the priority of part knowledge.
2
Follow the core claim
The author states that knowledge of each part is prior to knowledge of the whole, not subordinate. Then he refutes al-Mawaqif's claim that there is no circularity because the self-evidence of the whole depends on parts, but knowledge of whole's self-evidence does not depend on knowledge of parts' self-evidence.
The distinction between self-evidence and knowledge of self-evidence is key.
3
Examine the objection
An objection is raised: a composite may be conceived without detailed observation of its parts. The author responds with three 'if granted' (law sallama) arguments.
The responses cover real vs. accidental composites and the nature of tasdiq.
4
Analyze the three responses
First response: The objection only applies to real composites, not accidental ones. Second: Even for conceptualization, affirming the whole's self-evidence implies each part is self-evident. Third: Even then, no circularity because the proof may be known absolutely without depending on the claim.
Each response strengthens the author's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التصديق جزء من بداهة هذا التصديق، لأنه لا معنى لبداهة هذا التصديق سوى أن ما يتضمنه من الحكم والطرفين بديهي والعلم بالكل : إما نفس العلم بالأجزاء أو حاصل به على ما مرّ في تصور الماهية وأجزائها، فبالضرورة يكون العلم بكل جزء سابقاً على العلم بالكل لا تابعاً له ممكن الاستفادة منه، ونبطل ما ذكر في المواقف من أنا نختار أن هذا التصديق بديهي مطلقاً، أي: بجميع أجزائه، ولا مصادرة لأن بداهة هذا التصديق تتوقف على بداهة أجزائه، لكن العلم ببداهته لا يتوقف على العلم ببداهة الأجزاء. فالاستدلال : إنما هو على العلم ببداهة الأجزاء فيجوز أن يستفاد من العلم ببداهة هذا التصديق لأنه يستتبع العلم ببداهة أجزائه بمعنى أنه إذا علم بداهته فكل جزء يلاحظ من أجزائه يعلم أنه بديهي فإن قيل : قد يعقل المركب من غير ملاحظة الأجزاء على التفصيل . قلنا : لو سلم ففي المركب الحقيقي إذ لا معنى لتعقل المركب الاعتباري سوى تعقل الأمور الاعتبارية المتعددة، التي وضع الاسم بإزائها، ولو سلم ففي التصور للقطع بأنه لا معنى للتصديق ببداهة هذا المركب بجميع أجزائه سوى التصديق بأن هذا الجزء بديهي، وذاك وذاك ولو سلم فلا يلزم المصادرة في شيء من الصور لجواز أن يعلم الدليل مطلقاً من غير توقف على العلم بجزئه الذي هو نفس المذعي
The affirmation (tasdiq) is part of the self-evidence of this affirmation, because there is no meaning to the self-evidence of this affirmation except that what it includes of the judgment and the two extremes is self-evident (badihi). And knowledge of the whole is either the very knowledge of the parts or attained through it, as has passed concerning the conceptualization (tasawwur) of the essence (mahiyya) and its parts. So necessarily, knowledge of each part is prior to knowledge of the whole, not subordinate to it, possible to be benefited from it. And we refute what was mentioned in al-Mawaqif that we choose that this affirmation is entirely self-evident, i.e., with all its parts, and no circularity (musadara) because the self-evidence of this affirmation depends on the self-evidence of its parts, but knowledge of its self-evidence does not depend on knowledge of the self-evidence of the parts. So the inference (istidlal) is only about knowledge of the self-evidence of the parts, so it is permissible that one benefits from knowledge of the self-evidence of this affirmation because it entails knowledge of the self-evidence of its parts, meaning that if its self-evidence is known, then every part observed from its parts is known to be self-evident. If it is said: The composite (murakkab) may be conceived without detailed observation of the parts. We say: If granted, then in the case of a real composite (murakkab haqiqi), since there is no meaning to conceiving an accidental composite (murakkab i'tibari) except conceiving multiple accidental matters (umur i'tibariyya) for which the name was coined. And if granted, then in conceptualization (tasawwur), it is certain that there is no meaning to affirming the self-evidence of this composite with all its parts except affirming that this part is self-evident, and that, and that. And if granted, then no circularity (musadara) occurs in any of the cases because it is permissible that the proof (dalil) is known absolutely without depending on knowledge of that part which is the very claim.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is part of a proof and objection-and-answer about self-evidence. The author begins by stating that the affirmation (tasdiq) under discussion is part of its own self-evidence. He clarifies that self-evidence of a proposition means that the judgment and its two terms are self-evident (badihi). Knowledge of the whole is either identical to knowledge of the parts or attained through it, as established earlier for conceptualization of essence and its parts. Consequently, knowledge of each part must precede knowledge of the whole, not follow from it. The author then refutes a claim from al-Mawaqif that this proposition is entirely self-evident with all its parts, and that there is no circularity (musadara). The author explains that the self-evidence of the whole depends on the self-evidence of its parts, but knowledge of that self-evidence does not depend on knowledge of the self-evidence of the parts. The inference (istidlal) is about knowledge of the self-evidence of the parts, which may be derived from knowledge of the self-evidence of the whole, because knowing the whole as self-evident entails that each part, when observed, is also known to be self-evident. Then an objection is raised: one may conceive a composite without detailed observation of its parts. The author responds: if granted, this applies only to a real composite (murakkab haqiqi), since an accidental composite (murakkab i'tibari) is nothing but the conception of multiple accidental matters. Moreover, even if granted for conceptualization, there is no sense in affirming the self-evidence of a composite with all its parts except as an affirmation that each part is self-evident. Finally, even if all that is granted, no circularity follows because the proof (dalil) may be known absolutely without depending on knowledge of the part that is the very claim (al-mudda'a).

Key Terms
تصديق
بداهة
بديهي
مركب حقيقي
مركب اعتباري
مصادرة
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The self-evidence of a composite proposition requires prior knowledge of the self-evidence of each part individually.
Correction: The author argues that knowing the whole is self-evident entails that each part, when observed, is known to be self-evident, so no prior knowledge is needed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does knowledge of the self-evidence of the whole not depend on knowledge of the self-evidence of its parts?
A) Because the whole is known independently of its parts.
B) Because the self-evidence of the whole entails that each part, when observed, is known to be self-evident.
C) Because the parts are not necessary for the whole's self-evidence.
D) Because the whole and parts are identical in self-evidence.

Key Takeaway
Self-evidence of a proposition does not require prior separate knowledge of each part's self-evidence; rather, the whole's self-evidence implies the parts' self-evidence when considered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson shows that the whole can be self-evident and then lead you to see the self-evidence of its parts. How does this change how you approach learning and trusting complex theological truths? Will you now allow yourself to start from a self-evident whole and then examine its parts, trusting that the knowledge of the parts will follow?
?
You likely have certain beliefs that you consider 'self-evident'—like the existence of God or the truth of revelation. But have you ever stopped to check whether your belief in these wholes depends on a prior detailed understanding of every part? This passage warns against demanding that kind of prior knowledge before affirming the whole. Is there a belief you have been hesitant to embrace because you felt you needed to master all its parts first? What step will you take today to accept the whole and then grow in understanding of its parts?
?
The argument in this lesson refutes the idea that affirming the whole's self-evidence leads to circular reasoning. In your own spiritual life, have you ever worried that your faith is circular—that you believe because you believe? This lesson teaches that knowledge of the whole's self-evidence comes first, and then the parts become clear. Can you identify one area where you have been stuck in a loop of doubt, demanding proof of parts before accepting the whole? How will you break that cycle by starting with the self-evident whole and trusting that the parts will follow?

Answer to Objection on Defining Existence
Answers a previous objection and gives two arguments for impossibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments against defining existence by descriptive definition (rasm) and the author's reply denying their premises.

Core Idea
Defining existence by rasm leads to circularity or relies on what is already known.

In this passage, the author addresses a previous objection that existence cannot be defined by descriptive definition (rasm). He first recalls an earlier answer, then presents two new arguments for impossibility, and finally offers a concise rebuttal. This lesson unpacks each step of this intricate theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recall the prior answer
Read the first sentence where the author restates that definition depends on specification, not knowledge of specification. This reframes the objection.
Note the shift from knowledge to the thing itself.
2
Understand the first argument
The first argument claims circularity: defining existence requires prior knowledge of a necessary concomitant, which is more specific than existence itself.
3
Understand the second argument
The second argument says definition must use something better known, and nothing is better known than existence—by induction or universality.
Existence is the most universal in actualization.
4
Examine the author's answer
The author denies most premises. Even if existence were the most known, it would not need the other premises for definition.
This is a simple but powerful rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عما ذكر في امتناع اكتسابه بالرسم ما سبق من أنه إنما يتوقف على الاختصاص لا على العلم بالاختصاص وأنه وإن لم يستلزم إفادة معرفة الحقيقة، لكنه قد يفيدها، وقد يستدل على امتناع اكتسابه بالرسم بوجهين. أحدهما: أنه يتوقف على العلم بوجود اللازم وثبوته للمرسوم، وهو أخص من مطلق الوجود، فيدور. وثانيهما: أن الرسم إنما يكون بالأعرف ولا أعرف من الوجود بحكم الاستقراء، أو لأنه أعم الأشياء بحسب التحقق دون الصدق، والأعم أعرف لكون شروطه ومعانداته أقل. والجواب: منع أكثر المقدمات على أنه لو ثبت كونه أعرف الأشياء لم يحتج إلى باقي المقدمات.
And the answer to what was mentioned regarding the impossibility of acquiring it by definition (ar-rasm) is what preceded: that it depends on specification (al-ikhtiṣāṣ) and not on knowledge of specification, and that even if it does not entail imparting knowledge of the essence (al-ḥaqīqa), it may nevertheless impart it. And one may argue for the impossibility of acquiring it by definition in two ways: First: it depends on knowledge of the existence of the necessary concomitant (al-lāzim) and its being established for the defined (al-marsūm), which is more specific than absolute existence, so a circle results. Second: definition (ar-rasm) is only by what is more known (al-aʿraf), and nothing is more known than existence by inductive investigation (al-istiqrāʾ), or because it is the most universal of things in terms of actualization (al-taḥaqquq) without truth-value (aṣ-ṣidq), and the more universal is more known because its conditions and opposites are fewer. And the answer: denial of most of the premises, for if it were established that it is the most known of things, it would not need the remaining premises.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit regarding the impossibility of acquiring the concept of existence by definition (ar-rasm). The author first restates a previous answer: definition of existence depends on specification of the thing, not on knowledge of that specification, and even if definition does not necessarily convey knowledge of the essence, it might still do so. Then he presents two arguments for the impossibility of defining existence. The first argues that definition requires prior knowledge of a necessary concomitant (al-lāzim) being true of the defined (al-marsūm), which is more specific than absolute existence, leading to circularity. The second argument claims that definition must be by something better known, and nothing is better known than existence, as shown by induction, or because existence is the most universal in terms of actualization (without truth-conditional specificity), and the more universal is better known because it has fewer conditions and opposites. The author's answer is to deny most of these premises, arguing that even if existence were the most known, it would not need the other premises to define it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that a definition must always give full knowledge of the essence.
Correction: The author clarifies that even a descriptive definition (rasm) can be useful without conveying full essence.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument against defining existence by رسم, why does it lead to circularity?
A) Because it requires knowledge of the necessary concomitant, which is more specific than absolute existence.
B) Because existence is self-evident and cannot be defined.
C) Because definition must be by what is better known, but nothing is better known than existence.
D) Because definition depends on specification, not on knowledge of specification.

Key Takeaway
Defining existence by rasm faces objections of circularity and the need for something better known, but the author denies the premises.

?

Existence as a Common Concept: The Proofs
Al-Taftazani states the topic and gives two proofs for the commonality of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational proofs that existence is a common concept shared by all existents and how a potential objection is answered.

Core Idea
Existence is known as a single concept even when details are uncertain.

Al-Taftazani begins by stating the second topic of the chapter: existence is a single concept common to all existents and additional to essences. He then presents two concise proofs to establish this. This lesson unpacks those proofs and the response to a possible objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise style.
The passage includes footnote markers (1) and (2) which refer to editorial notes; focus on the main text.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the two proofs: (1) certainty of existence while doubting specificity, and (2) the valid division of existence into Necessary and contingent. Understand how each demonstrates commonality.
The second proof relies on the logical principle that division requires a common genus.
3
Understand the objection and reply
Note the objection concerning 'essence' (mahiyya) and 'individual' (shakhs). See how al-Taftazani replies that even their absolute sense is common.
The reply shows that the objection does not undermine the commonality.
4
Reflect on the point
Consider why this topic is foundational for kalam. If existence were not common, how could we speak of Allah's existence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
في أن الوجود مفهوم مشترك بين الموجودات
(قال): المبحث الثاني أن الوجود مفهوم واحد مشترك بين الموجودات وهي زائدة على الماهيات (1) ينبه على الأول الجزم بالوجود مع التردد في الخصوصية وصحة التقسيم إلى الواجب وغيره (2) مع قطع النظر عن الوضع واللغة، فإن نوقض بالماهية والشخص قلنا مطلقهما أيضاً مشترك وتمام الحصر في الموجود والمعدوم، والقطع باتحاد مفهوم العدم، ولو بمعنى رفع الحقيقة إذ لا تغاير إلا بالاضافة) .
المنقول عن الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري (3) أن وجود كل شيء غير ذاته (4) وليس للفظ الوجود مفهوم واحد مشترك بين الوجودات بل الاشتراك لفظي، والجمهور على أن له مفهوماً واحداً مشتركاً بين الوجودات، إلا أنه عند المتكلمين حقيقة واحدة تختلف بالقيود والإضافات، حتى إن وجود الواجب هو كونه في الأعيان على ما يعقل من كون الإنسان .
(1) زيادة في (أ) ولم يذكر في (ب). (2) وهو الوجود الممكن وتقسم الشيء إلى قسمين أو أقسام يدل على اشتراك القسمين أو الأقسام في المقسم لصدقه على كل وإلا كان بمثابة أن يقال مثلاً: الإنسان إما حجر وإما شجر. (3) هو علي بن إسماعيل بن إسحاق أبو الحسن من نسل الصحابي أبي موسى الأشعري : مؤسس مذهب الأشاعرة. كان الأئمة المتكلمين المجتهدين. ولد بالبصرة وتلقى مذهب المعتزلة من وتقدم فيهم، ثم رجع وجاهر بخلافهم، وتوفي ببغداد عام ٣٢٤هـ. من كتبه الرد على المجسمة، ومقالات الإسلاميين ،والإبانة ومقالات الملحدين والرد على ابن الرواندي وغير ذلك، رحمه الله
The second topic: That existence is a common concept among existents. (He said): The second topic is that existence is a single concept common among existents, and it is additional to essences (mahiyat). It alerts to the first: certainty of existence with doubt about specificity, and the validity of division into the Necessary and other than it, disregarding conventional and linguistic considerations. For if it is contested by the essence and the individual, we say that their absolute (mawdu') is also common, and the complete enumeration is in existence and nonexistence, and the certainty of the unity of the concept of non-existence, even if by meaning of removal of reality, for there is no difference except by relation. What is transmitted from Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari is that the existence of everything is other than its essence, and the term existence does not have a single common concept among existences, rather the sharing is verbal (ishtirak lafzi). The majority are of the opinion that it has a single common concept among existences, except that according to the mutakallimun it is one reality differing by restrictions and relations, such that the existence of the Necessary is its being in external entities according to what is understood from the existence of the human. (1) An addition in (A) not mentioned in (B). (2) It is possible existence, and dividing a thing into two or more parts indicates the sharing of the two parts or parts in the divided, because it is applicable to each; otherwise it would be like saying, for example: man is either stone or tree. (3) He is 'Ali ibn Isma'il ibn Ishaq Abu al-Hasan, from the progeny of the Companion Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, founder of the Ash'ari school. He was one of the leading theological jurists. He was born in Basra, learned the Mu'tazilite school and advanced in it, then recanted and openly opposed them, and died in Baghdad in 324 AH. His works include: The Refutation of the Anthropomorphists, The Doctrines of the Muslims, The Clarification, The Doctrines of the Heretics, and The Refutation of Ibn al-Rawandi, and others. May God have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage introduces the second topic of the chapter: that existence (al-wujud) is a common concept among existents. The author first states the topic, then mentions two proofs in summary: first, we can be certain of existence while doubting its specific nature, showing that existence is a common element; second, the division of existence into necessary and possible presupposes a common genus. He notes that these proofs disregard conventional and linguistic considerations. He then addresses a potential objection regarding the essence (mahiyya) and the individual (shakhs), replying that even their absolute sense is common. He then cites the view of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, who held that existence is not a single common concept but a verbal equivocation (ishtirak lafzi). The majority, however, hold that it is a single common concept, though the mutakallimun consider it to be one reality that differs by restrictions (quyud) and relations (idafat). The passage ends with a biographical note on al-Ash'ari, providing his lineage, his shift from Mu'tazilism, his positions, and his works.

Key Terms
الوجود
الماهية
الواجب
الممكن
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because existents are different, existence must be different for each.
Correction: The proofs show that existence is a single common concept despite differences in specific natures.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that existence is a common concept?
A) We can be certain of existence while doubting its specific nature.
B) Existence is divided into necessary and possible.
C) The concept of non-existence is one.
D) All existents share the same essence.

Key Takeaway
Existence is a common concept proven by certainty despite ignorance of specifics and by the division into necessary and contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are certain that you exist, but are you equally certain that your existence comes from the One whose existence is necessary? If not, what doubt remains, and what does that doubt reveal about your heart?
?
If existence is a single common concept shared by you and your Creator, how should that change the way you relate to your own existence? Are you living as if your existence is your own, or as a gift from the One who alone is necessary?
?
The division of existence into necessary and contingent places you squarely in the contingent category. Does your daily life reflect a reliance on the Necessary Being, or do you act as though you are self-sufficient? What would change if you truly internalized that you are contingent at every moment?

Al-Ash'ari vs. the Majority on the Unity of Existence
Al-Taftazani cites al-Ash'ari's view and contrasts it with the majority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement between Al-Ash'ari and the majority of theologians on whether 'existence' is a univocal or equivocal term.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari said existence is merely a shared word; the majority said it is one concept.

After establishing the commonality of existence, al-Taftazani presents the view of al-Ash'ari who denied that 'existence' has a single common meaning, considering it a verbal equivocation. He then states the majority position that it is indeed one concept, but with differences due to restrictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the contrast between al-Ash'ari's view and the majority's.
The passage contains a footnote marker (3) referring to a biography of al-Ash'ari, but focus on the main argument.
2
Identify the two positions
Al-Ash'ari held that existence is 'ishtirak lafzi' (verbal equivocation). The majority held it is a single common concept. Find the exact wording.
3
Understand the majority's nuance
The majority, especially the mutakallimun, said existence is one reality but differs by restrictions and relations. Example: existence of the Necessary is the same concept as existence of a human, but with different restrictions.
This nuance avoids pure univocity while maintaining commonality.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why does this matter for theology? If existence were merely equivocal, how could we apply the same term to Allah and creatures? The majority preserves theological discourse.

المبحث الثاني
في أن الوجود مفهوم مشترك بين الموجودات
(قال): المبحث الثاني أن الوجود مفهوم واحد مشترك بين الموجودات وهي زائدة على الماهيات (1) ينبه على الأول الجزم بالوجود مع التردد في الخصوصية وصحة التقسيم إلى الواجب وغيره (2) مع قطع النظر عن الوضع واللغة، فإن نوقض بالماهية والشخص قلنا مطلقهما أيضاً مشترك وتمام الحصر في الموجود والمعدوم، والقطع باتحاد مفهوم العدم، ولو بمعنى رفع الحقيقة إذ لا تغاير إلا بالاضافة) .
المنقول عن الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري (3) أن وجود كل شيء غير ذاته (4) وليس للفظ الوجود مفهوم واحد مشترك بين الوجودات بل الاشتراك لفظي، والجمهور على أن له مفهوماً واحداً مشتركاً بين الوجودات، إلا أنه عند المتكلمين حقيقة واحدة تختلف بالقيود والإضافات، حتى إن وجود الواجب هو كونه في الأعيان على ما يعقل من كون الإنسان .
(1) زيادة في (أ) ولم يذكر في (ب). (2) وهو الوجود الممكن وتقسم الشيء إلى قسمين أو أقسام يدل على اشتراك القسمين أو الأقسام في المقسم لصدقه على كل وإلا كان بمثابة أن يقال مثلاً: الإنسان إما حجر وإما شجر. (3) هو علي بن إسماعيل بن إسحاق أبو الحسن من نسل الصحابي أبي موسى الأشعري : مؤسس مذهب الأشاعرة. كان الأئمة المتكلمين المجتهدين. ولد بالبصرة وتلقى مذهب المعتزلة من وتقدم فيهم، ثم رجع وجاهر بخلافهم، وتوفي ببغداد عام ٣٢٤هـ. من كتبه الرد على المجسمة، ومقالات الإسلاميين ،والإبانة ومقالات الملحدين والرد على ابن الرواندي وغير ذلك، رحمه الله
The second topic: That existence is a common concept among existents. (He said): The second topic is that existence is a single concept common among existents, and it is additional to essences (mahiyat). It alerts to the first: certainty of existence with doubt about specificity, and the validity of division into the Necessary and other than it, disregarding conventional and linguistic considerations. For if it is contested by the essence and the individual, we say that their absolute (mawdu') is also common, and the complete enumeration is in existence and nonexistence, and the certainty of the unity of the concept of non-existence, even if by meaning of removal of reality, for there is no difference except by relation. What is transmitted from Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari is that the existence of everything is other than its essence, and the term existence does not have a single common concept among existences, rather the sharing is verbal (ishtirak lafzi). The majority are of the opinion that it has a single common concept among existences, except that according to the mutakallimun it is one reality differing by restrictions and relations, such that the existence of the Necessary is its being in external entities according to what is understood from the existence of the human. (1) An addition in (A) not mentioned in (B). (2) It is possible existence, and dividing a thing into two or more parts indicates the sharing of the two parts or parts in the divided, because it is applicable to each; otherwise it would be like saying, for example: man is either stone or tree. (3) He is 'Ali ibn Isma'il ibn Ishaq Abu al-Hasan, from the progeny of the Companion Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, founder of the Ash'ari school. He was one of the leading theological jurists. He was born in Basra, learned the Mu'tazilite school and advanced in it, then recanted and openly opposed them, and died in Baghdad in 324 AH. His works include: The Refutation of the Anthropomorphists, The Doctrines of the Muslims, The Clarification, The Doctrines of the Heretics, and The Refutation of Ibn al-Rawandi, and others. May God have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage introduces the second topic of the chapter: that existence (al-wujud) is a common concept among existents. The author first states the topic, then mentions two proofs in summary: first, we can be certain of existence while doubting its specific nature, showing that existence is a common element; second, the division of existence into necessary and possible presupposes a common genus. He notes that these proofs disregard conventional and linguistic considerations. He then addresses a potential objection regarding the essence (mahiyya) and the individual (shakhs), replying that even their absolute sense is common. He then cites the view of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, who held that existence is not a single common concept but a verbal equivocation (ishtirak lafzi). The majority, however, hold that it is a single common concept, though the mutakallimun consider it to be one reality that differs by restrictions (quyud) and relations (idafat). The passage ends with a biographical note on al-Ash'ari, providing his lineage, his shift from Mu'tazilism, his positions, and his works.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that Ash'ari denied the commonality of existence entirely.
Correction: He affirmed equivocal sharing (ishtirak lafzi) rather than univocal, meaning the word is shared but not the concept.

Key Takeaway

?

How should this affect your daily awareness of Allah's presence? What would change if you truly lived knowing that His existence is utterly unique and beyond your measure?

The Verbal Dispute Over Existence
Taftazani analyzes the existence quiddity debate as a verbal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani resolves the debate on existence as a verbal dispute by distinguishing mental addition from real addition.

Core Idea
The existence-quiddity debate dissolves once you see both sides accept mental distinction.

How can two scholarly camps argue for centuries about whether existence is additional to essence? Taftazani steps in to show that the argument is largely verbal: both sides actually agree that the mind can separate existence from quiddity, but they differ on whether that separation reflects a real distinction in external things. This passage clarifies the precise point of agreement and the source of the confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the reply
Begin by reading the opening objection (if you say...) and Taftazani's reply (I say...). Notice how he frames the issue as a question about the meaning of 'existence'.
The hypothetical objector is challenging the very possibility of a real dispute.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage contrasts the majority (theologians) and the Shaykh (Ibn Sina). Find what each side's proof actually shows according to Taftazani.
The majority focus on concept distinction; Ibn Sina focuses on reality distinction.
3
Understand the resolution
Taftazani says there is agreement on mental addition (zā'id dhihan) and disagreement only on real addition (zā'id 'aynan). Once this is clarified, the dispute becomes verbal.
This is the key insight: both sides accept one form of addition.
4
Reflect on the consequence
The passage ends by noting that mental existence only allows saying 'additional in the intellect', not a real separate thing. Consider why this is important for later discussions.
This prevents metaphysical errors about existence being a 'thing' alongside essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: فإن قلت لخفاء مثلاً أن ليس مفهوم الوجود مفهوم الإنشاء، وليس لفظ الوجود في اصطلاح جميع اللغات موضوعاً بالاشتراك لمعان لا تكاد تتناهى واحتجاج الفريقين يشهد بأن النزاع في الوجود بمعنى الكون، ولعلهم نائوا عن أن الوجود كما يطلق على الكون يطلق على الذات على أن مفهوم الذات أيضًا مشترك فما وجه هذا الاختلاف في العمل؟ قلت: مضمون أدلة الجمهور أن ليس مفهوم الموجود مفهوم الماهية المتصفة به، وأدلة الشيخ أن ليس لهما هويتان متمایزتان تقوم إحداهما بالأخرى كالجسم مع البياض، فلا خلاف في أن الوجود زائد ذهنا بمعنى أن للعقل أن يلاحظ الماهية دون الوجود وبالعكس و لا عيناً بأن يكون الماهية تحقق، ولعارضها المسمى بالوجود تحقق آخر، حتى يجتمعا اجتماع القابل والمقبول، كالجسم والبياض، فعند التحرير لا يبقى نزاع، ويظهر أن حصل الاشتراك لفظي مكابرة، ولا يتفرع على الوجود الذهني سوى أن للمثبت أن يقول زائد في العقل، وعلى النافي أن يقول عقلاً أو في التعقل وليس السلة نفي التغاير العقلي والاشتراك المعنوي كما في بدائره المفهومات الكلية سيا
He said: For if you say, for example, that the concept of existence is not the concept of creation, and the word 'existence' in the terminology of all languages is not used with univocal meaning for meanings that are almost infinite, and the argument of the two groups testifies that the dispute is about existence in the sense of 'being' (al-kawn), and perhaps they have denied that existence as applied to being is applied to essence, and that the concept of essence is also shared, so what is the basis of this difference in operation? I say: The import of the proofs of the majority is that the concept of 'existent' is not the concept of the quiddity qualified by it, and the proofs of the Shaykh [Ibn Sina] are that they do not have two distinct realities, one subsisting in the other like body with whiteness. So there is no disagreement that existence is additional mentally, meaning that the intellect can observe the quiddity without existence and vice versa, but not really, such that the quiddity has one realization and its attribute called existence has another realization, so that they come together as the receptive and the received, like body and whiteness. So upon analysis, no dispute remains, and it becomes apparent that a verbal equivocation occurred due to obstinacy. And nothing branches off from mental existence except that the proponent can say it is additional in the intellect, and the denier says intellectually or in intellection, and it is not a negation of mental distinction and univocal meaning, as in the principles of universal concepts, especially...
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a resolution of the debate about whether existence is additional to quiddity. The author introduces a hypothetical objection: if the concept of existence is not the concept of bringing into existence, and the word 'existence' is not univocal, then how can there be a real dispute? He then explains that the proofs of the majority (theologians) show that the concept of 'existent' is not identical to the quiddity, while the proofs of Ibn Sina (the philosopher) show that existence and quiddity are not two distinct realities subsisting in each other. The key is that both sides agree that existence is mentally distinct (zā'id dhihan), meaning the mind can separate the quiddity from existence, but they differ on whether it is really distinct (dhātan). Once this is clarified, the dispute becomes merely verbal, based on a misunderstanding of the term 'existence'. The author concludes that the only consequence of mental existence is that one can say it is additional in the intellect, but not that it is a separate thing.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume the dispute between theologians and philosophers about existence is a genuine disagreement about whether existence is a real property added to essence.
Correction: Taftazani shows both sides agree existence is mentally distinct (the mind can separate them) but differ only on whether that distinction corresponds to a real separation in extra-mental reality. Hence the dispute is verbal.

Checkpoint

B) That existence is mentally distinct from quiddity
C) That existence is identical to quiddity
D) That existence does not exist

Key Takeaway
The existence-quiddity debate is resolved by recognizing that both sides accept mental addition; only the question of real addition separates them, turning the dispute into a verbal one.

Topics Counted Among Secondary Intelligibles
The author lists matters counted among secondary intelligibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key topics that are counted among the secondary intelligibles in kalam, including the Necessary Being's attributes and revelatory actions.

Core Idea
Kalam examines the Necessary as source of contingent existence, with attributes and acts from revelation.

This lesson explores a foundational enumeration in kalam: the subjects considered 'secondary intelligibles'. The author concludes a list by specifying that among these are the Necessary Being's role as the source of contingent beings, His essential attributes, and His actions such as sending messengers and revealing books. This sets the stage for the entire discipline of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

The passage is a single sentence, so read it as a whole.
2

These are core topics in kalam.
3
Understand the context
Recognize that this list defines the scope of kalam's subject matter: it is not abstract metaphysics but engages with revelation.
The phrase 'as brought by the Shari'a' shows the integration of revealed data.
4
Reflect on the phrase 'secondary intelligibles'

This distinguishes kalam from pure philosophy.

The Four Types of Existence
Sanusi enumerates the four types of existence and explains their definitions and relationships.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of existence according to Sanusi: objective, mental, verbal, and written, and how they relate to each other.

Core Idea
Existence is not only physical—it has mental, verbal, and written levels.

Sanusi begins a foundational classification in Islamic theology: the division of existence into objective, mental, verbal, and written. This framework helps us understand how things exist in reality, in the mind, in speech, and in writing. By grasping this, you will see how later arguments about God and creation rely on clear distinctions between these levels.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi introduces the four types and their characteristics.
Pay attention to the order: ayni, dhihni, lafzi, khatti.
2
Identify the four types and their definitions
Find the definitions for each of the four existences. Note which are fundamental, non-fundamental, and metaphorical.
Objective existence is muta'asil (fundamental), mental is like a shadow, verbal and written are metaphorical.
3
Understand the indicational relationships
Read the part about each subsequent type indicating the preceding. Notice the distinction between rational and conventional indication.
The first two are rational; the last two are conventional with variability.
4
Consider the hierarchy
The passage mentions the highest level is objective existence. Think about why this hierarchy matters for theology.
Objective existence is the foundation for all other types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث: تقسيم الوجود عينياً وذهنياً ولفظياً وخطياً. والوجود يتناول عينياً، وذهنياً، ولفظياً، وخطياً. والأول : متأصل يكون الموجود به حقيقة الشيء . والثاني غير متأصل بمنزلة الظل من الجسم يكون الموجود به صورة الشيء. والأخيران مجازيان، يكون الموجود بهما اسم الشيء وصورة اسمه. ولكل لاحق دلالة على السابق، إلا أن الأولى عقلية لا يختلف فيها الطرفان، والأخريان وضعيتان يختلف في أولاهما الدال فقط . وفي ثانيهما الطرفان جميعاً على مراتب كثيرة أعلاها : الوجود الأعيان، وهو الوجود المتأصل المتفق عليه الذي به تحقق ذات الشيء وحقيقته بل نفس تحققها ثم الوجود في الأذهان وهو وجود غير متأصل بمنزلة الظل، يكون المتحقق به الصورة المطابقة للشيء بمعنى أنها لو تحقق في
The third section: The division of existence into 'ayni (objective), dhihni (mental), lafzi (verbal), and khatti (written). And existence comprises objective, mental, verbal, and written. The first is fundamental (muta'asil): by it the reality of a thing is existent. The second is non-fundamental, like a shadow of the body: by it the image of a thing is existent. The last two are metaphorical: by them the name of a thing and the image of its name are existent. And each subsequent one has indication upon the preceding, except that the first [two?] are rational: the two ends do not differ in them; the last two are conventional: in the first of them only the signifier differs, and in the second of them both ends [differ] upon many levels. The highest of them is al-wujud al-a'yan (objective existence), which is the fundamental existence agreed upon, by which the entity of a thing and its reality — indeed, its very realization — is realized. Then mental existence (al-wujud fi al-adhhan), which is a non-fundamental existence like a shadow, by which is realized the image matching the thing, meaning that if it were realized in
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a classification of existence into four types. The author begins by stating this division. The first type, 'ayni (objective, or external) existence, is fundamental (muta'asil) and is that by which the reality of a thing exists. The second type, dhihni (mental) existence, is non-fundamental, likened to the shadow of a body; by it the image (sura) of a thing exists in the mind. The third and fourth types, lafzi (verbal) and khatti (written), are metaphorical: by them the name of a thing and the image of its name exist, respectively. Each later type indicates or points to the earlier one, but with a difference: the first two (ayni and dhihni?) are rational — meaning the relation between the signifier and signified is rational and does not vary; the last two are conventional (wad'iyya), where in lafzi only the signifier (dal; i.e., the word) can vary, while in khatti both the signifier and signified can vary across many levels. The highest level is objective existence, which is the fundamental, agreed-upon existence that constitutes the reality of a thing itself. Then mental existence is like a shadow. The explanation of mental existence is cut off, but it describes it as a non-fundamental existence by which a matching image is realized.

Key Terms
وجود عيني
وجود ذهني
وجود لفظي
وجود خطي
متأصل
مجازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might conflate mental existence with objective existence, thinking they are the same or that mental existence is also fundamental.
Correction: This passage clearly distinguishes them: objective existence is fundamental (muta'asil) while mental existence is non-fundamental, like a shadow.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of existence is considered fundamental (muta'asil)?
A) Mental existence.
B) Verbal existence.
C) Objective existence.
D) Written existence.

Key Takeaway
The four types of existence form a hierarchy from fundamental objective existence to conventional written existence, with each level indicating the previous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How often do you let your speech become disconnected from what you truly know in your heart and what is actually true? ?
You treat your own thoughts as if they are the real thing, but this lesson says mental existence is non-fundamental, like a shadow. Where in your life have you mistaken a mental concept for objective truth? Will you commit to checking your assumptions against the actual reality of things this week?

The Four Types of Existence
The author lists the four types of existence and describes their ranks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the division of existence into external, mental, verbal, and written types, and how they differ in being original or metaphorical.

Core Idea
Existence is not only physical; it includes mental, verbal, and written realities.

This lesson introduces a foundational classification in Islamic theology. The author divides existence into four categories: external, mental, verbal, and written. Understanding this division is crucial for grasping how we talk about things that are not physically present, as it underlies discussions of divine attributes and the nature of language.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage in Arabic to get familiar with the original terms. Then read the translation to understand the meaning.
Pay attention to the terms 'متأصل' and 'مجازي'.
2
Identify the four types
List the four types: external (عيني), mental (ذهني), verbal (لفظي), and written (خطي). Note which are original and which are metaphorical.
External existence is called original; the last two are metaphorical.
3
Understand the rank order
The text mentions a hierarchy: external is highest, then mental, then verbal, then written. Reflect on why external is highest.
The mental existence is like a shadow; if it were external, it would be the thing itself.
4
Reflect on the tree analogy
Consider the example of a tree: if the mental image of a tree became embodied, it would be the actual tree. This illustrates that mental existence corresponds to external reality.

المبحث الثالث
تقسيم الوجود عينياً وذهنياً ولفظياً وخطياً
المبحث الثالث: الوجود يتناول عينياً، وذهنياً، ولفظياً، وخطياً. والأول : متأصل (١) يكون الموجود به حقيقة الشيء .
والثاني (٢) غير متأصل بمنزلة الظل من الجسم يكون الموجود به صورة الشيء. والأخيران مجازيان، يكون الموجود بهما اسم الشيء وصورة اسمه. ولكل لاحق دلالة على السابق، إلا أن الأولى عقلية لا يختلف فيها الطرفان، والأخريان وضعيتان يختلف في أولاهما الدال فقط .
وفي ثانيهما الطرفان جميعاً(۳) على مراتب كثيرة (٤) أعلاها : الوجود (ه) الأعيان، وهو الوجود المتأصل المتفق عليه الذي به تحقق ذات الشيء وحقيقته بل نفس تحققها ثم الوجود في الأذهان وهو وجود غير متأصل بمنزلة الظل (٦) ، يكون المتحقق به الصورة المطابقة للشيء بمعنى أنها لو تحقق في
للجسم (۱) في (ب) بكون. وقوله : متأصلاً لتقدمه في الثبات والتحقق والاتفاق عليه، وسمي عينياً لاقتضائه ظهور حقيقة صاحبه في العيان، ففهوم الإنسان مثلاً لما كان له وجود عيني تحققت له حقيقة خارجية عينية في أفراده بذلك الوجود . (۲) والثاني : الوجود الذهني غير ثابت في الحقائق العيانية التي هي الأصل في العلوم وفي
المدارك .
،
(۳) أي يصح اختلافهما معاً بل ويقع باعتبار أن المدلول الذي هو اللفظ الموضوع يجوز أن يختلف بكثرة الأوضاع، كما وضع لمعنى الأسد لفظ الأسد والهزبر مثلاً في لغة واحدة أو في لغتين، كما
وضع له الأسد في العربية وإزم» بكسر الهمزة وفتح الزاى ثم ميم ساكنة في البربر. (٤) في (أ) بزيادة لفظ (كثيرة) . (٥) في (ب) بزيادة لفظ (المشخص). (٦) في (ب) الشجر بدلاً من (الجسم).
٣٤٢
الخارج لكانت ذلك الشيء، كما أن الشجر لو تجسم لكان ذلك الشجر، ثم الوجود في العبارة ثم في الكتابة وهما من حيث الإضافة إلى ذات الشيء وحقيقته مجازيان، لأن الموجود من زيد في اللفظ صوت موضوع بإزائه، وفي الخط نقش موضوع بإزائه اللفظ الدال عليه، لا ذات زيد ولا صورته . نعم إذا أضيف إلى اللفظ الموضوع بإزائه أو النقش الموضوع بإزاء ذلك اللفظ كان وجوداً حقيقياً، (۱) من قبيل الوجود في الأعيان
The third section: The division of existence into external, mental, verbal, and written. The third section: Existence comprises external, mental, verbal, and written. The first is original (متأصل); by it the reality of the thing exists. The second is non-original, like the shadow of the body; by it the form of the thing exists. The last two are metaphorical; by them exist the name of the thing and the image of its name. And each subsequent one indicates the previous, except that the first indication is rational (عقلية) in which the two sides do not differ, while the last two are conventional (وضعيتان) in which only the indicator differs in the first of them, and in the second of them both sides differ. (They are) upon many ranks, the highest of which is the existence of external things, which is the original existence agreed upon, by which the essence of the thing and its reality, indeed the very realization of it, is actualized. Then existence in minds, which is a non-original existence like a shadow, by which is actualized the form that corresponds to the thing, meaning that if it were actualized in the external it would be that thing, just as if a tree were to become embodied it would be that tree. Then existence in utterance, then in writing; and these two, in terms of their relation to the essence of the thing and its reality, are metaphorical, because what exists of Zayd in utterance is a sound posited to correspond to him, and in writing is a shape posited to correspond to the utterance that indicates him, not Zayd's essence nor his form. Yes, if it is added to the utterance posited to correspond to it, or to the shape posited to correspond to that utterance, it becomes a real existence of the kind of existence in external things.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage defines the division of existence into four types: external (عيني), mental (ذهني), verbal (لفظي), and written (خطي). The author first states that existence includes these four categories. Then he explains each: the first (external) is original (متأصل) and through it the reality of the thing exists. The second (mental) is non-original, like a shadow, and through it the form of the thing exists. The last two (verbal and written) are metaphorical (مجازيان); through them the name and the image of the name exist. The author then introduces the idea that each subsequent type indicates the previous one. The indication from mental to external is rational (عقلية), where the indicator and indicated do not vary. The indications from verbal to mental and from written to verbal are conventional (وضعيتان); in the first of these (verbal to mental) only the indicator varies (different words for the same concept), and in the second (written to verbal) both the indicator and the indicated can vary (different scripts for the same word). Then he lists the ranks: the highest is external existence (existence of external things), which is the original and agreed-upon existence; then mental existence, which is like a shadow; then existence in utterance; then in writing. He explains that verbal and written existence are metaphorical when considered relative to the thing itself, but become real (حقيقية) when considered relative to the utterance or script that indicates them. The passage includes footnotes that provide variations in manuscripts and additional clarifications.

Key Terms

وجود ذهني
وجود لفظي
وجود خطي
متأصل
مجازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only physical external existence is real existence; mental images and words are not existence at all.
Correction: This passage shows that mental existence is a valid form of existence (non-original), and verbal/written are metaphorical existences that indicate the thing.

Checkpoint
According to the text, which type of existence is considered 'original' (متأصل)?
A) Mental existence
B) Verbal existence
C) External existence
D) Written existence

Key Takeaway
Existence is divided into four categories: external (original), mental (like a shadow), verbal (metaphorical), and written (metaphorical), each with its own rank and reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often say you 'believe' in something, but is that belief only mental or verbal? When was the last time you made a truth you hold in your mind actually visible in your external actions? This week, identify one belief you have not acted upon and take a concrete step to bring it into external existence.
?
Consider your daily speech: are your words mere verbal existence, or do they correspond to reality? Is there a gap between what you say and what is actually true in your external existence? Repent of any hypocrisy and commit to aligning your verbal existence with the truth.
?
The highest rank of existence is external. Your prayers, fasting, and charity are external acts—but are they truly real in the sense that they reflect the reality of your faith? If your faith were only mental, it would be a shadow. Ask yourself: is your Islam merely a mental image, or is it embodied in your external life? What is one thing you will change today to make your faith more externally real?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
8 min
Types of Indication: Rational and Conventional
The author describes the types of indication linking the four existences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of indication that link the four existences: rational indication from mental to external, and conventional indication from verbal to mental and written to verbal.

Core Idea
The relationship between existence types is governed by rational or conventional indication.

Having introduced the four existence types, the author now explains how they relate to each other. The indication from mental to external is rational and universal, while the indications from verbal to mental and written to verbal are conventional and subject to variation across languages and scripts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about indication
Focus on the sentence that begins 'ولكل لاحق دلالة على السابق'. This explains how each existence type points to the previous one.
Notice the three types of indication mentioned.
2
Distinguish rational from conventional
The first indication (mental to external) is rational and does not vary. The other two are conventional and vary.
Rational here means based on the nature of things, not arbitrary.
3
Analyze the two conventional indications
In the indication from verbal to mental, only the indicator (word) varies. In the indication from written to verbal, both the indicator (script) and the indicated (word) can vary.
Example: The concept 'cat' is same in mind, but words differ across languages; scripts differ even for same word.
4
Reflect on implications
This distinction explains why different languages can refer to the same reality, and why writing systems can differ.

?
When you speak, do you assume your words perfectly transfer your mental concepts to others? How does knowing that verbal indication is conventional—not rational—humble you to check that you are understood correctly?
?
Your mental concepts indicate external reality rationally. If your concepts are shaped by unchecked desires or false assumptions, what does that imply about your grasp of truth? Will you commit to examining your inner conceptions today?
?
The written word indicates the spoken word conventionally. How often do you let the imperfections of writing (your own or others') distort the intended meaning? What will you do to ensure your reading seeks the intended message beyond the script?

6 min
When Verbal and Written Existence are Real
The author clarifies the dual nature of verbal and written existence as metaphorical and real depending on the relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that verbal and written existences are metaphorical relative to the thing itself, but can be considered real when related to the word or script itself.

Core Idea
Words and writing are real in their own right, but only metaphorical when standing for the thing.

The author qualifies the metaphorical nature of verbal and written existence. While they are not the thing itself, they become real existences when considered as utterances or inscriptions that have their own reality. This nuance prevents misunderstanding that words and writing are completely unreal.

Guided Path
1
Read the qualifying statement
Read the Arabic 'نعم إذا أضيف ...' and its translation. Note the shift from metaphorical to real.
This is a clarification of the earlier statement that verbal and written existences are metaphorical.
2
Distinguish two relations
Relative to the thing they signify, verbal and written existences are metaphorical. Relative to themselves (the sound or shape), they are real existences like external things.
The utterance is a real sound; the script is a real inscription.
3
Reflect on the example of Zayd
The author mentions Zayd: his name uttered is a sound, not his essence. But the sound itself is real. Similarly, the written name is a real shape.
This shows that the same existence can be considered from different perspectives.
4
Understand the analogy
When verbal/written existence is considered in relation to the word itself, it is like external existence (from the category of existence in external things).

Correction: They are metaphorical relative to the thing, but real as sounds and inscriptions themselves.

Checkpoint
According to the text, when does verbal existence become 'real existence'?
A) When it refers to an external object
B) When it is considered in relation to the thing it signifies
C) When it is considered in relation to the utterance itself
D) When it is written down

Key Takeaway
Verbal and written existences are real when considered as sounds and script themselves, even though they are metaphorical representations of the things they signify.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak the name of Allah, do you treat that sound as merely a tool, or do you recognize that the sound itself has a real existence that should be honored? How does this change the way you utter His name?
?
If written words of the Quran are real in themselves, what does that demand of you when you handle a mus'haf? Are you careful with the physical book and the ink, not just the meaning?
?
This lesson shows that words and writing are not mere illusions but have real existence. How does that affect your understanding of the hadith about the Quran interceding for its reciter? Does the physical recitation have real weight?

Definition of Indication and Its Divisions: Rational, Natural, Conventional
The author defines indication and classifies it into rational, natural, and conventional types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of indication (dalala) and its three-fold classification into rational, natural, and conventional types.

Core Idea
Indication is a relationship that can be based on reason, nature, or convention.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid establishes the foundational concept of indication (dalala), which is essential for understanding how signs and meanings relate. The author first defines indication as the necessary connection between two known things, then divides it into three types based on the nature of the relationship. Each type plays a crucial role in theological and linguistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the formal definition of indication and the three types.
Notice that the passage begins with 'If it is said' but then gives the definition.
2
Identify the definition and the three types
Find the sentence that defines indication: knowledge of one thing necessitates knowledge of another. Then note the division into rational, natural, and conventional.
The passage also distinguishes between verbal and non-verbal indication before the three types.
3
Examine the examples
The passage gives examples for rational indication (redness for shyness) and conventional indication (word for meaning). What might be an example of natural indication?
Natural indication is instinctive, like a baby crying when hungry.
4
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this classification matters: Not all indication is conventional; some are based on reason or nature, which is important for arguments about God's existence and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : -
ذلك
اتحاده
معنى الدلالة : () كون الشيء بحيث يفهم منه شيء آخر، فإذا اعتبرت فيما
(۱)
الدلالة هي أن يلزم من العلم بالشيء علم بشيء آخر، والشيء الأول هو الدال، والثاني هو المدلول. فإن كان الدال لفظاً كانت الدلالة لفظية وإن كان غير ذلك كانت الدلالة غير لفظية . وكل واحدة من اللفظية وغير اللفظية تنقسم إلى عقلية وطبيعية ووضعية . أن فالعقلية : هي
يجد العقل بين الدال والمدلول علاقة طبيعية تنقله من أحدهما إلى الآخر.
كدلالة الحمرة على الخجل والصفرة على الوجل. والدلالة الوضعية أن يكون بين الدال والمدلول
علاقة الوضع كدلالة اللفظ على المعنى .
راجع تعريفات الجرجاني).
If it is said: ... the meaning of indication is: that something be such that another thing is understood from it. Therefore, if it is considered in that... Indication is that knowledge of a thing necessitates knowledge of another thing, and the first thing is the indicator (دال) and the second is the indicated (مدلول). If the indicator is a word, the indication is verbal; if it is other than that, the indication is non-verbal. And each one of the verbal and non-verbal is divided into rational, natural, and conventional. As for rational: it is that the intellect finds a natural relation between the indicator and the indicated that transfers it from one to the other, like the indication of redness upon shyness and yellowness upon fear. And conventional indication is that there is a relation of convention between the indicator and the indicated, like the indication of the word upon the meaning. (Refer to al-Jurjani's Definitions).
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage provides a formal definition of indication (دلالة) and its divisions. It begins with a question (فإن قيل) that leads into the definition: indication is when knowledge of one thing (the indicator) necessarily leads to knowledge of another thing (the indicated). The indicator can be a word (verbal indication) or something else (non-verbal). Both verbal and non-verbal indications are then divided into three types: rational (عقلية), natural (طبيعية), and conventional (وضعية). Rational indication is when the intellect perceives a natural relationship between the indicator and the indicated, such as redness indicating shyness or yellowness indicating fear. Natural indication would be when the relationship is instinctive. Conventional indication is when the relationship is established by convention, like words indicating meanings. The passage ends by referring to al-Jurjani's Definitions for more details.

مدلول

دلالة وضعية
لفظية
غير لفظية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All indication is conventional, relying on human agreement.
Correction: Some indication is rational (based on natural relations perceived by intellect) or natural (instinctive), not merely conventional.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the definition of indication (دلالة)?
A) The correspondence between a word and its meaning
B) That knowledge of one thing necessitates knowledge of another thing
C) The relation of a sign to its referent in the external world
D) A mental concept that is expressed by language

Key Takeaway
Indication is a necessary relation between two known things, and it can be based on reason, nature, or human convention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You understand that rational indication connects things naturally in the intellect. Look at the universe: do you see it as a rational indicator of its Creator, or do you need conventional revelation to be certain? What would change if you relied solely on rational indication for your faith?
?
The passage distinguishes types of indication. When you read the Qur'an, do you treat it as purely conventional, or do you also seek the rational and natural signs within it? How might that affect your understanding of its meanings?
?
Consider a moment you misread a sign—like mistaking shyness for guilt. Reflect: how often do you misread God's signs in your life because you assume the wrong type of indication? What step can you take today to see His signs as they truly are?

The Universal Exists Only in the Mind
Taftazani argues that universals exist only in the mind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why universals, as concepts, can only exist in the mind and not in the external world.

Core Idea
Universals are mental concepts, not external realities.

Taftazani presents a crisp argument for the mental existence of universals. He defines the universal as a concept and then shows that since everything external is particular, the universal must be in the mind. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Taftazani structures his argument in two clear sentences.
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the definition
Find where Taftazani defines the universal. He calls it a concept (mafhum) and says every concept is established in the mind.
This is the starting point of the argument.
3
Understand the proof
Notice the premise that everything in the external world is particular (mushakhkhas). Since the universal applies to many, it cannot be external.
This is the key logical move.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on why this matters: it refutes the view that universals exist as Platonic forms independently in the world.
The lesson guards against a common misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن الكلي، مفهوم، وكل مفهوم ثابت ضرورة تميزه عند العقل. فالكلي ثابت وليس في الخارج، لأن كل ما هو في الخارج مشخص فيكون في الذهن.
Second: The universal is a concept, and every concept is necessarily established by its distinction in the mind. So the universal is existent and not in the external world, because everything in the external world is particular, so it is in the mind.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a concise definition and proof. The author (Taftazani) presents the second point (al-thani) in a series of arguments for mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). He defines the universal (al-kulli) as a concept (mafhum), which is distinguished in the mind. Therefore, it must be established (thabit) in the mind. He argues that everything in the external world is particular, so what is true of many (the universal) cannot be externally; it must be in the mind.

Key Terms
الكلي
المفهوم
الوجود الذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universals exist as Platonic forms independent of the mind.
Correction: According to Taftazani, universals are concepts that exist only in the mind, not as external realities.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why must the universal be in the mind?
A) Because it is a concept distinguished by the mind.
B) Because the external world contains only particulars.
C) Both A and B.
D) Because the universal is a substance.

Key Takeaway
Universals are mental concepts that do not exist as physical entities in the external world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of the attribute 'merciful' (الرحمن) of Allah, do you consider it as a universal concept in your mind, or as something real in the external world? This passage forces you to admit that the universal 'mercy' is a mental concept, but Allah's mercy is real. How do you reconcile that? Does this change how you think about the reality of divine attributes? Will you now be more careful to avoid reducing God's attributes to mere mental concepts?
?
If universals like 'justice' only exist in the mind, on what basis do you claim that an action is truly just? Is your concept of justice grounded only in your mind, or does it correspond to an external reality commanded by Allah? This lesson challenges you to examine the foundation of your moral judgments. Are they built on mental constructs alone, or do they have a firm basis in revelation? Resolve to check your understanding of right and wrong against the Qur'an and Sunnah, not just your own ideas.

The State (al-Hal) as an Intermediary and Refutation of al-Katibi's Objection
Defines the state and rebuts al-Katibi's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-hal as an attribute that is neither existent nor non-existent, and how the response to al-Katibi's objection clarifies its scope.

Core Idea
The state is an attribute that bridges existence and non-existence, but not an independent entity.

This lesson explains the key kalam concept of 'al-hal' (the state), a special category of attributes that cannot be classified as either existent or non-existent. The passage from al-Sanusi's work defines it carefully and then addresses an objection raised by al-Katibi based on Mu'tazilite views, showing the nuance within different schools.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the careful phrasing: 'attribute of an existent that is neither existent nor non-existent'.
Focus on the phrase 'واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم'.
2
Identify the three qualifiers
The passage lists three restrictions: 'for an existent', 'not existent', 'not non-existent'. Understand what each excludes.
These qualifiers prevent the definition from including attributes of non-existents, positive attributes, or negative attributes.
3
Understand al-Katibi's objection
Al-Katibi claims the definition fails for Mu'tazila because they consider substantiality (al-jawhariyya) a state for essences in both existence and non-existence.
This objection targets the 'for an existent' qualifier.
4
Analyze the response
The response shows that al-Katibi's objection only applies to a specific view within Mu'tazila (Abu Hashim's), not the entire school. Thus the definition stands for the intended audience.
Note the conditional 'لو ثبت' — the objection is not automatically accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للغير فهو الحال، فهو واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم، لأنه عبارة عن صفة للموجود لا تكون موجودة ولا معدومة مثل العالمية والقادرية ونحو ذلك . والمراد بالصفة مالا يعلم ولا يخبر عنه بالاستقلال بل بتبعية الغير، والذات بخلافها، وهي لا تكون إلا موجودة أو معدومة، بل لا معنى للموجود إلا ذات لها صفة العدم، والصفة لا يكون لها ذات فلا تكون موجودة ولا معدومة. فلذا قيد بالصفة واحترز بقولهم : لموجود عن صفات المعدوم فإنها تكون معدومة لا حالا . وبقولهم : لا موجودة عن الصفات الوجودية مثل السواد والبياض وبقولهم : ولا معدومة عن الصفات السلبية. قال الكاتبي: وهذا الحد لا يصح على مذهب المعتزلة، لأنهم جعلوا الجوهرية من الأحوال مع أنها حاصلة للذات حالتي الوجود والعدم . قلنا : إنما يتم هذا الاعتراض لو ثبت ذلك من أبي هاشم، وإلا فمن المعتزلة من لا يقول بالحال، ومنهم من يقول بها لا على هذا الوجه
is for the other, then it is the state. So it is an intermediary between the existent and the non-existent, because it is an expression of an attribute of an existent that is neither existent nor non-existent, such as 'worldliness' (al-'alamiyya) and 'powerfulness' (al-qadiriyya) and the like. And what is meant by attribute (al-sifa) is that which is not known or reported about independently but by following another, whereas the essence (al-dhat) is contrary to it, and it (the essence) is only existent or non-existent. Rather, there is no meaning for 'existent' except an essence having the attribute of existence, and the attribute does not have an essence, so it is neither existent nor non-existent. Therefore, it was restricted by 'attribute' and guarded by their saying: 'for an existent' against the attributes of the non-existent, for they are non-existent, not a state. And by their saying: 'not existent' against existence-attributions such as blackness and whiteness. And by their saying: 'and not non-existent' against negative attributes. Al-Katibi said: 'This definition is not valid according to the school of the Mu'tazila, because they made substantiality (al-jawhariyya) one of the states, even though it belongs to the essence in both states of existence and non-existence.' We said: 'This objection would only be valid if that were established from Abu Hashim; otherwise, among the Mu'tazila are those who do not speak of the state, and among them are those who speak of it, but not in this manner.'
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'al-hal' (the state) and responds to an objection. It begins by explaining that the state is a middle category between existent and non-existent, exemplified by attributes like 'worldliness' and 'powerfulness'. It clarifies that an attribute is not known independently but only through its subject, unlike the essence. The definition is then qualified with three conditions: 'for an existent' excludes attributes of non-existents; 'not existent' excludes positive attributes like colors; 'not non-existent' excludes negative attributes. Then al-Katibi's objection is presented: the definition fails on Mu'tazilite grounds because they consider substantiality a state even for non-existent essences. The reply refutes this by noting that not all Mu'tazila hold that view; it is specific to Abu Hashim, and even among his followers there is variation.

الحال
الموجود
المعدوم
الصفة
الذات
العالمية
القادرية
الجوهرية
أبو هاشم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the state is a third independent entity, like a separate substance.
Correction: The passage makes clear that the state is always an attribute of an existent and cannot exist independently; it is a dependent property.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of 'al-hal' according to this passage?
A) Something that is existent and non-existent simultaneously.
B) An attribute of an existent that is neither existent nor non-existent.
C) A non-existent attribute.
D) The essence itself.

Key Takeaway
The state is a carefully defined attribute category that avoids being classified as existent or non-existent, serving as a middle term in kalam ontology.
