The Two-Part Classification by Majority Mu'tazila
Majority Mu'tazilite classification of the known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the majority of Mu'tazila classify the known using two distinct perspectives, and the logical relationships between the resulting categories.

Core Idea
The known is classified by being and by self-realization, yielding four distinct categories.

The majority of Mu'tazila reject the notion that the non-existent is established. Instead, they offer two complementary ways to analyze the known: one based on whether it has being in external things (existent vs. non-existent), and another based on whether it has realization in itself (established vs. negated). Understanding these two perspectives and their interaction is essential for grasping early kalām ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on majority view
Read the Arabic passage describing the majority Mu'tazilite classification. Notice that they deny a middle state between existent and non-existent.
The passage starts with 'ومنهم من خالف في نفي كون المعدوم ثابتاً'.
2
Identify the two perspectives
The passage gives two 'اعتبارات': one based on 'كون في الأعيان' (being in external things) and another based on 'تحقق في نفسه' (self-realization). Identify the categories resulting from each.
First perspective: existent vs. non-existent. Second: established (ثابت) vs. negated (منفي).
3
Analyze the logical relationships
The passage notes that existent is more specific than established, and negated is more specific than non-existent. This implies some non-existent things are established (thabit). Try to see why.
Everything existent has self-realization, but not everything self-realized has external being. Similarly, everything negated lacks external being, but not everything lacking external being is negated.
4
Answer the checkpoint
Test your understanding with the multiple-choice question about the relationship between existent and established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من خالف في نفي كون المعدوم ثابتاً وهم أكثر المعتزلة، حيث زعموا أن المعلوم إن كان له كون في الأعيان فموجود وإلا فمعدوم فلا واسطة بينهما وباعتبار آخر : المعلوم إن كان له تحقق في نفسه وتقرر فثابت وإلا فمنفي وكل ما له كون في الأعيان فله تقرر في نفسه من غير عكس فيكون الموجود أخص من الثابت. وكل ما لا تقرر له في نفسه لا كون له في الأعيان وليس كل ما لا كون له تقرر له، فيكون المنفي أخص من المعدوم، فيكون بعض المعدوم لا منفياً بل ثابتاً، ومنهم من خالف في الأمرين جميعاً وهم بعض المعتزلة قالوا : - المعلوم إن كان له كون في الأعيان فإن كان له ذلك بالاستقلال فهو الموجود، وإن كان بتبعية الغير فهو الحال، وإن لم يكن له كون في الأعيان فهو المعدوم، والمعدوم إن كان متحققاً في نفسه فثابت وإلا فمنفي. فقد جعلوا بعض المعدوم ثابتاً . وأثبتوا بين الموجود والمعدوم واسطة هو الحال، وظاهر العبارة يوهم أن الثابت قسم من المعدوم ، وليس كذلك. بل بينهما عموم من وجه لأنه يشمل الموجود والحال بخلاف المعدوم والمعدوم يشمل المنفي بخلاف الثابت وان كان المعدوم مبايناً للمنفي على ما صرح به في تلخيص المحصل من أن القائلين بكون المعدوم شيئاً لا يقولون للممتنع معدوم بل منفي . كان الأول في هذا التقسيم أن يقال : المعلوم إن لم يتحقق في نفسه فمنفي، وإن تحقق فإن كان له كون في الأعيان فإما بالاستقلال ،فموجود، أو بالتبيعة فحال ، وإن لم يكن له كون في الأعيان فمعدوم وفي التقسيم السابق أنه إن لم يتحقق فمنفي، وان تحقق فثابت، وحينئذ إن كان له كون في الأعيان فموجود، وإلا فمعدوم
And among them are those who opposed the denial that the non-existent is established, and they are the majority of the Mu'tazila, for they claimed that the known (al-ma'lum) if it has being (kawn) in external things (a'yan) then it is existent, otherwise it is non-existent, so there is no intermediary between them. And from another perspective: the known if it has realization (tahaqquq) in itself and determination (taqarrur) then it is established (thabit), otherwise it is negated (munfi). And everything that has being in external things has determination in itself, without the reverse, so the existent is more specific than the established. And everything that has no determination in itself has no being in external things, and not everything that has no being has determination, so the negated is more specific than the non-existent. Thus, some of the non-existent is not negated but rather established. And among them are those who opposed both matters, and they are some of the Mu'tazila. They said: the known, if it has being in external things, then if it has that independently, it is the existent; if it has it by following another, it is the state; and if it has no being in external things, it is the non-existent. And the non-existent, if it is realized in itself, then it is established, otherwise it is negated. So they made some of the non-existent established. And they affirmed between the existent and the non-existent an intermediary which is the state. And the apparent wording implies that the established is a division of the non-existent, but it is not so. Rather, between them there is general-specific from one aspect because it includes the existent and the state, unlike the non-existent; and the non-existent includes the negated, unlike the established, even if the non-existent is distinct from the negated according to what was clarified in Talkhis al-Muhassal: that those who say the non-existent is a thing do not call the impossible non-existent but rather negated. The first in this division is to say: the known, if it is not realized in itself, then it is negated; and if it is realized, then if it has being in external things, then either independently, then it is existent, or by following, then it is a state; and if it has no being in external things, then it is non-existent. And in the previous division, if it is not realized, it is negated; if realized, it is established. In that case, if it has being in external things, it is existent, otherwise non-existent.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed classification of the known (al-ma'lum) according to different Mu'tazilite groups, and then offers a preferred division. It first describes the view of the majority of Mu'tazila who deny a middle state between existent and non-existent, using two perspectives: one from being in external things (resulting in existent vs non-existent), another from self-realization (resulting in established (thabit) vs negated (munfi)). They note that existent is more specific than thabit, and munfi is more specific than non-existent, so some non-existent things are thabit. Then it presents another group that accepts both: they introduce the state as a middle when being is dependent, and also allow that some non-existent are thabit. The author points out that this could wrongly suggest thabit is a subset of non-existent, but clarifies that thabit is broader, including the existent and the state, while non-existent includes the negated. Finally, the author gives his preferred classification: the known is either not realized (negated) or realized. If realized, it either has being in external things (independently = existent, dependently = state) or has no such being (non-existent). This corrects the previous divisions.

Key Terms
المعلوم
الكون
الأعيان
الموجود
المعدوم
الثابت
المنفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'thabit' (established) is synonymous with 'mawjud' (existent).
Correction: The passage clarifies that existent is more specific than thabit; everything existent is thabit, but some thabit things (like possible non-existents) are not existent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following correctly describes the relationship between existent (mawjud) and established (thabit)?
A) They are equivalent.
B) Existent is more specific than established.
C) Established is more specific than existent.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
The majority Mu'tazila classify the known using two independent perspectives, yielding four categories, with existent as a subset of thabit and negated as a subset of non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you sometimes treat your thoughts and plans as if they are already real (mawjud), ignoring the hard work needed to bring them into external existence? How can the distinction between 'thabit' (established in mind) and 'mawjud' (established in reality) help you realign your priorities?
?
The passage shows that some non-existent things are still 'established' (thabit) in themselves. Think of your unfulfilled aspirations—are they merely absent, or do they have a kind of reality in your heart? What does that demand of you? Are you content with them being only thabit, or will you strive to make them mawjud?
?
If God knows all 'known' things, including those that are nonexistent yet thabit, how does this challenge your assumption that only what exists matters? How should this affect your confidence in God's knowledge of every possibility, and your trust that He judges what is truly established in your character, even if not yet manifested?

The Intermediary State (al-Hal) in Mu'tazilite Ontology
Minority Mu'tazilite view introducing the state as intermediary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how some Mu'tazila introduce the concept of 'state' (hal) as an intermediary between existent and non-existent, and how they distinguish between independent and dependent being.

Core Idea
The state (hal) acts as a third ontological category: it has dependent being in external things.

While the majority Mu'tazila denied any middle ground between existence and non-existence, a minority group proposed an intermediary called the 'state' (al-hal). For them, some entities have being in external things but only in a dependent manner, such as attributes inhering in substances. This lesson explores that view and its implications for the classification of the known.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the minority view
Read the Arabic passage that describes the view of those who oppose both matters (the majority's two perspectives). They introduce a tripartite division.
The passage starts with 'ومنهم من خالف في الأمرين جميعاً وهم بعض المعتزلة'.
2
Identify the three categories
The minority group divides the known based on being in external things: if it has being independently → existent; if dependently → state; if no being → non-existent. Also, they apply the second perspective (self-realization) to non-existent: if realized → thabit; if not → negated.
This results in five categories: existent, state, non-existent (which can be thabit or negated).
3
Understand the role of the state
The state is considered an intermediary between existent and non-existent because it has being in external things, but not independently. It 'follows' another entity (e.g., an accident exists in a substance).
This was a key concept in later kalām discussions on attributes.
4
Reflect on the overlap with thabit
Note that the minority still consider some non-existent things as thabit (established). So thabit now includes existent, state, and some non-existent (the possible but non-existent).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من خالف في نفي كون المعدوم ثابتاً وهم أكثر المعتزلة، حيث زعموا أن المعلوم إن كان له كون في الأعيان فموجود وإلا فمعدوم فلا واسطة بينهما وباعتبار آخر : المعلوم إن كان له تحقق في نفسه وتقرر فثابت وإلا فمنفي وكل ما له كون في الأعيان فله تقرر في نفسه من غير عكس فيكون الموجود أخص من الثابت. وكل ما لا تقرر له في نفسه لا كون له في الأعيان وليس كل ما لا كون له تقرر له، فيكون المنفي أخص من المعدوم، فيكون بعض المعدوم لا منفياً بل ثابتاً، ومنهم من خالف في الأمرين جميعاً وهم بعض المعتزلة قالوا : - المعلوم إن كان له كون في الأعيان فإن كان له ذلك بالاستقلال فهو الموجود، وإن كان بتبعية الغير فهو الحال، وإن لم يكن له كون في الأعيان فهو المعدوم، والمعدوم إن كان متحققاً في نفسه فثابت وإلا فمنفي. فقد جعلوا بعض المعدوم ثابتاً . وأثبتوا بين الموجود والمعدوم واسطة هو الحال، وظاهر العبارة يوهم أن الثابت قسم من المعدوم ، وليس كذلك. بل بينهما عموم من وجه لأنه يشمل الموجود والحال بخلاف المعدوم والمعدوم يشمل المنفي بخلاف الثابت وان كان المعدوم مبايناً للمنفي على ما صرح به في تلخيص المحصل من أن القائلين بكون المعدوم شيئاً لا يقولون للممتنع معدوم بل منفي . كان الأول في هذا التقسيم أن يقال : المعلوم إن لم يتحقق في نفسه فمنفي، وإن تحقق فإن كان له كون في الأعيان فإما بالاستقلال ،فموجود، أو بالتبيعة فحال ، وإن لم يكن له كون في الأعيان فمعدوم وفي التقسيم السابق أنه إن لم يتحقق فمنفي، وان تحقق فثابت، وحينئذ إن كان له كون في الأعيان فموجود، وإلا فمعدوم
And among them are those who opposed the denial that the non-existent is established, and they are the majority of the Mu'tazila, for they claimed that the known (al-ma'lum) if it has being (kawn) in external things (a'yan) then it is existent, otherwise it is non-existent, so there is no intermediary between them. And from another perspective: the known if it has realization (tahaqquq) in itself and determination (taqarrur) then it is established (thabit), otherwise it is negated (munfi). And everything that has being in external things has determination in itself, without the reverse, so the existent is more specific than the established. And everything that has no determination in itself has no being in external things, and not everything that has no being has determination, so the negated is more specific than the non-existent. Thus, some of the non-existent is not negated but rather established. And among them are those who opposed both matters, and they are some of the Mu'tazila. They said: the known, if it has being in external things, then if it has that independently, it is the existent; if it has it by following another, it is the state; and if it has no being in external things, it is the non-existent. And the non-existent, if it is realized in itself, then it is established, otherwise it is negated. So they made some of the non-existent established. And they affirmed between the existent and the non-existent an intermediary which is the state. And the apparent wording implies that the established is a division of the non-existent, but it is not so. Rather, between them there is general-specific from one aspect because it includes the existent and the state, unlike the non-existent; and the non-existent includes the negated, unlike the established, even if the non-existent is distinct from the negated according to what was clarified in Talkhis al-Muhassal: that those who say the non-existent is a thing do not call the impossible non-existent but rather negated. The first in this division is to say: the known, if it is not realized in itself, then it is negated; and if it is realized, then if it has being in external things, then either independently, then it is existent, or by following, then it is a state; and if it has no being in external things, then it is non-existent. And in the previous division, if it is not realized, it is negated; if realized, it is established. In that case, if it has being in external things, it is existent, otherwise non-existent.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed classification of the known (al-ma'lum) according to different Mu'tazilite groups, and then offers a preferred division. It first describes the view of the majority of Mu'tazila who deny a middle state between existent and non-existent, using two perspectives: one from being in external things (resulting in existent vs non-existent), another from self-realization (resulting in established (thabit) vs negated (munfi)). They note that existent is more specific than thabit, and munfi is more specific than non-existent, so some non-existent things are thabit. Then it presents another group that accepts both: they introduce the state as a middle when being is dependent, and also allow that some non-existent are thabit. The author points out that this could wrongly suggest thabit is a subset of non-existent, but clarifies that thabit is broader, including the existent and the state, while non-existent includes the negated. Finally, the author gives his preferred classification: the known is either not realized (negated) or realized. If realized, it either has being in external things (independently = existent, dependently = state) or has no such being (non-existent). This corrects the previous divisions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the state (hal) is a kind of non-existent because it is not an independent substance.
Correction: The state has being in external things, albeit dependently. It is not non-existent; it is an intermediary existent.

Checkpoint
According to the minority Mu'tazilite view, what distinguishes the state (hal) from the existent (mawjud)?
A) The state has no being in external things.
B) The existent has independent being, while the state has dependent being.
C) The state is a type of non-existent.
D) The existent is always a substance, while the state is always an accident.

Key Takeaway
Some Mu'tazila introduce the state (hal) as an intermediary ontological category for entities with dependent being, and they maintain that some non-existent things are thabit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the attributes of Allah, do you instinctively treat them as independent realities or as dependent states? How does this classification challenge your current understanding? Will you study the Mu'tazilite position on attributes more deeply to test your own assumptions?
?
The passage says some non-existent things are 'established' in themselves. How does this affect your understanding of Allah's knowledge of possible worlds before creation? Can you accept that possibilities have some form of reality in the divine mind? What change in your worship might that require?
?
If the state (al-hal) is ontologically dependent, how does that shape your view of the relationship between Allah and His attributes? Are you comfortable with the idea that attributes are not separate entities but modes of the Essence? How does this affect your reliance on Allah's attributes in your daily life?

Refining the Classification: The Author's Preferred Division
Author's correction and preferred classification of the known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's correction of the previous classifications, especially the clarification that thabit is not a subset of non-existent, and his own preferred orderly division of the known.

Core Idea
Thabit is broader than non-existent; the correct division starts with self-realization, then external being.

After presenting the various Mu'tazilite views, the author (Sanusi or the commentator) offers his own refined classification. He warns against a misunderstanding that might arise from the minority view—that the established (thabit) might be considered a subdivision of the non-existent. He clarifies that thabit actually encompasses both the existent and the state, while non-existent includes only the negated. Then he provides a systematic division that avoids confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the author's refinement
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وظاهر العبارة يوهم' to the end. The author critiques the apparent implication and provides the correct division.
This passage contains the author's own preferred order.
2
Understand the critique
The author notes that the wording of the minority view might suggest that thabit is a part of non-existent. But that is wrong. Actually, thabit and non-existent overlap partially: thabit includes existent and state, while non-existent includes negated. They are not in a genus-species relationship.
He says: 'بل بينهما عموم من وجه' – they have a general-specific relationship from one aspect.
3
Learn the preferred division
The author orders the divisions logically: First, check self-realization: if not realized → negated (munfi). If realized, then check external being: if independent → existent; if dependent → state; if none → non-existent. Note that in this scheme, 'non-existent' is restricted to realized but not externally present.
He also mentions an alternative previous division: if realized → thabit; then if external being → existent, else non-existent.
4
Compare the two orders
Compare the author's preferred division (starting with self-realization) with the previous one (starting with external being). See how they differ in the placement of 'non-existent' and 'thabit'.
In the preferred order, non-existent is a subset of thabit (since non-existent is realized but not externally present). The author's order avoids confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من خالف في نفي كون المعدوم ثابتاً وهم أكثر المعتزلة، حيث زعموا أن المعلوم إن كان له كون في الأعيان فموجود وإلا فمعدوم فلا واسطة بينهما وباعتبار آخر : المعلوم إن كان له تحقق في نفسه وتقرر فثابت وإلا فمنفي وكل ما له كون في الأعيان فله تقرر في نفسه من غير عكس فيكون الموجود أخص من الثابت. وكل ما لا تقرر له في نفسه لا كون له في الأعيان وليس كل ما لا كون له تقرر له، فيكون المنفي أخص من المعدوم، فيكون بعض المعدوم لا منفياً بل ثابتاً، ومنهم من خالف في الأمرين جميعاً وهم بعض المعتزلة قالوا : - المعلوم إن كان له كون في الأعيان فإن كان له ذلك بالاستقلال فهو الموجود، وإن كان بتبعية الغير فهو الحال، وإن لم يكن له كون في الأعيان فهو المعدوم، والمعدوم إن كان متحققاً في نفسه فثابت وإلا فمنفي. فقد جعلوا بعض المعدوم ثابتاً . وأثبتوا بين الموجود والمعدوم واسطة هو الحال، وظاهر العبارة يوهم أن الثابت قسم من المعدوم ، وليس كذلك. بل بينهما عموم من وجه لأنه يشمل الموجود والحال بخلاف المعدوم والمعدوم يشمل المنفي بخلاف الثابت وان كان المعدوم مبايناً للمنفي على ما صرح به في تلخيص المحصل من أن القائلين بكون المعدوم شيئاً لا يقولون للممتنع معدوم بل منفي . كان الأول في هذا التقسيم أن يقال : المعلوم إن لم يتحقق في نفسه فمنفي، وإن تحقق فإن كان له كون في الأعيان فإما بالاستقلال ،فموجود، أو بالتبيعة فحال ، وإن لم يكن له كون في الأعيان فمعدوم وفي التقسيم السابق أنه إن لم يتحقق فمنفي، وان تحقق فثابت، وحينئذ إن كان له كون في الأعيان فموجود، وإلا فمعدوم

According to the author's preferred division, if something is realized in itself but has no being in external things, what is it called?
A) Negated (munfi)
B) Non-existent (ma'dum)
C) State (hal)
D) Existent (mawjud)

Key Takeaway
The author corrects a potential confusion by showing that thabit is broader than non-existent, and his preferred division orders categories by self-realization first, then mode of external being.

?

The Literal and Metaphorical Use of 'Thing'
Taftazani presents his view and the Mu'tazilite view on 'thing'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's own view that 'thing' is literal for the existent and metaphorical for the non-existent, as well as the Mu'tazilite view that it is a name for the known and the problem of the impossible.

Core Idea
The term 'thing' is literally for existents, not for non-existents.

Taftazani begins by clarifying that the debate is not about metaphorical usage but about literal meaning. He aligns himself with the view of al-Basri and al-Nasibi, who held that 'thing' (shay) is used literally for existing entities and metaphorically for non-existing ones. He then presents the Mu'tazilite understanding that 'thing' is a name for what is known, which leads to the problematic conclusion that the impossible would be a thing, a consequence they do not accept.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Focus on the author's own view and the Mu'tazilite objection.
Notice the phrase 'وهو مذهبنا بعينه'—the author is stating his position.
2
Identify the two main views
The passage presents two views: (1) the author's view (with al-Basri and al-Nasibi) that 'thing' is literal for existent and metaphorical for non-existent; (2) the Mu'tazilite view that it is a name for the known. Ask yourself: what is the problem with the Mu'tazilite view?
3
Reflect on the implications
The passage implies that if 'thing' covers all knowns, then the impossible (al-mustahil) would be a thing, but the Mu'tazilites deny that. Consider the exception mentioned: perhaps they deny that the impossible is known.
This connects to the doctrine of ma'lum in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا ينفي الاستعمال المجازي بل الحقيقي ومـا ذكــره أبو الحسين (٢) البصري (١) والنصيبي ) (٢) من أنه حقيقة في الوجود مجاز في المعدوم، وهو مذهبنا بعينه، وعند كثير من المعتزلة هو اسم للمعلوم، ويلزمهم أن يكون المستحيل شيئاً وهو لا يقولون به ، اللهم إلا أن يمنع كون المستحيل معلوماً على ما سيأتي أو يمنع عدم قولهم بإطلاق الشيء عليه. فقد ذكر جار الله أنه اسم لما يصح أن يعلم يستوي فيه الموجود والمعدوم، والمحال والمستقيم، والذي لا قائل به هو كونه شيئاً، بمعنى الثبوت في الخارج. وعند بعضهم هو اسم لما ليس بمستحيل موجوداً كان أو معدوماً، وما نقل عن أبي العباس الناشيء (٣) . أنه اسم للقديم، وعن الجهمية (٤) . أنه اسم للحادث، وعن هشام بن الحكم (٥) أنه اسم للجسم، فبعيد جداً من جهة أنه لا يجوز أن يقبله أهل اللغة ولا تقوم عليه شبهة لا من جهة وقوع استعماله في غير ما ذكر كل منهم، فإن له أن يقول هو مجاز. كما نقول نحن في مثل قوله تعالى إنما قولنا الشيء) وكون الأصل في الإطلاق هو الحقيقة مشترك الإلزام، فلا بد من الرجوع إلى أمر آخر من نقل، أو كثرة استعمال أو مبادرة فهم أو نحو ذلك .
It does not negate the metaphorical usage but the literal one. And what Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and al-Nasibi mentioned – that it is literal for existence and metaphorical for non-existence – is precisely our doctrine. According to many Mu'tazilites, it is a name for the known [entity], and they would be required that the impossible be a thing, but they do not hold that, unless it is argued that the impossible is not known as will come, or that they do not deny applying 'thing' to it. Jar Allah stated that it is a name for what can be known, equally applicable to existent and non-existent, to impossible and possible; what no one holds is that it is a thing in the sense of external subsistence. According to some, it is a name for what is not impossible, whether existent or non-existent. What is reported from Abu al-Abbas al-Nashi' that it is a name for the eternal, and from the Jahmiyyah that it is a name for the originated, and from Hisham ibn al-Hakam that it is a name for the body, is extremely remote, because it cannot be accepted by linguists, nor can any evidence support it; and the fact that it is used in other than what each of them mentioned can be argued as metaphorical. As we say in the case of His statement 'Our saying to a thing is but...' and the principle that the literal meaning is the default in usage is a common point – so one must refer to another matter: transmission, frequency of use, immediate understanding, or the like.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the various definitions of the term 'شيء' (thing) in kalam, beginning with the author's own view. The passage opens by clarifying that the issue is not about metaphorical usage but literal meaning. The author states that Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and al-Nasibi held that 'thing' is literal for the existent and metaphorical for the non-existent, which is precisely the author's own doctrine. Then he notes that many Mu'tazilites consider it a name for what is known, which would entail that the impossible (al-mustahil) is a thing, but they do not accept that, unless they deny that the impossible is known or that they would deny the application of 'thing' to it. Jar Allah (al-Zamakhshari) is cited as saying it is a name for what can be known, equally applying to existent and non-existent, to impossible and possible; what no one holds is that it is a thing in the sense of external subsistence. Other views are mentioned: some say it is a name for what is not impossible; Abu al-Abbas al-Nashi' says it is a name for the eternal (al-qadim); the Jahmiyyah say it is a name for the originated (al-hadith); Hisham ibn al-Hakam says it is a name for the body. The author dismisses these as extremely remote, because linguists would not accept them and they have no evidence; moreover, any usage in other than the claimed sense can be explained as metaphorical. The passage concludes that since the default is literal meaning, one must rely on transmitted usage, frequency, immediate understanding, or similar evidence to settle the definition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute about 'thing' is merely lexical and has no theological implications.
Correction: The passage shows that the definition of 'thing' directly affects whether the impossible (al-mustahil) can be considered a thing, which has implications for the reality of non-existents and the scope of Allah's knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the author's own view on the meaning of 'thing' (shay)?
A) It is literal for the existent and metaphorical for the non-existent.
B) It is a name for what is known (al-ma'lum).
C) It is a name for what is not impossible.
D) It is a name for the eternal.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani holds that 'thing' literally refers to existents, not non-existents, and he critiques the Mu'tazilite view for implying the impossible is a thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak of 'nothing' or 'non-existence', do you ever consider that such words carry theological weight? After learning that Taftazani insists 'thing' is literally only for existents, will you be more careful to not treat non-existents as if they have any reality? What change in your speech does this lesson demand?
?
The Mu'tazilites wanted to include everything known as a 'thing', but Taftazani shows this leads to including impossibilities. Are there areas in your own faith where you hold a broad definition that, when pushed, leads to contradictions you deny? How will you now examine your beliefs to ensure they are logically consistent with the teachings of the Salaf?

Extreme Definitions and the Method of Lexical Resolution
Taftazani lists and rejects extreme definitions of 'thing' and prescribes a method for lexical resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various heterodox definitions of 'thing' that Taftazani dismisses as remote and the principle he advocates for settling lexical disputes: reliance on transmitted usage, frequency, and immediate understanding.

Core Idea
Extreme definitions of 'thing' are linguistically untenable; only transmitted usage can decide.

After discussing mainstream views, Taftazani turns to more eccentric definitions that restrict 'thing' to the eternal, the originated, or the body. He dismisses these as extremely remote and unsupported by linguistic evidence. He then explains that since the default meaning is literal, the correct definition must be determined by transmission, frequency of use, or immediate understanding, not by arbitrary stipulation.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic from 'فقد ذكر جار الله' to the end. Notice the list of different opinions and how the author evaluates them.
The author uses the phrase 'فبعيد جداً' to strongly reject the last three opinions.
2
Identify each opinion and its proponent
Find the opinions of Jar Allah, 'some' (unnamed), Abu al-Abbas al-Nashi', the Jahmiyyah, and Hisham ibn al-Hakam. Note what each group claims 'thing' means.
3
Understand the author's critique
The author rejects the last three opinions as extremely remote (بعيد جداً) because linguists would not accept them. He also notes that any usage contrary to the claimed meaning can be explained as metaphorical.
The principle of default literal meaning (الحقيقة) is a key point; the burden of proof is on those who claim a non-literal or restricted meaning.
4
Grasp the method for resolution
The passage concludes that to settle the dispute, one must refer to transmission (نقل), frequency of use (كثرة استعمال), or immediate understanding (مبادرة فهم). This is the proper method in lexical debates.
This methodological rule applies beyond this specific issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا ينفي الاستعمال المجازي بل الحقيقي ومـا ذكــره أبو الحسين (٢) البصري (١) والنصيبي ) (٢) من أنه حقيقة في الوجود مجاز في المعدوم، وهو مذهبنا بعينه، وعند كثير من المعتزلة هو اسم للمعلوم، ويلزمهم أن يكون المستحيل شيئاً وهو لا يقولون به ، اللهم إلا أن يمنع كون المستحيل معلوماً على ما سيأتي أو يمنع عدم قولهم بإطلاق الشيء عليه. فقد ذكر جار الله أنه اسم لما يصح أن يعلم يستوي فيه الموجود والمعدوم، والمحال والمستقيم، والذي لا قائل به هو كونه شيئاً، بمعنى الثبوت في الخارج. وعند بعضهم هو اسم لما ليس بمستحيل موجوداً كان أو معدوماً، وما نقل عن أبي العباس الناشيء (٣) . أنه اسم للقديم، وعن الجهمية (٤) . أنه اسم للحادث، وعن هشام بن الحكم (٥) أنه اسم للجسم، فبعيد جداً من جهة أنه لا يجوز أن يقبله أهل اللغة ولا تقوم عليه شبهة لا من جهة وقوع استعماله في غير ما ذكر كل منهم، فإن له أن يقول هو مجاز. كما نقول نحن في مثل قوله تعالى إنما قولنا الشيء) وكون الأصل في الإطلاق هو الحقيقة مشترك الإلزام، فلا بد من الرجوع إلى أمر آخر من نقل، أو كثرة استعمال أو مبادرة فهم أو نحو ذلك .
It does not negate the metaphorical usage but the literal one. And what Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and al-Nasibi mentioned – that it is literal for existence and metaphorical for non-existence – is precisely our doctrine. According to many Mu'tazilites, it is a name for the known [entity], and they would be required that the impossible be a thing, but they do not hold that, unless it is argued that the impossible is not known as will come, or that they do not deny applying 'thing' to it. Jar Allah stated that it is a name for what can be known, equally applicable to existent and non-existent, to impossible and possible; what no one holds is that it is a thing in the sense of external subsistence. According to some, it is a name for what is not impossible, whether existent or non-existent. What is reported from Abu al-Abbas al-Nashi' that it is a name for the eternal, and from the Jahmiyyah that it is a name for the originated, and from Hisham ibn al-Hakam that it is a name for the body, is extremely remote, because it cannot be accepted by linguists, nor can any evidence support it; and the fact that it is used in other than what each of them mentioned can be argued as metaphorical. As we say in the case of His statement 'Our saying to a thing is but...' and the principle that the literal meaning is the default in usage is a common point – so one must refer to another matter: transmission, frequency of use, immediate understanding, or the like.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the various definitions of the term 'شيء' (thing) in kalam, beginning with the author's own view. The passage opens by clarifying that the issue is not about metaphorical usage but literal meaning. The author states that Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and al-Nasibi held that 'thing' is literal for the existent and metaphorical for the non-existent, which is precisely the author's own doctrine. Then he notes that many Mu'tazilites consider it a name for what is known, which would entail that the impossible (al-mustahil) is a thing, but they do not accept that, unless they deny that the impossible is known or that they would deny the application of 'thing' to it. Jar Allah (al-Zamakhshari) is cited as saying it is a name for what can be known, equally applying to existent and non-existent, to impossible and possible; what no one holds is that it is a thing in the sense of external subsistence. Other views are mentioned: some say it is a name for what is not impossible; Abu al-Abbas al-Nashi' says it is a name for the eternal (al-qadim); the Jahmiyyah say it is a name for the originated (al-hadith); Hisham ibn al-Hakam says it is a name for the body. The author dismisses these as extremely remote, because linguists would not accept them and they have no evidence; moreover, any usage in other than the claimed sense can be explained as metaphorical. The passage concludes that since the default is literal meaning, one must rely on transmitted usage, frequency, immediate understanding, or similar evidence to settle the definition.

Extreme definitions of 'thing' are linguistically untenable; the correct meaning must be derived from transmitted usage, frequency, or immediate understanding.

Definition of al-Ḥāl and the Problem of Infinite Regress
Defines al-ḥāl and refutes infinite regress with two rebuttals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-ḥāl (the state) in kalām and how the infinite regress objection is rebutted by two different scholars.

Core Idea
Al-ḥāl is not an entity but a descriptor that avoids infinite regress through negative or positive nuance.

This passage introduces the concept of al-ḥāl (the state) and presents a classic objection: if states share a common feature, that feature would itself be a state, leading to infinite regress. Two major scholars—the Imam and al-Ḥakīm al-Muḥaqqiq—offer distinct rebuttals. Their arguments reveal deep commitments about the nature of attributes in kalām. By working through this passage, you will see how the concept of al-ḥāl is defended and refined.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation together. Try to grasp the overall flow: a problem is stated, then two responses are given.
The passage is dense; focus on the structure first.
2
Identify the objection
The passage begins with an objection: if states share in the generality of being a state, that shared aspect would require another state, leading to infinite regress. Understand why this is a problem for the theory of states.
This sets up the need for a rebuttal.
3
Analyze the Imam's rebuttal
The Imam argues that 'statehood' is a negative attribute (ṣifa salbiyya), not a positive one. Therefore, sharing in it does not create a new state. Note how he uses the distinction between positive and negative attributes.
This is a defensive move that avoids regress by denying the positive nature of statehood.
4
Analyze al-Ḥakīm al-Muḥaqqiq's rebuttal
Al-Ḥakīm argues that al-ḥāl is not pure negation; it has a positive content but is not a self-subsistent essence (dhāt). He explains why states are not similar or different, and why this prevents regress. Compare this to the Imam's approach.
This rebuttal preserves the positive reality of states while avoiding regress through a different metaphysical framework.
5
Synthesize the main points
Summarize how each rebuttal handles the infinite regress objection. Consider the implications for the theory of states in kalām.
Both rebuttals seek to save the concept of al-ḥāl from logical collapse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخصوصياتها، ومتشاركة في عموم كونها حالاً، وما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة فيلزم أن يكون للحال حال آخر إلى غير النهاية. ودفعه الإمام بأن الحالية ليست صفة ثبوتية، حتى يلزم أن يكون للحال حال آخر، وذلك لأنه لا معنى للحال إلا ما يكون موجوداً، ولا معدوماً وهو صفة سلبية، فلا يكون الاشتراك فيها اشتراكاً في حال ليلزم تسلسل الأحوال. ورده الحكيم المحقق، بأن الحال عندهم ليس سلباً محضاً، بل هو وصف ثابت للموجود ليس بموجود ولا معدوم، ولهذا لم يجعلوا المستحيل حالاً مع أنه ليس بموجود ولا معدوم، فإذن الحال يشتمل عندهم على معنى غير سلب الوجود والعدم يختص بتلك الأمور التي يسمونها حالاً، وتشترك الأحوال فيه وهي لا توصف بالتماثل والاختلاف، لأن المثلين عندهم يفهم منهما معنى واحد والمختلفان ذاتان لا يفهم منهما معنى واحد، والحال ليس بذات لأنها التي تدرك بالانفراد والحال لا تدرك بالانفراد والمشترك ليس بمدرك بالانفراد حتى يحكم بأن المدرك من أحدهما هو المدرك من الآخر أو ليس.
With their particularities, and sharing in the generality of being a state (ḥāl), and that by which they share is other than that by which they are distinguished, so it would necessitate that the state has another state ad infinitum. The Imam refuted this by saying: 'Statehood (al-ḥāliyya) is not a positive attribute (ṣifa thubūtiyya) such that it would necessitate that the state has another state. That is because the meaning of state is nothing but what is existent and not non-existent, and it is a negative attribute (ṣifa salbiyya), so sharing in it is not sharing in a state that would necessitate an infinite regress of states.' The Hakīm al-Muḥaqqiq refuted him by saying: 'The state according to them is not pure negation; rather it is an established description of the existent which is neither existent nor non-existent. For this reason they did not consider the impossible to be a state, even though it is neither existent nor non-existent. Therefore the state according to them includes a meaning other than the negation of existence and non-existence, specific to those things they call states, and the states share in it while they are not described as similar or different. For similars according to them involve a single meaning, and differents are two entia (dhātān) that do not involve a single meaning. The state is not an entity because it is perceived alone, and the state is not perceived alone, and what is shared is not perceived alone such that it could be judged that what is perceived from one is what is perceived from the other or not.'
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage presents a definition and discussion of the concept of al-ḥāl (the state) in kalām. It begins by stating that states share in the general notion of being a state but are distinguished by particularities, leading to an objection: if states share in something and are distinguished by something else, then that shared aspect would itself be a state, leading to an infinite regress. The Imam (al-Ghazālī or al-Rāzī, depending on context) rebuts this by asserting that 'statehood' is not a positive attribute but a negative one—it merely negates existence and non-existence. Thus, sharing in it is not sharing in a positive state that could generate a regress. Then al-Ḥakīm al-Muḥaqqiq (likely Naṣīr al-Dīn al-Ṭūsī or similar) responds that for proponents of states, the state is not pure negation; it is a real description of the existent that is neither existent nor non-existent. They do not apply it to impossible entities, so it has positive content. States share in this meaning but are not classifiable as similar or different because similarity requires a single essence, difference requires distinct essences, and the state is not an essence (dhāt). It is not perceived independently, so one cannot say the perceived from one state is identical to that from another. This preserves the coherence of the theory against the regress objection.

الصفة السلبية
الصفة الثبوتية
التسلسل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-ḥāl is a pure negation, like the impossible.
Correction: The Hakim clarifies that al-ḥāl has a positive meaning beyond mere negation, as it only applies to existent attributes, not impossible ones.

Checkpoint
Why does the Imam argue that the infinite regress objection fails?
A) Because states are not real entities.
B) Because 'statehood' is a negative attribute, so sharing it does not create a new state.
C) Because infinite regress is impossible in any case.
D) Because the hakim already proved states are positive.

Key Takeaway
The objection that states lead to infinite regress is rebutted either by denying the positivity of statehood (Imam) or by affirming a positive but non-essential meaning of the state (Hakim).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes like knowledge or power, do you unconsciously treat them as separate 'things' attached to God? ?
The passage warns against infinite regress in reasoning about God. Are there areas in your own faith life where you keep asking 'why' without ever settling on God as the ultimate answer? How can you apply the lesson's closure (stopping regress) to ground your trust in Allah?
?

Definition of Quiddity and Its Explanation
Taftazani defines quiddity and gives its synonyms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the formal definition of quiddity (al-mahiyyah) as that by which a thing answers the question 'what is it?' and its distinction from the efficient cause, along with its synonyms.

Core Idea
Quiddity is the 'whatness' of a thing—that by which it is itself.

This lesson introduces Taftazani's definition of quiddity, a cornerstone of Islamic metaphysics. The passage lays out what quiddity means and what it does not mean, setting the stage for deeper theological discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the first sentence: 'mahiyyat al-shay' ma bihi yujab 'an al-su'al bima huwa, wa yufassiruhu bima bihi al-shay' huwa huwa.' This is the core definition.
Notice the two complementary descriptions.
2
Understand the rejection of the agent
The passage says: 'wa la yantaqidu bil-fa'il idh bihi wujud al-shay' la huwa.' This clarifies that the efficient cause gives existence, not the essence.
This prevents a common confusion.
3
Learn the synonyms
Quiddity is called dhat and haqiqah with respect to realization, and huwiyyah with respect to individuation.
These terms are used interchangeably in different contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول: ماهية الشيء ما به يجاب عن السؤال بما هو، ويفسره بما به الشيء هو هو، ولا ينتقض بالفاعل إذ به وجود الشيء لا هو. وهي باعتبار التحقق تسمى ذاتا وحقيقة، وباعتبار التشخص هوية. وهي لفظة مشتقة عما هو، ولذا قالوا: ماهية الشيء ما به يجاب عن السؤال بما هو، كما أن الكمية ما به يجاب عن السؤال بكم هو. ولا خفاء في أن المراد بما هو الذي تطلب الحقيقة دون الوصف، أو شرح الاسم، وتركوا اعتماداً على أنه المتعارف، واحترازاً عن ذكر الحقيقة في تفسير الماهية. ومنهم من صرح بالقيد فقال: الذي يطلب به جميع ما به الشيء هو هو. وأنت خبير بأن ذلك بعينه معنى الماهية، وأن هذا التفسير لفظي فلا دور، ويشبه أن يكون هذا تحديداً، إذ لا دور، وقد يفسر بما به الشيء هو هو. لا مفهوم سوى هذا، وزعم بعضهم أنه صادق على العلة الفاعلية، ويتصوّر.
The first investigation: The quiddity of a thing is that by which one answers the question 'what is it?', and it is explained by 'that by which the thing is itself'. And it is not invalidated by the agent, for by the agent is the existence of the thing, not itself. And it, with respect to realization, is called essence (dhat) and reality (haqiqah), and with respect to individuation, identity (huwiyyah). And it is a word derived from 'what is it' (ma huwa). Hence they said: The quiddity of a thing is that by which one answers the question 'what is it?', just as quantity (kammiyyah) is that by which one answers the question 'how much is it?' And it is not hidden that what is meant by 'what is it' is that which seeks the reality, not the description or explanation of the name, and they omitted [the condition] relying on it being customary, and to avoid mentioning 'reality' in the interpretation of quiddity. And among them is one who explicitly stated the condition and said: That by which all of what makes the thing itself is sought. And you are an expert that this is exactly the meaning of quiddity, and that this interpretation is verbal, so there is no circularity, and it resembles that this is a definition, since there is no circularity. And it is sometimes interpreted by 'that by which the thing is itself'. There is no concept other than this. And some claimed that it applies to the efficient cause, and it is conceivable.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage defines the philosophical term 'quiddity' (al-mahiyyah). It begins by giving two complementary definitions: quiddity is that by which one answers the question 'what is it?' (ma huwa), and it is explained as 'that by which the thing is itself' (ma bihi al-shay' huwa huwa). The author immediately addresses a potential objection: the efficient cause (al-fa'il) might be thought to fit this definition because a thing exists through its cause. He clarifies that the agent gives existence, not the 'selfhood' of the thing. He then provides the synonyms of quiddity: when considered in terms of realization (tahaqquq), it is called essence (dhat) or reality (haqiqah); when considered in terms of individuation (tashakhkhus), it is called identity (huwiyyah). Next, he explains the etymology: 'mahiyyah' is derived from 'ma huwa' (what is it?), analogous to 'kammiyyah' (quantity) from 'kam huwa' (how much is it?). The author then clarifies a subtle point: the question 'what is it?' here seeks the true nature (haqiqah), not a mere description or explanation of the name. The omission of this condition in the definition is said to be conventional and to avoid redundancy. Some scholars explicitly add the condition that it is 'that by which all of what makes the thing itself is sought'. The author asserts that this is exactly the meaning of quiddity, that the interpretation is merely verbal (lafzi) and does not involve circularity (dawr). In fact, it resembles a true definition (tahdid). He concludes that quiddity has no other concept, rejecting the claim that it might apply to the efficient cause, though he allows that such an application is conceivable but not what is intended.

Key Terms
الماهية
الذات
الحقيقة
الهوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quiddity is what makes a thing exist, like an efficient cause.
Correction: Quiddity is about essence, not existence; the efficient cause gives existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of quiddity (al-mahiyyah)?
A) That by which a thing exists
B) That by which one answers the question 'what is it?' and that by which the thing is itself
C) That which answers the question 'how much is it?'
D) The efficient cause

Key Takeaway
Quiddity is the essence of a thing, defining its 'whatness' and distinguished from existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If quiddity is what makes a thing itself, then what makes you yourself? Is it your body, your soul, your actions, or something else? How does this lesson challenge how you define your own identity?
?
The passage distinguishes quiddity from the agent that brings it into existence. In your life, do you confuse your own worth (your quiddity) with external factors (agents) that give you existence or success? How can this distinction help you see yourself more clearly?
?
Consider the synonyms: essence, reality, identity. In your relationship with Allah, are you more focused on your existence (that you are alive) or on your essence (what you truly are)? What does this lesson tell you about what is truly important?

Etymology and Verbal Interpretation of Quiddity
Taftazani explains the etymology and clarifies the verbal interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the etymology of 'mahiyyah' from 'ma huwa', the analogy with 'kammiyyah', and why the interpretation is verbal without circularity.

Core Idea
'Mahiyyah' is derived from 'ma huwa' just as 'kammiyyah' is from 'kam huwa'.

This lesson delves into the linguistic origins of the term 'quiddity' and clarifies that the definition given is merely verbal, not circular. Taftazani explains why the condition 'seeking the reality' is omitted and addresses the claim that quiddity could apply to the efficient cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the etymology
Read: 'wa hiya lafzat mushtaqqa 'amma huwa...' to see how mahiyyah is derived from the question 'ma huwa'.
Compare with kammiyyah from kam huwa.
2
Understand the condition
The passage notes that 'ma huwa' here seeks the reality (haqiqah), not a description or explanation of the name. Some scholars explicitly state this condition.
The condition is omitted to avoid redundancy.
3
Grasp the verbal interpretation
Taftazani says this interpretation is verbal (lafzi) and not circular (la dawr). It is similar to a definition (tahdid).
This resolves worries about circular definitions.
4
Note the rejection of alternative
Some claimed quiddity applies to the efficient cause, but Taftazani says there is no concept other than the one given.
This reinforces the earlier distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول: ماهية الشيء ما به يجاب عن السؤال بما هو، ويفسره بما به الشيء هو هو، ولا ينتقض بالفاعل إذ به وجود الشيء لا هو. وهي باعتبار التحقق تسمى ذاتا وحقيقة، وباعتبار التشخص هوية. وهي لفظة مشتقة عما هو، ولذا قالوا: ماهية الشيء ما به يجاب عن السؤال بما هو، كما أن الكمية ما به يجاب عن السؤال بكم هو. ولا خفاء في أن المراد بما هو الذي تطلب الحقيقة دون الوصف، أو شرح الاسم، وتركوا اعتماداً على أنه المتعارف، واحترازاً عن ذكر الحقيقة في تفسير الماهية. ومنهم من صرح بالقيد فقال: الذي يطلب به جميع ما به الشيء هو هو. وأنت خبير بأن ذلك بعينه معنى الماهية، وأن هذا التفسير لفظي فلا دور، ويشبه أن يكون هذا تحديداً، إذ لا دور، وقد يفسر بما به الشيء هو هو. لا مفهوم سوى هذا، وزعم بعضهم أنه صادق على العلة الفاعلية، ويتصوّر.
The first investigation: The quiddity of a thing is that by which one answers the question 'what is it?', and it is explained by 'that by which the thing is itself'. And it is not invalidated by the agent, for by the agent is the existence of the thing, not itself. And it, with respect to realization, is called essence (dhat) and reality (haqiqah), and with respect to individuation, identity (huwiyyah). And it is a word derived from 'what is it' (ma huwa). Hence they said: The quiddity of a thing is that by which one answers the question 'what is it?', just as quantity (kammiyyah) is that by which one answers the question 'how much is it?' And it is not hidden that what is meant by 'what is it' is that which seeks the reality, not the description or explanation of the name, and they omitted [the condition] relying on it being customary, and to avoid mentioning 'reality' in the interpretation of quiddity. And among them is one who explicitly stated the condition and said: That by which all of what makes the thing itself is sought. And you are an expert that this is exactly the meaning of quiddity, and that this interpretation is verbal, so there is no circularity, and it resembles that this is a definition, since there is no circularity. And it is sometimes interpreted by 'that by which the thing is itself'. There is no concept other than this. And some claimed that it applies to the efficient cause, and it is conceivable.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage defines the philosophical term 'quiddity' (al-mahiyyah). It begins by giving two complementary definitions: quiddity is that by which one answers the question 'what is it?' (ma huwa), and it is explained as 'that by which the thing is itself' (ma bihi al-shay' huwa huwa). The author immediately addresses a potential objection: the efficient cause (al-fa'il) might be thought to fit this definition because a thing exists through its cause. He clarifies that the agent gives existence, not the 'selfhood' of the thing. He then provides the synonyms of quiddity: when considered in terms of realization (tahaqquq), it is called essence (dhat) or reality (haqiqah); when considered in terms of individuation (tashakhkhus), it is called identity (huwiyyah). Next, he explains the etymology: 'mahiyyah' is derived from 'ma huwa' (what is it?), analogous to 'kammiyyah' (quantity) from 'kam huwa' (how much is it?). The author then clarifies a subtle point: the question 'what is it?' here seeks the true nature (haqiqah), not a mere description or explanation of the name. The omission of this condition in the definition is said to be conventional and to avoid redundancy. Some scholars explicitly add the condition that it is 'that by which all of what makes the thing itself is sought'. The author asserts that this is exactly the meaning of quiddity, that the interpretation is merely verbal (lafzi) and does not involve circularity (dawr). In fact, it resembles a true definition (tahdid). He concludes that quiddity has no other concept, rejecting the claim that it might apply to the efficient cause, though he allows that such an application is conceivable but not what is intended.

Key Terms
الماهية
الكمية
التفسير اللفظي
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of quiddity is circular because it uses 'what is it?' to define itself.
Correction: The interpretation is verbal and not circular; it simply explains the meaning of the word.

Checkpoint
Why is the interpretation of quiddity considered 'verbal' (lafzi)?
A) Because it uses the same words
B) Because it explains the meaning of the word without involving circularity
C) Because it is derived from the Arabic language
D) Because it is a literal translation

Key Takeaway
Mahiyyah is derived from 'ma huwa' and its definition is a verbal explanation, not circular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define yourself or your purpose, do you give a true answer to 'what is it?' seeking the reality, or do you settle for a mere description? What would it take to move from describing to defining?
?
In what areas of your life have you become comfortable with 'verbal interpretations'—explanations that sound right but lack depth? How can you push yourself toward a more direct encounter with reality, even if it feels uncomfortable?

The Threefold Division of Quiddity: Mixed, Abstract, and Absolute
The author defines mixed and abstract quiddity and footnotes the threefold division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ways quiddity is considered in relation to accidents: mixed (with accidents), abstract (without any accidents), and absolute (unrestricted).

Core Idea
Quiddity can be viewed as mixed, abstract, or absolute, each distinct in existence and conception.

This lesson examines how scholars of kalam categorize quiddity (essence) in relation to accidents. The author distinguishes between quiddity taken with a condition (mixed) and quiddity taken with the condition of no accidents (abstract). A footnote clarifies the third category: absolute quiddity. Understanding these divisions is foundational for later discussions on universals and particulars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the footnotes, especially footnote (1) which adds the third division.
2
Identify the three divisions
The passage presents three divisions of quiddity. The main text discusses mixed and abstract, while footnote (1) explicitly lists all three. Find each one and note their defining conditions.
Mixed: بشرط شيء; Abstract: بشرط لا; Absolute: لا بشرط.
3
Understand the existence status of each
The author explains that mixed quiddity exists clearly (e.g., Zayd and Amr). Abstract quiddity does not exist even in the mind because mental existence is itself an accident. Absolute quiddity is unrestricted and more general.
Consider why abstract quiddity cannot exist—because being in the mind is an accident.
4
Reflect on why this matters for theology
This classification helps in discussing divine attributes and whether they are identical to or distinct from the essence. It clarifies how we conceive of quiddity in relation to accidents.
This connects to broader kalām debates on the nature of God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
تقسيم الماهية باعتبار ثبوت العوارض أو نفيها (١)
(قال : المبحث الثاني : الماهية قد تؤخذ(٢) بشرط شيء وتسمى المخلوطة ولا خفاء في وجودها وقد تؤخذ بشرط لا شيء وتسمى المجردة، ولا توجد في الأذهان فضلاً عن الأعيان وإن قيدت اللواحق بالخارجية، لأن الكون في الذهن مما يلحقها في نفسها، وإن لم يتصوره العقل، ولم يجعله وصفاً لها، وما يقال من أن للعقل أن يلاحظها وحدها، أو يعتبرها مجردة عن ما عداها حتى عن الكون في الذهن لا يقتضي تجردها، وإن اكتفى بمجرد اعتبار العقل جاز وجودها في الخارج (٣) أيضاً بأن يعتبر المقرونة بالمشخصات كذلك .
جميع
مقارنة العوارض وتسمى المخلوطة (٤) ، والماهية بشرط شيء، ولا خفاء في وجودها كزيد وعمرو من أفراد ماهية الإنسان، وقد تؤخذ بشرط ألا يقارنها
(١) الماهية : تارة تؤخذ مقيدة بالعوارض وتسمى مخلوطة، وتارة تؤخذ مقيدة بنفي جميع العوارض وتسمى مجردة وتارة تؤخذ غير مقيدة بثبوتها ولا نفيها وتسمى مطلقة، فالقسمان الأولان كل منهما أخص من الثالث لاختصاصه بالقيد والقسم الثالث : أعم من كل منهما لإطلاقه من التقييد وكل من المقيدين مباين لصاحبه فهي أقسام ثلاثة . (٢) في (ب) توجد بدلاً من (تؤخذ) .
(٣) أي لزم صحة وجودها خارجاً بدلك التقدير.
.
(٤) مخلوطة : لأنها أخذت خلط شيء زائد عليها مثالها : ماهية الإنسان تؤخذ مخلوطة مع بالمشخصات في زيد وعمرو، والمراد من أخذها مع المشخصات اعتباراتها كذلك في نفس الأمر
لا تقدير الخلط ولو لم يحصل فإنه يصح في المعدومة .
The second investigation: Division of quiddity by considering the establishment of accidents or their negation. (He said: The second investigation: Quiddity is sometimes taken with a condition of something and is called mixed, and there is no obscurity in its existence; and it is sometimes taken with the condition of nothing and is called abstract, and it does not exist in minds, let alone in external entities, even if the accidents are restricted to external ones, because being in the mind is among the things that attach to it in itself, even if the mind does not conceive it and does not make it a description of it. And what is said that the mind can observe it alone, or consider it abstracted from everything else even from being in the mind, does not necessitate its abstraction, and if one is content with mere consideration of the mind, its existence in external would also be permissible, by considering what is conjoined with individuators likewise. All of the accompaniment of accidents is called mixed, and quiddity with a condition of something, and there is no obscurity in its existence, like Zayd and Amr from the individuals of the quiddity of man. And it is sometimes taken with the condition that nothing accompanies it. (Footnote 1: Quiddity: sometimes taken as restricted by accidents and called mixed, sometimes taken as restricted by the negation of all accidents and called abstract, and sometimes taken as unrestricted by their affirmation or negation and called absolute. So the first two divisions are each more specific than the third because of their restriction, and the third is more general than each because of its lack of restriction, and each of the restricted ones is mutually exclusive with the other; so they are three divisions.) (Footnote 2: In copy (b) 'takes' instead of 'is taken'.) (Footnote 3: That is, the validity of its existence externally is necessitated by that estimation.) (Footnote 4: Mixed: because it is taken with the admixture of something additional to it. Its example: the quiddity of man is taken mixed with individuators in Zayd and Amr. And the meaning of taking it with individuators is its considerations as such in actual fact, not the estimation of admixture even if it does not occur, for it is valid in the non-existent.)
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage defines the threefold division of quiddity (الماهية) based on how accidents (العوارض) are considered. The author first states that quiddity can be taken 'with the condition of something' (بشرط شيء), meaning it is considered together with an accident, and this is called mixed (المخلوطة). Mixed quiddity exists clearly, such as the individuals Zayd and Amr. Second, quiddity can be taken 'with the condition of nothing' (بشرط لا شيء), called abstract (المجردة). The abstract quiddity does not exist even in the mind, let alone externally, because being in the mind is itself an accident that attaches to it. Even if the mind tries to consider it alone, it is not actually abstract. The footnote (1) clarifies that there is also a third division: absolute (المطلقة), which is quiddity taken without any condition of affirmation or negation of accidents. The absolute is more general than the mixed and abstract, while the mixed and abstract are mutually exclusive. The other footnotes provide textual variants and explanations of terms.

العوارض
المخلوطة
المجردة
المطلقة
بشرط شيء
بشرط لا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think that abstract quiddity can exist in the mind simply by mental abstraction.
Correction: The author clarifies that being in the mind itself is an accident, so true abstraction from all accidents is impossible even in the mind.

Checkpoint
What are the three divisions of quiddity according to footnote (1)?
A) Mixed, abstract, and absolute
B) Mixed, abstract, and conditioned
C) Conditioned, unconditioned, and absolute
D) External, mental, and absolute

Key Takeaway
Quiddity is divided into three: mixed (with accidents, exists externally), abstract (without accidents, does not exist even mentally), and absolute (unrestricted, more general).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own identity, do you see yourself as a 'mixed quiddity'—defined by your accidents (like your job, family, appearance)—or as the pure humanity that connects you to every other person? Which view draws you closer to recognizing your dependence on Allah?
?
The passage argues that abstract quiddity cannot exist even in the mind. If your understanding of Allah is necessarily filtered through mental concepts, how does this limitation humble your claim to know Him as He is?
?
Absolute quiddity is unrestricted and more general than both mixed and abstract. Can you identify any belief or habit you hold that is 'mixed'—too tied to your own circumstances—that prevents you from seeing the absolute truth of tawheed? What would it take to strip away that accident?

The Abstracted Essence: Definition and Refutation
Definition and refutation of two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-mujarrada (the abstracted essence) and why its existence in external entities and in the mind is impossible, along with the refutation of two arguments that claim otherwise.

Core Idea
The abstracted essence cannot exist anywhere—not externally nor even in the mind.

This passage from the matn introduces the concept of al-mujarrada, the essence stripped of all accidents. The author first defines it and then demonstrates its impossibility in both external existence and mental existence. He then addresses and refutes two arguments that attempt to argue for its possible existence, showing the limits of conceptual abstraction.

Guided Path
1
Define al-mujarrada
Read the opening line identifying it as the essence with the condition of nothing else (bi-sharṭ lā).
2
Understand impossibility in the external world
Note that existence and individuation are accidents, so the mujarrada cannot be externally realized.
3
Understand impossibility in the mind
Recognize that being in the mind is itself an accident, so even mental existence is impossible.
4
Refute the restriction claim
Consider the argument that if external accidents are restricted, the mujarrada could exist in the mind, and see why the author finds it problematic.
5
Refute the two direct arguments
Examine the two arguments that claim the intellect can conceive the mujarrada, and see why each fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شيء من العوارض وتسمى المجردة والماهية بشرط لا ولا خفاء في امتناع وجودها في الأعيان، لأن الوجود من العوارض وكذا التشخص، وفي الأذهان أيضاً سواء أطلقت العوارض، أو قيدت بالخارجية، لأن الكون في الذهن أيضاً من العوارض التي لحقت الصورة الذهنية، بحسب الخارج لا بمجرد اعتبار العقل، وجعله إياه وصفاً لها، وقيداً فيها. وزعم بعضهم أنه يجوز وجودها في الذهن، إذا قيدت العوارض الخارجية زعماً منه أن الكون في الذهن من العوارض الذهنية وكأنه أراد بالعوارض الخارجية ما يلحق الأمور الحاصلة في الأعيان، وبالذهنية ما يلحق الأمور القائمة بالأذهان، وعلى هذا فكون الوجود في الخارج من العوارض الخارجية، محل نظر على سبقها في بحث الوجود، فلا يتحقق امتناع وجود المجردة في الخارج أيضاً، وذكر بعضهم أنها موجودة من غير تقييد العوارض بالخارج وبينوه بوجهين : أحدهما، أن للعقل أن يلاحظ الماهية وحدها من غير ملاحظة شيء معها. ورد بأن مثل هذا لا يكون مأخوذاً (بشرط لا). وهو ظاهر، وثانيهما : أن للعقل أن يعتبر عدم كل شيء حتى عدم نفسه فجاز أن يعتبر الماهية مجردة عن جميع العوارض حتى عن الكون في الذهن، وإن كانت هي في نفسها مقرونة بها. ورد بأن هذا لا يقتضي كونها مجردة بل غاية الأمر أن العقل قد تصورها كذلك تصوراً غير مطابق.
It is something of the accidents, and it is called the abstracted (al-mujarrada) and the essence (al-māhiyya) with the condition of nothing [else] (bi-sharṭ lā). There is no concealment of the impossibility of its existence in external entities, because existence is among the accidents, and likewise individuation (al-tashakhkhuṣ). And [it is impossible] in minds also, whether the accidents are left unrestricted or restricted to external [accidents], because being in the mind is also among the accidents that attach to the mental form, according to the external [world], not by mere consideration of the intellect and its making it a description of it and a restrictor in it. And some have claimed that it is permissible for it to exist in the mind if the external accidents are restricted, supposing that being in the mind is among the mental accidents. It is as if he meant by external accidents what attaches to things occurring in external entities, and by mental [accidents] what attaches to things subsisting in minds. On this basis, existence in the external [world] being among external accidents is open to scrutiny based on its precedence in the discussion of existence, so the impossibility of the existence of the abstracted in the external [world] is not realized. And some have mentioned that it exists without restricting the accidents to the external [world], and they explained it in two ways: First, that the intellect can observe the essence alone without observing anything with it. It was refuted by [saying] that such a thing is not taken with the condition of nothing (bi-sharṭ lā), and that is apparent. Second, that the intellect can consider the negation of everything, even the negation of itself, so it is permissible for it to consider the essence abstracted from all accidents, even from being in the mind, even if it is in itself conjoined with them. It was refuted by [saying] that this does not entail that it is abstracted; rather the utmost is that the intellect has conceptualized it in such a way, a non-corresponding conceptualization.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage defines al-mujarrada (the abstracted essence) and then refutes two arguments that claim it can exist. The author begins by stating that the essence taken with the condition of nothing (bi-sharṭ lā) is called al-mujarrada. Its existence is impossible in external entities because existence itself is an accident, and likewise individuation. It is also impossible in the mind because 'being in the mind' is also an accident that attaches to the mental form according to the external world, not by mere intellectual consideration. The author then addresses a claim that if external accidents are restricted, the mujarrada could exist in the mind. This supposition is based on a distinction between external and mental accidents. The author notes that this classification is questionable given earlier discussions on existence. Next, two arguments for the existence of the mujarrada are presented and refuted. The first argument: the intellect can observe the essence alone. Refutation: such observation is not 'taken with the condition of nothing' (bi-sharṭ lā) because the intellect still considers it as existing in the mind. The second argument: the intellect can consider the negation of everything, even itself, so it can consider the essence abstracted from all accidents. Refutation: this yields a conceptualization that does not correspond to reality; it does not prove that the essence is actually abstracted.

Key Terms
المجردة (al-mujarrada)
الماهية بشرط لا (al-māhiyya bi-sharṭ lā)
العوارض (al-aʿrāḍ)
التشخص (al-tashakhkhuṣ)
الصورة الذهنية (al-ṣūra al-dhihniyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mujarrada can exist in the mind because the intellect can conceive an essence without accidents.
Correction: The passage shows that such conception is non-corresponding; being in the mind is itself an accident, so the mujarrada cannot even be mentally realized.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the mujarrada cannot exist even in the mind?
A) Because the mind cannot conceive of an essence without accidents.
B) Because 'being in the mind' is itself an accident that attaches to the mental form.
C) Because the mujarrada is defined only as existing in external entities.
D) Because the intellect's consideration is always corresponding to reality.

Key Takeaway
The abstracted essence (mujarrada) is impossible both externally and mentally; conceptual abstraction does not correspond to reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that any time you conceive something, you necessarily attach accidents to it—even if you try to strip them away. Where in your life have you mistaken your simplified mental image of a person, a situation, or even yourself for the full, complex reality? Will you commit to re-examining one such image today, allowing it to be messier than you've assumed?
?
The passage warns that the mind can negate everything, even itself, but that does not make the negation real. When have you convinced yourself of a falsehood simply because you could imagine it clearly? What are you currently believing about yourself or your faith that you have never tested against reality, and how can you test it this week?
?
If al-mujarrada is impossible, then no part of creation—including your own soul—can be known without its inseparable attributes. Are there any attributes of Allah that you have tried to strip away or ignore because they made you uncomfortable? How does this lesson call you to accept that His attributes are real and inseparable from His essence, even when they challenge your comfort?
